Epigraph and Dedication, Main Street Millionaire
		
		
		

	
	
		
			
			
				

You aren’t truly in the game unless you find yourself in the dead of the night, head in hands, sitting in the dark, with no idea what to do next.
				

—My father
			
			

I dedicate this book to those who chose to get into the game of building. Entrepreneurs created this world we live in, and yet you are the few. Thank you for doing what most will not; without you this book would be nothing more than noise. A special thank-you to the best entrepreneur I know, Stan Sanchez. I promise we will continue your legacy.
			

One more thing: Behind every successful entrepreneur is a bad former boss. So, I also dedicate this to that boss you hated—you know which one. Thanks, bud—your doubt was our fuel. Wink.

---

Start Here, Main Street Millionaire
		
		
		

	
	
		
			

## Start Here

			

Wayne Huizenga was a billionaire many times over. He was the owner of garbage behemoth Waste Management, AutoNation, and Blockbuster Video (he later sold Blockbuster for $8.4 billion). He also owned three major league sports teams: the Miami Dolphins (NFL), the Florida Marlins (MLB), and the Florida Panthers (NHL). If you Google Wayne’s name, you’ll see the words “founder” and “empire builder” again and again.
			

What’s much less known about Wayne is that before he made billions, he was a poor failure who had dropped out of college. Wayne was the son of an immigrant father who beat him and his mother. His childhood was violent, chaotic, and shrouded in secrecy.
			

After his parents got divorced, Wayne watched his friends go off to college and take white-collar jobs while he drifted from one low-wage job to another. In fact, after a six-month stint in the army, the newly married Wayne had to become a garbage man. One of his old friends recalled seeing Wayne on the job and thinking, “I better hit the books. It must be rough out there if Wayne’s already driving a garbage truck.”
			

So how did this floundering young man with no connections, no family, and no wealth become a billionaire? Did he grab himself by the bootstraps, camp out in a garage, and dream up a new company to rule them all? No, he took a job selling trash-hauling services door-to-door. He drove, he sold, and he studied his boss, Wilbur Porter, who ran Porter’s Rubbish Service, one of Broward County’s first major garbage firms. He figured if he studied a great founder, someday he could become one.
			

Then one day, something clicked. Why start a company when he could buy one that already works? For months, he pressed Porter to let him purchase a share of the business in an asset sale. Porter relented in 1962. He sold Wayne a snub-nosed truck and $500 worth of customers. By 1969, Wayne’s single truck had become a fleet that dwarfed Porter’s. He brought on partners and bought routes and companies from Key West to Tampa. Wayne’s climb continued from there. In 1972, he drove across the United States and bought out ninety haulers in nine months.
			

Those early years taught him three things. First, you don’t need a college degree to make more money than you can imagine. Second, there is incredible profit to be made by buying a small, boring business.
			

And third? The power of creative financing. Wayne Huizenga, the kid who originally didn’t have two pennies to rub together, who couldn’t get a loan even if he begged for it, became a billionaire because of this one essential ingredient. It allowed him to own Waste Management, even though he wasn’t the founder. To the victors go the history books.
			

Why don’t we hear more incredible stories like Wayne Huizenga’s? Because we’ve been lied to about who gets rich and how for decades. Today, we take off the blindfold.
			

This is a book about seeing opportunities for financial freedom all around you, in the overlooked and unassuming businesses that we all take for granted. As someone who specializes in making good, profitable deals, I can promise you that success doesn’t require flashy start-ups or cutting-edge new products. In fact, that’s where you lose money. Instead, you’ll see why finding a “Main Street business” with steady cash flow, acquiring it, and using the profits to live life on your terms is the smart, extremely underrated way to build extraordinary wealth.
			

I will take your hand and show you how. But first, let’s define the lie before we can reveal the truth.
			

## The 9-to-5 Trap: Escaping the System That Keeps You Poor

			

Have you ever felt like you’ll never make enough money? Like you’ve been dealt a lifelong sentence of financial stress, worry, and anxiety? That no matter how hard you work, you’ll never be able to relax and feel financially free?
			

If so, you’re not alone. Billions of workers are hustling all over the world. Trying to keep up with inflation, barely scraping by, and losing hope in the promise of retirement. But here’s the thing: it’s not their fault. All of us were programmed to be poor.
			

Here’s how we’re taught to make money in America. First, sit still and listen to authority figures for eight hours a day until we’re eighteen years old. Then, go to college and take on $30,000-plus in unforgivable debt, in exchange for a degree. Without that piece of paper, we’re told, we will never succeed. We’re not allowed to gain real-world skills through unpaid internships (the ones where you actually do useful work instead of just theorizing) unless they’re officially tied to an educational program, because otherwise they’re considered illegal.
			

Then it’s graduation day. We’re finally allowed to join the working world. Soon after, we discover that most jobs do not, in fact, require a college degree. Nevertheless, we all compete for jobs that barely cover our bills, much less our debts. Our top performers pursue the two highest-paying yet least creative jobs: consulting and Wall Street. Or, they play the lottery by joining a start-up. One that requires sleeping on floors and racking up debt from venture capitalists. The concept of “golden handcuffs” becomes very real.
			

Most of us suspect this game is rigged. We can’t afford houses, we hate our jobs, and our loans keep stacking up. Yet we can’t stop playing. We have bills to pay, with very real consequences if we don’t. So, we stick to what we know. Keep my job, ask for a raise once a year, and look for another job that pays a bit more. When we stop wanting to play, the old guard gets mad at us for not working “hard enough” and “quietly quitting.” No wonder so many do not trust the system.
			

As the proverb goes: Play stupid games, win stupid prizes.[*1] Fortunately, people are starting to wake up. They’ve decided to play a better game, one where they actually have a shot at freedom. Millions are leaving their jobs (risky!) and becoming freelancers (literally half of all workers in Gen Z). Why? Because we want autonomy, upside, and work that aligns with our values. There’s just one problem: freelancing is just like being an employee, in that it’s nearly impossible to build real wealth. A mistake smart people make is trying to get rich as a freelancer. I have bad news: this probably won’t work.
			

Why is this? Because of a simple rule that wealthy people follow. One that separates them from everyone else who struggles with money. I had to work for more than a decade in big finance to learn it. You will learn it in a few hours with this book. It will turn on what is called your reticular activating system, a bundle of nerves at our brain stem that filters out unnecessary information so the important stuff gets through. Once you see the truth of ownership, you cannot unsee it. Here’s your first and most important lesson:
			

Your salary will never set you free. Your financial freedom can only come through ownership. More specifically, through equity done the right way.
			

This is not what most of us were taught. Every day in school all the way up to your first job, you’ve been trained like Pavlov’s dog to follow the path of non-ownership. You’ve been zapped and given treats to believe that a good job is the real key to financial stability. Lies. No matter how hard you work, you will forever be a cocker spaniel begging for biscuits. That’s because you are trading your time for money, which means it’s impossible for you to break free. Warren Buffett said it best: “If you don’t find a way to make money while you sleep, you will work until you die.” It’s time to shed those lies and run toward the truth.
			

The good news is there’s a way out. And no, you don’t need to come up with a genius invention, buy crypto, or own apartment complexes in Miami. Instead, just like Wayne Huizenga, you’ll make a few strategic moves that will allow you to efficiently become an owner. And once you experience the freedom of ownership, you’ll never go back.
			

Here’s the best part: it might not even cost you a dime to start.
			
			• • •
			
			

The central argument of this book is that buying profitable, established, cash-flowing businesses is the most underrated path to wealth. One that, if you do it right and are willing to put in the work, not only pays you enough money to quit your job but also allows you to pay someone else to run it with you. That’s right: you own the business, you make a full-time salary, but you’re potentially not stuck with another full-time job forever.
			

And unlike buying a house, you won’t always need a big down payment to own this business. The previous owner may even hand you the keys to their legacy for less than you can imagine. But you have to understand the game. They’ll want these three things in return:
			
				
- 
					

You’ll give them the ability to exit their business and retire.
				
				
- 
					

You’ll keep their business and legacy alive.
				
				
- 
					

You’ll give them a cut of future profits.
				
			
			

Sound too good to be true? It’s not. This is a proven path that allows normal people to become wealthy. Everyone? No. Only those willing to do the work and dedicate themselves to the rarest of things: being a builder in a world of consumers. It’s what I’ve done, more than a hundred times. Which is how a small corner of the internet came to know me as “the chick who buys laundromats”—not my favorite title, but hey, I’ve been called worse.
			

I’ve also helped thousands of people just like you do it too.
			

Like Renan, a medical device salesperson turned millionaireowner of a service business he bought entirely with creative financing…
			

Or Katie, an employee turned owner of a multi-six-figure dog-grooming business, soon to acquire her second…
			

Or Chris, the new owner of a plumbing business, a locksmith business, and now building out an eight-figure holding company…
			

All these people chose the path of the Main Street Millionaire.[*2] Their companies aren’t fly-by-night, meaningless commodities. They are essential businesses that have contributed real value to their local communities for decades. They have hundreds of paying customers and a profit margin that earns their owners a very healthy income and a newfound ability to live life on their terms.
			

It is scary, but maybe that’s how you know it’s worth it. I’m here to find you, the aspiring owner in a world of owned. Are you one of us? We are a group that rejects the status quo and all the broken systems attached to it. Together, we are taking back Main Street.
			
			• • •
			
				

While the rest of the world laughs at “dry cleaning,” we make millions.
				

—Jim Bowen, CEO of First Trust
			
			

You might be wondering: What is a Main Street business?
			

It’s a small, local business—typically run by mom and pop—that involves minimal intellectual property and provides a needed product or service. I’m talking about car washes, laundromats, vending machines, storage centers, repair shops, pack-and-ship centers, porta-potty rentals, landscaping, electrical services, bookkeeping, carpet cleaning, tiling and grouting, plumbing, pet grooming, and HVAC installation. These are the modest yet profitable businesses that most people overlook.
			

I call them “Main Street” or “boring” businesses because they lack the societal sexiness of Wall Street, tech start-ups, and social media side hustles. Their owners are rarely on the covers of magazines or interviewed on Good Morning America. Instead, they get rich quietly. They build “stealth wealth” by owning the types of businesses that make up the backbone of America. These businesses are dependable, meaningful, successful, and cash flowing. And yet they are rarely considered a go-to option for entrepreneurs. (I aim to change that with this book.) Don’t be fooled; boring businesses do not equal boring lives. A business that has steady cash flow, lets you be with your family, is simple, has great customers, gives you a happy life…that is the REAL unicorn.
			

The hundred-plus businesses I’ve owned and invested in are mostly run-of-the-mill operations that have been around for a long time, surviving and thriving through economic downturns, bringing in profits year after year. Do they have down periods? Yes. But they are not rocket ships that take off or explode spectacularly.
			

Why? There’s an idea called the Lindy effect. It states that if something has been successful for a long time, its success will likely continue for just as long. Nassim Taleb said it best in his book Antifragile:
			
				

If a book has been in print for forty years, I can expect it to be in print for another forty years. But…if it survives another decade, then it will be expected to be in print for another fifty years.
				

This, simply, as a rule, tells you why things that have been around for a long time are not “aging” like persons, but “aging” in reverse. Every year that passes without extinction doubles the additional life expectancy.
			
			

I use that theory to ensure that I don’t fail in business, and it’s worked like a charm. And yet, in the fast-paced world of entrepreneurship, where the siren call of starting a brand-new company drowns out everything else, the Lindy effect continues to be ignored. Business failure abounds, with founders wearing their Fs like badges of honor.
			
				
					

The Lindy Effect
				
				
					
				
			
			

Guess what: 35 percent of boomer-owned businesses have been in operation for more than a decade. Nearly 80 percent are profitable, with steady, loyal customers. Want to guess how much longer their success will continue? Just ask Lindy.
			

This is the secret that Main Street Millionaires know and that most modern entrepreneurs completely ignore: Steady cash flow is NOT found in flashy start-ups or trendy new products. It’s in those established small businesses in your neighborhood, the ones you drive by without a second glance. This is where millions are being made, right here on Main Street.
			

## What You’ll Get from This Book

			

Here’s a big, hefty question for you: Instead of working for decades to make a million dollars, how can you acquire a cash-flowing business that’s making a million dollars right now?
			

At the heart of Main Street Millionaire lies the answer to that question—a simple yet profoundly effective formula: R.I.C.H. This acronym encapsulates the step-by-step process you’ll use to buy your way into becoming a Main Street business owner:
			
				

R for Research ensures that you pursue the business opportunities that are best aligned with you. You’ll clarify your unique skills, the type of owner experience you want, your risk appetite, and how much you need to get paid. You’ll also learn how to find motivated sellers, the right way to talk with them about the prospect of buying their business, and how to evaluate whether it’s a good acquisition or not.
				

I for Invest opens up a whole new world of possibilities, with multiple strategies to buy cash-flowing businesses for little money down or using creative financing. You’ll learn how to informally propose a deal to the owner, work with professionals on due diligence, get the right financing, draft the papers, and seal the deal. You’ll also get my battle-tested negotiation tips, which have saved me millions of dollars over the last decade.
				

C for Command shows you how to avoid the “whoops, I bought myself a job” trap, or worse yet, “whoops, I bought myself a nightmare.” You’ll learn how to find, interview, and hire an operator—and eventually a team—who can help you run your business. You’ll learn the most important things to do (and avoid) in your first ninety days. You’ll establish a culture of continual improvement, track your money, and implement simple marketing systems that efficiently double your profits.
				

H for Harness is where you’ll learn how to manage multiple businesses on autopilot, without losing your sanity. You’ll get a simple scorecard that will keep your businesses on track, add more revenue streams, and prepare for your next acquisition (or an exit).
			
			

R.I.C.H. is the most efficient path I’ve found to financial freedom. Why? Because it doesn’t hinge on having extensive capital or brilliant ideas to start. You can acquire a cash-flowing business that not only replaces your salary but also sets you financially free. I’ve done it, and so have countless business owners. You can do it too.
			
				
				
			
			

## Disclaimer: The Realities of Becoming a Main Street Millionaire

			

A lot of business books set the wrong expectations. My dad always said, “Just tell the truth, it’s easier to remember.” So, here’s the truth: you’ll need to buckle up for the ride ahead. I won’t promise you this journey will be rainbows and kittens (I’m allergic, probably to both). The path I teach is hard. Becoming an owner is 10 percent the business you buy, 10 percent knowledge, 10 percent talent, and 70 percent don’t F-ing stop. Grit is the secret ingredient that makes it all work.
			

Here’s what you can expect during the journey to becoming a Main Street Millionaire:
			

You won’t look cool. There’s no drop-shipping, no crypto, no start-ups, no inventing, and no picture of you on the cover of Forbes. If you want to be the next Steve Jobs or Elon Musk, I am not your girl. If you want to run a pleasure parlor in the Metaverse, be my guest. But you ain’t gonna find it here. Why? Because all those paths share the same fatal flaw: your revenue starts at $0.
			
				
			
			

Instead, you’ll take the road less traveled. The road with cash flow that offers healthy profits and a monthly salary on Day 1. The road where you won’t spend years of your life on a gamble, or slowly saving away until you’re too old to enjoy the money. Does this path look cool? Not particularly. But is it cool to get rich quietly? You tell me.
			

You won’t be an overnight success. If you’re just getting started, I advocate for the 3-9-12 framework. You take 3 months to learn, 9 months to find and close your first deal, and 12 months to stabilize the business. That’s a total of two years, or about as long as it takes to get an MBA. Patience is the playbook, and that’s where the profits lie. I go back to when Jeff Bezos, founder of Amazon, asked Warren Buffett, “If your investing thesis is so simple, why doesn’t everyone just copy you?” Buffett’s response: “Because nobody wants to get rich slowly.”
			
				
					

3-9-12 Business-Buying Framework
				
				
					
				
			
			

Your income won’t be passive. This journey is for those who want meaning and fulfillment, but NOT those who want to buy a business for “passive income” and spend their days sipping piña coladas on a beach halfway across the world. Diabetes much? You will have to work hard to become an owner. Whether you are buying a repair shop, landscaping company, garbage-hauling service, car wash, laundromat, or murder-scene cleanup operation, there is one common thread: When you own it, you gotta work on it. This includes managing employees, suppliers, customers, vendors, administrative tasks, marketing, sales, and so much more. It is not passive, nor is it for the faint of heart. Which brings me to the most important point…
			

This won’t be easy. I’ve been sued, lied to, stolen from, made fun of, slandered, and even thought one of my employees was coming back for blood. If you know you’re going to give up when the going gets hard, I’d suggest you put down the book now. Seriously, just save yourself the time. Not everyone is cut out to be in charge, and that is okay. There’s a reason most people settle into jobs instead of stretching for ownership. It certainly feels safer to let someone else take all the responsibility and risk. And yet, salary is the ultimate addiction, a job the ultimate golden handcuffs.
			

Having a company is hard—responsibilities, lies, slander. Having a job is hard—underpaid, underpromoted, underchallenged, bosses you hate. Start-ups are hard—no money, sleepless nights, dealing with investors. It’s all hard. You choose your hard. I will always choose the path to freedom. Yes, there are risks on the path to becoming a Main Street Millionaire, though it’s not as risky as you might think, especially when done right.
			

Bottom line: If you choose this path, you’ll need to fully commit. Things will get hard. You have been warned. But if you’re like me, I hope you’re getting a little tingly and feeling the truth that the rich know all too well: Behind a little pain is all the gain.
			
			• • •
			
			

Why should you listen to me? Let’s start at the beginning of my entrepreneurial journey. I’ll never forget the day I bought a laundromat nearly a decade ago. It was my first real step toward financial freedom. But that day, it didn’t feel like it. My heart raced while my mind freaked out.
			

As I turned the key and shoved my shoulder into the jammed door, I paused to breathe it all in. That sweet smell of detergent…and mold. Ahhh. And those charming fluorescent lights clinging on to the popcorn ceiling tiles. That soft whistling of shiny washing machines. My washing machines. I’d finally done it.
			

Then the doubts flashed through my mind: Codie, WTF have you gotten yourself into? You’re going to get fired from your job. This is a horrible idea, everyone is going to laugh at you. You know nothing about washing machines. You barely do your own laundry. Yet for some reason, your existential crisis is becoming a laundromat owner?! I batted the thoughts away. For better or worse, everything was about to change.
			

In my first year, I made a modest profit. Not earth-shattering, and not enough to quit my full-time job. But it gave me hope. I never could have imagined that less than a decade later, that little laundromat would lead to a not-so-little portfolio of twenty-four Main Street businesses that do tens of millions in revenue, and a fund with companies that do hundreds of millions. As a mid-thirties Latina woman who used to think a mutual fund meant “group fun,” it’s still hard for me to believe.
			

I wasn’t born into wealth. I’m not that smart. My parents were hardworking Americans. My dad worked at and then one day finally owned a local marble company. My mom was a special needs teacher. Growing up out West in Arizona, I learned how to track a deer before I learned about money. My high school math teacher literally told me Helen Keller had a better chance at winning an archery contest than I did at excelling in math (nice guy). Nothing about my childhood hinted at a future in which I’d become a voice for financial empowerment.
			

But then, after college, I went to work as a journalist in Juárez, Mexico. The harsh realities of reporting on human trafficking taught me a valuable lesson: Money is power. That realization inspired me to dive into the belly of the beast. I worked for financial firms like Vanguard, State Street, and Goldman Sachs so I could master wealth-building strategies. That led me to start buying and owning businesses for myself, and publicly sharing what I learned online. My former bosses were less than thrilled. I keep a quote to this day from an email one sent me: “You continue to dumb down our firm, I hope you gained more followers today.” Dan sucked then and still probably sucks.
			

Yet I’d rather help a huge number of ordinary people get rich together than help a small group of already rich people get obscenely wealthy. I watched Dan and all those dudes on their second and third wives racing around in Ferraris and boasting about who bought the most expensive bottle of wine, and I thought…These guys are the ones who run our world? Time for a reboot.
			

So today, I share their homework at our financial media company, Contrarian Thinking. At the time of this writing, we have over six million social media followers, one million email subscribers, and five thousand–plus students. Our community has reported millions and millions in revenue bought, transitioning over one hundred companies to new ownership. I happen to think we’re just getting started on stealing Dan’s lunch. Our mission is to transform America into a nation of owners so we can save our country’s Main Street businesses, and so you never have to work for an insufferable boss again.
			

Main Street Millionaire is my call to action. With this book, I aim to guide thousands of readers toward owning profitable businesses. I hope you’re one of them. Because as much as I’d love to help you get rich, there’s a far bigger and more pressing issue at stake. Unless we start a movement to save these Main Street businesses, we’re all in serious trouble.
			

## The Secret Gold Mine on Main Street

			
				
			
			

Did you know that Main Street Millionaires own half of all job-generating businesses in the United States? That’s nearly three million companies providing jobs for thirty-two million people and making $6.5 trillion in annual revenue. If you enjoy a nice paycheck right now, it is highly likely you can thank a Main Street Millionaire for that.
			

But there is a dark side to being a Main Street Millionaire: they’re getting too old for this sh*t. Talk to any boomer business owner over the age of sixty-five, buy them a beer or six, and they will admit that they want to exit their business…but have no idea how.
			

Throughout history, the answer was to hand the business over to the kids. Ah, the times they are a-changin’. These days, only about 30 percent of businesses transition from one family generation to the next. The kids don’t want Dad’s plumbing business. They want to dance on TikTok, even if plumbing makes millions.
			

Here’s the craziest part: most of these MSMs will end up permanently shutting down their businesses. When they retire, they won’t hand off or even sell their cash-printing machines. Instead, they will simply turn off the lights and put the Closed sign up one last time. Game over.
			

Don’t believe me? It’s already happening, with businesses all over the world.
			

Hidekazu Yokoyama spent thirty years building a profitable, thriving logistics business in Japan. Last year, he tried to give it away, for free. He is not alone. In a country where the average business owner is sixty-two, and where 60 percent of them don’t have a transition plan, free is often the only option.
			

Hidekazu is in his seventies, and his legacy was wanted by no one. Not his kids. Not his employees. No one. Think about that for a second. It’s not like he was trying to give away a dilapidated, fixer-upper house in an undesirable location. He was trying to give away a successful business worth millions. And no one wanted it!
			

This struggle of an aging population could be the cause of one of the most devastating recessions in Japan, as tens of thousands of small and medium-size companies go out of business while the population shrinks. Do you know what will happen to Japan if that transpires?
			

In an apocalyptic 2019 presentation, Japan’s trade ministry said that by 2025, around 630,000 profitable businesses could close permanently, costing the economy $165 billion and as many as 6.5 million jobs. That’s nearly 1 in 10 jobs in the whole country.
			

It is already happening. In 2021, forty-four thousand businesses were abandoned. And 90 percent of those businesses—most of which were profitable and could have been transitioned to a new owner—closed forever. This still blows my mind.
			

If the magnitude of this problem isn’t hitting you yet, I promise you it will. It hit me and my family. My uncle founded Eb Holmes Plumbing in Phoenix, a thriving business that made him millions of dollars for several decades. At its height it was worth several million. Then one day, Eb got older and sicker. Suddenly, the company meant much less to him than spending time with his wife and kids.
			

So, what did Eb do with the business? Did he sell it and make millions? Hand it off to a member of his family or one of his trusted employees? Nope, neither. He slowly closed it down. In fact, he had to pay money, and his wife had to do a lot of work to shut it down compliantly.
			

On the one hand, it’s understandable. Eb was in his seventies. He was tired. He didn’t think of selling his business as an option; I think he would say it wasn’t a sellable asset. Or that even if he’d wanted to do it, he didn’t have the energy to go through a sale or find buyers to buy it from him.
			

Still, this plumbing business was generating millions in revenue. Eb spent his entire career turning it into a cash-flowing asset, which allowed him and my aunt to live the American Dream. It allowed them to take boat trips down to Mexico. It even bought them a little two-prop plane and a cabin in the mountains.
			

But it didn’t matter. Eb’s business first scaled down, then shut down. His customers, his team, his processes…poof. All gone. Don’t get me wrong, his incredible legacy continues on in his family and impact, BUT could it also have lived on in the actual business?
			

As tragic as this story sounds, it’s a lot more common than you think. And like I said, it’s playing out all over the world, and it’s coming to your neighborhood soon. This is the big emergency we’re all facing. Millions of boomer business owners are stranded in their companies, with no one to hand it off to. Their businesses are too big for their competitors to buy out yet too small for Wall Street to acquire. So, the owners just keep working, and working, and working.
			

The impact of millions of small businesses quietly disappearing over the next two decades will be catastrophic to our economy. It’s going to make the Great Depression seem like a cakewalk.
			

And therein lies your opportunity. My dear reader, you should be leaning forward in your seat by now. This is where your freedom awaits. YOU could be the new owner. YOU could be their succession plan. YOU could preserve their legacy, serve your community, gain financial freedom, and help save America. These owners are quietly desperate to save their businesses. They are praying for a miracle. They are praying for YOU.
			

This is the gold mine on Main Street.
			
				
					

Boomers are Stranded in Their Businesses
				
				
					
				
			
			
			• • •
			

Now it’s time for your story. The book you’re holding is your guide to ownership and financial freedom. It is also a giant “F you” to those who want us to beg for scraps while they own everything. The content in these chapters has helped people from all walks of life achieve these outcomes. That’s why I believe in you and your right to live free. Because if I can do it, and they can do it, so can you.
			

None of this is rocket science. It’s a simple process that you can successfully execute, so long as you remain committed to becoming an owner. I wish you the heartbreakingly beautiful feeling of being in charge of your own destiny. Nothing quite like it. The one who sets the hours can usually work the longest. So, if you have the drive and follow the steps, the only thing that can stop you from becoming rich and free is you.
			
				
			
			
				

Skip Notes
				
					

*1 Also a Taylor Swift lyric, but I digress.
				
				
					

*2 Disclosure: The testimonials provided are real results from clients who have worked with me, but please understand that these results are not typical. Your results will vary and depend on many factors, including but not limited to your background, experience, and work ethic. All business entails risk, as well as massive effort and consistent action. If you’re not willing to accept that, please do not proceed with this book. Seriously. As my grandma says, “Business ain’t for p*ssies.” And yes, my grandma did say that.

Step 1: R Is for Research, Main Street Millionaire

---

Your Perfect Fit Business: Define the Right Acquisition for You, Main Street Millionaire
		
		
		

	
	
		
			

## Your Perfect Fit Business: Define the Right Acquisition for You

			

## Choosing Your Starting Point

			

The most common DM I get goes something like this:
			

What type of boring business should I buy for cash flow?
			

What they want to hear from me is that they should go out and buy a laundromat, car wash, mini storage, or pack-and-ship center. But those are horrible answers because it’s the wrong question to begin with.
			

The better question to ask is this:
			

What type of boring business is right for me?
			

The truth is there is no such thing as the right or wrong business. There is only the business that’s right or wrong for you.
			

Buying a business isn’t one-size-fits-all; it’s a deeply personal decision that should be in sync with who you are, what you can handle, and where you see your life heading. The worst thing you can do is dive headfirst into a business that’s completely misaligned with your financial needs, risk tolerance, and lifestyle preferences.
			

So, before we talk about the “what,” we need to talk about the “who.” That’s you.
			

I want to set you up for success by ensuring you pick the right starting point. Your first task is to choose the level best suited to who you are and where you’re going. Businesses change materially at each level, requiring a different type of operator and buyer. So it’s critical that you start at the right level for you. (Spoiler alert: most readers of this book will be at either Level 1 or 2.) This way, not only will you be set up for financial success, you’ll also have greater satisfaction and peace of mind.
			

## Competition Is for Losers: Why Small Businesses Are Easier to Buy

			

Most people who talk about acquisitions refer to businesses doing $100 million or more in annual revenue. In other words, Fortune 1000 companies and the like, which the average Joe cannot afford. Fortunately, that is an advantage for us. All the buyers are competing for the top part of the pyramid, overlooking profitable small businesses. This is where the opportunities lie, in a sea with plenty of fish and surprisingly few fishermen.
			
				
			
			

## Level 1: Solo Venture

			

This level is for you if: You’ve never bought a business before. You’re a bit scared (it’s okay, I was there too). You want to acquire a business and still be able to maintain your W-2 income or just don’t want a business that requires you to put in a full-time effort. You also want a business that’s easy to run and doesn’t require a lot of training or highly skilled employees.
			

What you can expect: The books are going to be a mess. The processes are probably not going to be outlined. The owner doesn’t need to be there 24/7. But you are going to be doing the work. That said, solo ventures are much less expensive, much less complex, and can often be run as a part-time job (not a business).
			

Examples of Level 1 businesses: laundromats, vending machines, ice-vending machines, self-serve car washes, self-serve storage centers, camping sites, mailbox centers, and beyond.
			

## The numbers:

			
				
- 
					

Cost to acquire: less than $1 million
				
				
- 
					

Annual revenue: less than $300,000
				
				
- 
					

Total profits: between $50,000 and $200,000
				
				
- 
					

Multiples you can sell it for: 1–2X
				
				
- 
					

Your annual salary: less than $200,000
				
				
- 
					

Number of employees: fewer than 3 (potentially none)
				
				
- 
					

Time commitment: 10 to 30 hours per week
				
			
			

## Level 2: Hands-On CEO

			

This level is for you if: You have more cash, higher income, management experience, and a higher risk tolerance. The risk is greater, because you’re borrowing more money, or the businesses are more expensive and profitable and you’re likely dealing with more employees and FF&E (furniture, fixtures, and equipment). This is you essentially buying a CEO job. The main benefit is getting out of the corporate/W-2 world, and even though you’re still “working” for a living, you get to at least work for yourself with the potential to build something better.
			

What you can expect: You may run the business yourself, or you may hire an experienced operator (though at this stage you might not have enough cash to start with that; next level we can get to hiring your own CEO). The business is likely to have tech-enabled books, some knowledge base of systems and processes, and a second tier of management. When sh*t goes wrong at this stage, it’s on you; it is a very hands-on, owner-centric business.
			

Examples of Level 2 businesses: So many industries can fall into this category. It includes almost every industry on Main Street—contractor/trades (although licensing is potentially an issue), cleaning services, education (preschools), etc. So pick your industry.
			

## The numbers:

			
				
- 
					

Cost to acquire: less than $5 million
				
				
- 
					

Annual revenue: less than $3 million
				
				
- 
					

Total profits: less than $1 million
				
				
- 
					

Multiples you can sell it for: 3–5X
				
				
- 
					

Your annual salary: less than $750,000
				
				
- 
					

Number of employees: fewer than 15
				
				
- 
					

Time commitment: You can likely afford an operator or a manager to run the business alongside you. Either way, someone will need to work full time on this business, but you may not have to run it all yourself.
				
			
			

## Level 3: On-Deck Operator

			

This level is for you if: You’re comfortable with the complexities of larger businesses, including managing a team of twenty to fifty people and implementing advanced systems and processes. You have access to capital or the ability to raise capital; you have management experience and the ability to scale a business toward the lower-middle market (LMM) and sell for a higher multiple to private equity firms.
			

What you can expect: A structured hierarchy with tiered management, consistent growth metrics, streamlined processes, and an operator already inside the business. The owner can certainly be CEO, but it’s large enough for that to be optional.
			

Multiple of profit or EBITDA:[*1] 3–8X
			

Examples of Level 3 businesses: The characteristics here are not sectors but size of the company.
			

## The numbers:

			
				
- 
					

Cost to acquire: less than $10 million
				
				
- 
					

Annual revenue: $3–$10 million
				
				
- 
					

Total profits: $1–$5 million
				
				
- 
					

Multiples you can sell it for: 3–8X
				
				
- 
					

Your annual salary: $300,000–$1 million (really depends on your level of CEO)
				
				
- 
					

Number of employees: 20 to 50
				
				
- 
					

Time commitment: 10 to 40 hours per week, with a management team handling day-to-day operations
				
			
			

## Level 4: Market Leader

			

This level is for you if: You’re ready to take on competition with institutional investors like private equity firms. Likely scenario is that there’s an operator in place but there will certainly be owner-operator opportunities if desired. This is the last level we’re recommending that most readers look at. If you go any higher you have serious competition and serious costs. At this level, you have significant capital at your disposal, experience in acquisitions, a deep understanding of operations, and the capability to scale a business. You’re not just playing the game; you’re leading it.
			

What you can expect: Established and extensive client bases, mature processes, and a skilled workforce. These are sizable operations that may already have a strong middle-management layer and potentially several layers of staff. You’ll be involved in strategic decision-making, overseeing operations, and driving growth.
			

## The numbers:

			
				
- 
					

Cost to acquire: less than $15 million
				
				
- 
					

Annual revenue: $5–15 million
				
				
- 
					

Total profits: $1–5 million
				
				
- 
					

Multiples you can sell it for: 5–10X
				
				
- 
					

Your annual salary: $600,000–$2 million
				
				
- 
					

Number of employees: 25 to 100
				
				
- 
					

Time commitment: Your involvement could vary widely, from strategic oversight requiring 20 to 30 hours per week to a more engaged role demanding 40-plus hours.
				
			
			
				
				

Level 1
				

Micro Acquisition for a Solo Operator
				

Good for less experienced first-time buyer
				

The Numbers
				
					
- 
						

Cost to acquire: <$1 million
					
					
- 
						

Annual revenue: <$300,000
					
					
- 
						

Number of employees: <3 (potentially none)
					
				
				
				

Level 2
				

Tiny Acquisition
				

More experienced first- or second-time buyer
				

The Numbers
				
					
- 
						

Cost to acquire: <$5 million
					
					
- 
						

Annual revenue: <$3 million
					
					
- 
						

Number of employees: <15
					
				
				
				

Level 3
				

Very Small Business
				

VSBMid-Level
				

Experienced buyer or investor
				

The Numbers
				
					
- 
						

Owner operated or Operator run
					
					
- 
						

Cost to acquire: <$10 million
					
					
- 
						

Annual revenue: $3-$10 million
					
					
- 
						

Number of employees: 20 to 50
					
				
				
				

Level 4
				

Small-Business Acquisition
				

Big Acquisition
				

Very experienced buyer, institutional level
				

The Numbers
				
					
- 
						

Owner operated or Operator run
					
					
- 
						

Cost to acquire: <$15 million
					
					
- 
						

Annual revenue: $5-$15 million
					
					
- 
						

Number of employees: 25 to 100
					
				
			
			

## Level 5: Corporate Battleground

			

In this book, we aren’t going to play at this level. We’ll leave this to Wall Street and their Gucci loafers for now. (Fun sidenote: Gucci loafers in Wall Street speak are called deal sleds. I don’t know why, I don’t make the rules. Also: nerds.)
			

Remember, you’re not going to be starting a company. You’re going to be buying a business—with as little of your own money as possible and a win-win for you and the owner—earning profits on Day 1. That is the goal with smart structuring. You do not have to be a surveyor to own a surveying business; you just need to find a business where the cash flow is enough for you to hire the right people, including subject matter and industry experts, all while taking home a few Benjamins for yourself.
			

## My Four Favorite Types of Businesses to Buy

			

Over the years, I’ve discovered what businesses are right for me. That’s what we’re going to do for you next. While I started my business-buying career at Level 1 with laundromats and deals below $300K, I now only buy businesses at Level 2 and above. Here are my four favorites:
			

## 1. Digital Businesses

			

Granted, these are not “boring businesses.” They are also competitive to buy and scale. Still, I love them because of these four words: Build once, sell forever. Internet businesses have a near-zero cost for additional users. No other type of business touches that, so huge upside here. Digital businesses are easier to scale and market using search-engine optimization (SEO), content marketing, pay-per-click, and other methods. They typically have better net profit margins because they don’t have real estate or hard-asset expenses. On the website Flippa, you can find established profitable websites selling for 1X to 4X net profit. I prefer completely online businesses without physical inventory, but I have friends who have made hundreds of millions with e-commerce as well. Just remember: if you are going to play on the interwebs, do not forget the Lindy effect and the methods you’ll learn in this book. Here we buy profitable realities, not start-up dreams.
			

## 2. Consumer or Home Services

			

Consumer services are largely sold to individuals (B2C), as opposed to organizations (B2B). Think roofing, landscaping, pool cleaning, and so on. These are similar to B2B services but can be more volatile. For instance, an established home-cleaning business might have mostly regularly scheduled clients, making the margins more reliable. They’re also scalable and easy to finance through the seller or Small Business Administration. I buy these bad boys all day long. The hardest parts are the labor, logistics, and licensing, as well as implementing systems up front.
			

## 3. Professional Services or Labor-Moated Businesses

			

Professional services are businesses that provide services usually accompanied by a unique skill set, certification, training, or specialty. These include accounting, advertising, marketing, and legal services. They are some of my favorite businesses to buy because they have more of a moat around them (you need a CPA to be an accountant; you only need two hands to cut lawns). This is why I call them labor-moated businesses. Your labor needs a license, certification, and registration, which creates a slight moat for others wanting to enter the market. People who provide these services typically charge a premium, have recurring revenue components, and can also sell B2B or B2C. I get all worked up when I think about turning them into a productized professional service.[*2] It makes them easier to sell and manage, but I’ll get to how to do that later.
			
				
					

Labor Moated
				
				
					
				
			
			

## 4. Real Estate Enhanced Businesses

			

Here’s my bread and butter. A real estate enhanced business is any business that has a large portion of the acquisition price assigned to the real estate but a cash-flowing component above and beyond typical real estate. Think the good ole laundromat, car wash, self-storage, mobile-home park, camping site. These straddle the realms of real estate and business, in my opinion. I love adding complementary businesses (aka “satellites”) to these. For instance, owning a mobile-home park plus a property management company, a roofing company, and a landscaping company…which are all servicing the mobile-home park. Talk dirty to me.
			
				
			
			

## The Seven Deadly Businesses: Avoid These at All Costs

			

I opened my email to a frantic note from Mary (name changed because that would be rude), one of my business-buying community members. She’d bought her first business, and it was not going well. Losing money, partnership imploding, and an incoming lawsuit. All the things that go bump in the night. How did this happen?
			

To make matters worse: Mary was a new mom with a baby that needed her, and I couldn’t see any way out of her losing her $100,000 on this deal. Mary, oh Mary. She was in the arena, and it was hard. But this all could have been avoided.
			

Here’s what Mary did. She bought a restaurant franchise that was losing money. Strike one. Then she did a fifty-fifty partnership deal with someone she hadn’t done business with before. He brought no money to the table, but got all 50 percent of his equity on the very first day. Strike two. Then, she and her partner hired an inexperienced operator. Strike three. Cherry on top: the business was a three-hour flight from Mary’s home.
			

Dear, sweet Mary, what the literal F.
			

We eventually got Mary out of this deal, thankfully. But even if she’d bought the restaurant and done everything else right, she still would have found herself in a losing situation. Why? Because some businesses are deadly to your money—restaurants being one of them—and you should avoid them at all costs. Yes, you want to find the right business for you, but if that business has such a high failure rate that you know you’re going to lose, treat it like a land mine. Do not go anywhere near it.
			

Whenever I buy a boring business, I’m obsessed with one thing: probabilities. What is the likelihood that money leaving my hands comes back with more money as friends? That is the only game we play, and those are the only businesses we buy. So let’s talk about these rascal businesses that love to murder your money, shall we?
			

## 1. Restaurants

			

I don’t care if you’re a “foodie,” your burgers are legendary, and your buddies all tell you that you should have your own restaurant. Don’t do it. Restaurants are for people who simply cannot exist without running one. They ain’t for making money. Am I just an a-hole? No, I’m realistic. The numbers don’t lie. The average small business in the United States sells for around $800,000; the average restaurant goes for only $198,000. Why? Because 60 percent fail in the first year and 80 percent after four years. The restaurant business is one tough slog. I mean, can you imagine wrangling servers all day? Guessing if fickle crowds will eat your three-day-old shrimp? No wonder banks don’t lend to restaurants.[*3]
			

## 2. Hotels

			

Hotels aren’t businesses. They’re real estate with too many parts masquerading as a business. The cash flow typically isn’t enough to cover the real estate transaction. If you are dying to own one, do a small version before you go mega. Otherwise: pass. These things are expensive, asset heavy, have massive wear and tear, and often include…restaurants. They’re also one of the few businesses where your customers require your help 24/7. If you are a top top top-tier operator, you can make a killing. But don’t jump into shark-infested waters to start. Pass.
			

## 3. Retail Storefronts

			

It’s every lady’s dream to start a little boutique with all their favorite things, have friends come in, and sell as many “Live, Laugh, Love” tees as their heart desires. Boutique owning can be a fun job—for a bored, very rich housewife who wants to pretend she runs a business. Small problem—her business is awesome at only one thing: burning money. To be fair, it’s not just ladies. Many a gent wants to own that store with a bourbon bar and clothes that are “actually cool, man.” Nine times out of ten, they are terrible businesses. The future in retail continues to be online, except in high-traffic “experience”-based areas (the types of stores that are focused on creating memorable, interactive experiences for customers rather than just selling products). Just ask the flagship Dillard’s, a 200,000-square-foot dark and empty store. If I had to own retail, I’d own luxury, because rich people pay more to solve their problems. But unless you’re buying Louis Vuitton or Christian Dior (both of which were massively distressed and had to be turned around through a merger now known as LVMH), hard pass. Here’s why:
			
				
- 
					

You buy product up front (no float). No float means you have to spend your money up front on inventory. This ties up your cash without a guarantee that the products will sell. The items are shipped to you, and you need to stock them and likely hold them for a season. Leftover inventory? Too bad. Order new fall styles in summer even though you can’t sell them for sixty to ninety days? Ouch.
				
				
- 
					

You pay high rent for high traffic. The cash-flow realities of a high-overhead business model like specialty retail are not to be understated. Most won’t ever tell you how much they really make. A lot of these mom-and-pop stores are funded by people with big pockets, as a defective side project, or to bolster a brand’s online e-commerce business. You live and die by sales per square foot, but in order to get enough volume, you typically have to pay higher rent.
				
				
- 
					

The financials are tough. The high turnover of inventory necessary to keep customers coming back gives me more issues than I’m willing to deal with. What do I stock? When? How do I ensure my lower-pay employees aren’t stealing? How come no one will give me a loan to start this business? Those are all fair questions. Your largest problem won’t be your gross margin—the percentage you make on each item you buy and then sell—it’ll be your net profit, and getting enough volume to handle your fixed costs.
				
			
			

## 4. Consulting Firms

			

Consulting firms have one thing more dangerous to a business than almost anything else: key person risk. When the head-honcho consultant with all the relationships leaves, there goes your business right along with him. One of the kisses of death to enjoying running a business is playing the RFP (request for proposal) game, which means your clients get to ask you to do work for them for free up front, to prove you are going to do work well for them later. I’m a pass. Show me the money, then we can talk RFPs. Even at a smaller size, consulting firms tie up too much business capital in individual consultants. This is not a business but a collection of individual jobs. Don’t buy jobs. There are a couple of exceptions to the rule:
			
				
- 
					

Government consulting firms owned by a member of a specialty group. For example, veteran-owned firms are often given preferential treatment if you transfer to another veteran when you sell. These have long-term contracts often with strong margins.
				
				
- 
					

Having a highly established consulting firm with many levels of management, more than one key consultant, and ideally productized services that are not customizable.
				
			
			

## 5. Personal Brands

			

Until AI completely takes over and can deep-fake mimic everything, you can’t transition someone’s personal brand to yourself. I’ve seen lots of people try to sell their face-branded YouTube account, social site, or education business (with their face all over it). It doesn’t really work. This, too, is a key person risk. Once someone sells their business, they are about as engaged in running it as your cat is in coming when called. Unless you tie the deal to a ton of milestones and performance-based earn-outs—where part of the payout hinges on hitting future targets—it will be a slog that ends in failure.
			

If you insist on doing one of these deals, do me a solid: buy the rights to their likeness also. A famous(ish) example of this is James Altucher. He sold his newsletter to Agora Financial (for I’d guess eight figures), and they had the right to email for a set period of time. So no one knew the difference between Altucher’s newsletter and Agora Financial’s. Downside: I would never sell my likeness. Next thing you know, I’d be in your inbox in a bikini asking you to buy crypto. Now you see both sides of the coin.
			

## 6. Amazon FBA/Drop-Shipping

			

Fulfillment by Amazon (FBA) doesn’t sound like a bad idea at first glance. You get to leverage Amazon’s massive scale and logistics by letting them handle the fulfillment of your orders. All you need to do is set up the product listing pages, then Amazon takes care of the rest. They’ll send you the customers via search, they’ll handle product storage, shipping, and handling, all while you collect the checks. Heck, you can even pay one of those scammy TikTokers $10K and they’ll set up your store for you!
			

But here’s the dirty truth: the only way people can reliably make money with drop-shipping through Fulfillment by Amazon is to teach others how to do it. Here’s why I would never, ever do this type of business:
			
				
- 
					

Platform risk: Amazon controls everything. The biggest feature is the biggest bug. What happens when Amazon decides your product is a scam because a competitor puts up a bunch of fake reviews? That happens. I know a seller who was doing $1.5 million in sales, had his account banned for fake negative reviews, and couldn’t get it back for ninety days, by which time he’d gone out of business. Another crippling scenario: Amazon gets all your data on how incredible your niche is and duplicates your product with a cheaper version of their own. They instantly become the number-one search result and your sales drop permanently.
				
				
- 
					

Insanely competitive: The barriers to entry to this game are lower than FTX’s actual balance-sheet assets. Amazon is flooded with me-too sellers pushing their cheesy knockoffs of popular products. The Chinese, in particular, are remarkably fast at copycatting products. Their speed is almost as impressive as their indifference to the words “intellectual property.” Chinese sellers outnumber U.S. sellers by a wide margin on Amazon. It’s just a matter of time before they crush you.
				
				
- 
					

Amazon sets your price: Amazon uses a bot army to scrape other websites for pricing information. If they see your prices as too high, they may shadow-ban your account, meaning you get deprioritized on the website, which is a sales killer. So if you want to sell higher on your own website or elsewhere, it’s a no. You have to give Amazon your lowest price even though they take a cut. Even if you sell your own branded products on Amazon, it doesn’t mean that you get to set your own prices.
				
			
			

## 7. Dry Cleaners

			

What?! Codie loves laundromats and hates dry cleaning businesses? Boy, do I. Dry cleaners appear to be a stable, reliable business. But the truth is different. The chemicals used in dry cleaning are often toxic, and if you’re not up to code, the cleanup costs can be astronomical. It also makes it extremely difficult to find reliable employees who want to stick around.
			

Rule: Avoid deadly businesses. They typically have some combination of high failure rates, asymmetric risks, high expenses, and low transferability. We don’t want that.
			
				
					

Is it a Deadly Business?
				
				
					
				
			
			

Now that you know the seven deadly businesses to avoid, we can move on to the next step in the process. Let’s talk about what you want as an owner.
			

## Know Thyself: Defining What You Want from Your Business

			
				

The more clarity you get as to who you want to become, the quicker you can start making the choices needed to get you there.
				

—Robin Sharma, author of The Monk Who Sold His Ferrari
				

A goal properly set is halfway reached.
				

—Zig Ziglar
			
			

Have you ever noticed that the clearer you are on what you want, the faster and easier it is to get it? On the flip side, the more vague you are about what you want, the harder it is to stay motivated and go after it. You can only hit the bull’s-eye when you have a clear target. That applies 10X when you’re buying a business.
			

If you want to achieve ninja-level status at this game, you can’t just say, “Welp, guess I’ll buy a laundromat because Codie did it.” No, no, no. You want the right business for you. That means you need to be super clear about what you want to get out of your deal, what you bring to the table, and what specific criteria your business needs to meet.
			

That’s what you’re going to do now. Grab a pen and paper, because these exercises will help you find the business that fits you like a deliciously worn boot.
			

## Step 1: Uncover Your Zone of Genius

			

The first step is to find out what you uniquely bring to the table. To buy the right business for you, you need to be self-aware and have a realistic understanding of your strengths. Most humans do not have this awareness, so I got you. A simple way to achieve this is to draw a Venn diagram, like this one:
			
				
					

Your Zone of Genius is your Business Love Language
				
				
					
				
			
			

Label the circles Passion, Experience and Skills, and Network. Then write the following inside each of those circles:
			

Passion. What topics or activities can you engage in for hours without getting bored? Or when was the last time you got so lost in something that time became irrelevant? Do you love solving difficult problems? Can you not shut up about the new copy you wrote for a website? Are you endlessly tinkering on your woodworking project? This is your business love language. List everything you love to do, even if it doesn’t seem directly related to business.
			

Experience and Skills. Reflect on what you’re good at. These are the tasks that you can do better than most people you know. You don’t need to have a PhD; you just have to be better than the average Joe. Consider the skills you have that could translate well into running a business, such as logistics, people management, marketing, or sales. When we talk about skills, it’s never just one thing. It’s what Scott Adams calls your skill stack, the unique combination of your skills. It’s extremely difficult to be in the top 1 percent in any single skill, but being in the top 10 percent? You can do that. And if you can be in the top 10 percent in three or four skills, you now have a world-class skill stack.
			

Warning: Do not write about your skill or passion for turning horrible situations around. Otherwise you could fool yourself into thinking that you should buy a failing business. Derelict businesses and turnarounds are really hard. Only play that game if you know what you’re doing, are a sucker for pain, get turned on by lawsuits, and have the capital. You can make millions doing it (check out Cerberus Capital), but for most people, it leads to a miserable life and is generally a very bad idea.
			

Network. Find your tribe, as the kids say these days. Or in other words, who do you know who could help make your future business a success? These could be people you already know and work with—like colleagues, service providers, mentors, suppliers, or customers—or they could be total strangers who happen to live in your community. Think broadly here. Networks aren’t just professional contacts; they can be family, friends, local groups, even online connections. I like to imagine a boardroom table and then think about who would be sitting in each chair. Oh, there’s my dad for help with commercial real estate; there’s Charlie, who is a master at branding; and I don’t want to forget about Lloyd, who already runs a laundromat, since I want to buy one. Build your board in your mind—they don’t even need to know they’re on it!
			

Feeling stuck? Take a look at this example:
			
				
- 
					

Passion: John loves to build things and help his community. He loves the idea of being the go-to guy, with his team around town, helping locals and seeing his work in the real world. He’s passionate about building things that last and making the operations run smoothly. You should see his toolshed—organized to perfection.
				
				
- 
					

Experience and Skills: John played with train sets as a kid. If things are on track and on time he’s in his happy place. This is called logistics. He is also that guy everyone goes to when they need direction. He has a knack for seeing ways that employees can spend their time doing what they do best. He’s great with technology and understands how to leverage online tools to automate processes and be more efficient. Cool, John has a skill stack of logistics, leadership, and tech enablement.
				
				
- 
					

Network: Turns out, John also has a brother-in-law who is a tradesman in plumbing. John has paid him to come fix plumbing issues at his house, and his brother-in-law is always telling him how busy he is. John never thought that was important before, but he has read this book, so now he’s getting that tingly feeling. John has always been pushing his brother-in-law to get on a text follow-up system, use a CRM (customer relationship management) tool, and systematize referrals. But a sideline quarterback ain’t that helpful. Wait—could John buy a plumbing company with his brother-in-law’s expertise, or maybe with him? Let his brother-in-law do the fixing he loves, and John adds tech, processes, and hires employees?
				
			
			

After reviewing his answers in the three circles, running them past his wife and brother-in-law, John decides his Zone of Genius might translate well to running a plumbing company. He writes “Plumbing” in the center of the Venn diagram, and there goes that tingle again. It feels right. Then, because he wants to have a few options, he searches “plumbing-adjacent businesses.” He finds commercial plumbing and residential plumbing. Hmm. He realizes that plumbing companies often offer HVAC services (heating, ventilation, and air-conditioning). He continues the search and sees that septic tank installation could also be part of his sphere. Ooh, maybe a landscape architecture firm to fix the front yard after he redoes the plumbing! Nice. John now sees that his little niche plumbing business could be a wedge into several satellite businesses.
			

You’re going to carry your Zone of Genius sheet with you as you explore potential business ventures. You’ll mentally refer to it during your upcoming discussions with owners, where you’ll be better equipped to see what’s right for you—and what’s not. Half the battle is knowing who you’re fighting.
			

For instance, say you come across a great manufacturing company in your hometown. It’s trading at an amazing valuation, the profits are beyond your expectations, and the seller is motivated to keep it in the hands of a local. You think, Holy sh*t. I should buy this business! But then you pull out your Zone of Genius sheet, and you’re reminded that Oh yeah, I’m not detail oriented. I’m a big-picture visionary. I like to travel. That’s why I’m hardly in my hometown. I’m great at visualizing things, but not at building things. I love online commerce, cryptocurrency, and video advertising across multiple screens. As you tuck the sheet back in your pocket, you realize, I would not feel comfortable with a manufacturing company. Great! You just saved yourself from a potential nightmare!
			

Let’s say it together again: there is no such thing as a “good business to buy,” there’s only the right business for you. The best deal you could ever do is one that feels like it was specifically made for you. So, if you’re a doctor, you might look for a doctor’s office to buy, or a company that sells surgical tools or software for doctor’s offices. If you’ve been a landlord or an apartment-complex manager, you might look to buy property management companies. This isn’t rocket science, but it’s where most people go sideways.
			

The goal of this first exercise is to build a tangible connection between your intrinsic qualities and the real-world opportunities that await you. By uncovering your Zone of Genius, you’ll be better equipped to find a business that both fulfills you personally and succeeds financially. This is where I am completely different from private equity in how I think about acquisitions. Private equity wants a few things: cash flow, repeatability, scalability, upside potential, resale potential, and recurring revenue. They largely do not care about “happiness” in their metrics. We are going to layer “not hating your life” into this equation. You’ll thank me later.
			
				
			
			

## Step 2: Describe Your Ideal Owner Experience

			

Vision boarding, but for business. That’s basically this step. Describe what you’re hoping to get from this whole game of owning a business. Think of this like writing your online dating profile and describing your ideal partner. You’re not going to write “Open to whatever, good vibes only” and leave it at that. Having low or minimal standards guarantees that you’re gonna have a bad time. Or a GREAT time, but not for a long time (wink). So dig deeper. Start by writing down answers to the following questions:
			
				
- 
					

What is the one outcome that would make you consider this a win?
				
				
- 
					

What impact would achieving that result have on your life and your business?
				
				
- 
					

What might get in your way? How will you overcome that?
				
				
- 
					

What geographic region do you want the business to be in?
				
				
- 
					

Which sectors are you most comfortable in? In what sector does your innate ability and experience give you an unfair advantage (i.e., your Zone of Genius)?
				
				
- 
					

Where can you add the most value to the business?
				
				
- 
					

What would you need to learn to make this leap?
				
				
- 
					

How big will the business need to be? (Revenue and profit—aka show me the money)
				
				
- 
					

Based on your goals, knowledge, and skill set, which businesses appeal most to you?
				
				
- 
					

Are you after one business or many?
				
				
- 
					

How much do you want to work in the business?
				
			
			

Knowing the type of experience you want will help you start to notice the right business opportunities for you, the future owner. Do yourself a solid—pause here, write these down. Next, you’ll get clear on the numbers.
			

## Step 3: Define Your Deal Box

			

The final step is to create your Deal Box. This is where you’ll really drill down into the specific criteria you need, so you can tell people exactly what you are looking to buy.
			

Think of this part like buying a house. When you open up the Zillow app, you’re greeted with every single option in your market. Thousands and thousands of homes for sale. It’s impossible to sort through, leading to what I lovingly call “mental masturbation,” until you turn on the filters:
			
				
- 
					

Three bed, three bath
				
				
- 
					

Fifteen minutes from downtown
				
				
- 
					

Built after 2015
				
				
- 
					

Pool in the backyard
				
				
- 
					

Chicken coop (just me?)
				
			
			

Those filters slim down the thousands of homes for sale in your area to a few dozen options. Jackpot. It is the same for businesses. You have to define your filters, and go narrow to win.
			
				
			
			

With your Deal Box, you’ll establish the specific criteria that make sense for you. Can I just tell you…I wasted countless hours without this thing. I looked at every Tom, Dick, and Harry business that came across my desk. Steal my ten thousand hours, I beg you. Here are the keys to the castle:
			
				
- 
					

Valuation
				
				
- 
					

Asking price
				
				
- 
					

Revenue range
				
				
- 
					

Profit range
				
				
- 
					

Margin
				
				
- 
					

The sector of business you want
				
				
- 
					

The type of seller you’re looking for
				
				
- 
					

Geographic region
				
				
- 
					

Scale (Will it be one business or many?)
				
				
- 
					

Owner or operator (Do you want to be full time, part time, or little time?)
				
				
- 
					

Multiple of profit you are going to buy your business at
				
				
- 
					

Money down you’re comfortable with
				
			
			

BIG NOTE: This deal criteria is great for a stand-alone business. Later we will talk about additional considerations for your deal thesis if you want to grow through acquisitions and you’re buying for synergies. We’ll go over platforms, add-ons, and the like, as there are additional steps in deal clarity when you’re integrating businesses for synergies. I remember when I owned a business and first learned how important it is to smoothly integrate new acquisitions and make sure everything works together, I wondered, Why didn’t anyone else mention this to me? Don’t worry, current business owners, I got you.
			

Steal my homework. You can use our Deal Clarity Worksheet and Deal Box:
			
				
					

Deal Clarity Worksheet
				
				
					
					
				
			
			
				
					
					
					
					
					
					
				
				
					
						
							

Deal Box: Lock in Your Wants
						
					
					
						
							

Purchase Price and Cash-Flow Requirements
						
						
							

Description
						
						
							

My Deal Box
						
						
							

Business A
						
						
							

Business B
						
						
							

Business C
						
					
				
				
					
						
							

Ideal Annual Income
						
						
							

This is how much cash (your income) you’d like to make from the deal.
						
						
							

$250,000
						
						
						
						
					
					
						
							

Minimum Annual Income
						
						
							

This is the minimum cash flow (your income) you’d have to make to move forward on a deal.
						
						
							

$120,000
						
						
						
						
					
					
						
							

Operator Salary
						
						
							

This is what you’d have to pay the operator, if you choose to have one.
						
						
							

$100,000
						
						
						
						
					
					
						
							

Target Business Cash Flow
						
						
							

This is the minimum business cash flow you’d need in order to meet your minimum annual cash flow + the cash flow required to pay your operator’s salary.
						
						
							

$220,000
						
						
						
						
					
					
						
							

Debt Service Cost
						
						
							

If you are going to use financing, this is the potential cost of your debt service.
						
						
							

$30,000
						
						
						
						
					
					
						
							

Total Cash Flow Needed
						
						
							

This is the cash flow you must have from the business in order to meet your income goals, pay your operator’s salary, and pay for any debt service.
						
						
							

$250,000
						
						
						
						
					
					
						
							

Minimum Purchase Price
						
						
							

This is the minimum price you’d likely pay for your business using a 2X multiple. Do not obsess over this number; this is negotiable and cash flow is most important.
						
						
							

$500,000
						
						
						
						
					
					
						
							

Maximum Purchase Price
						
						
							

This is the maximum price you’d likely pay for your business using a 4X multiple. Again, do not fixate on this number; cash flow is most important.
						
						
							

$1,000,000
						
						
						
						
					
				
			
			

Here’s the BEAUTIFUL part. Now when people ask me what kind of business I’m looking to buy, I don’t waste my time or theirs. I can tell them exactly what’s in my Deal Box:
			
				
- 
					

Revenue of $1 million to $20 million. (Anything over $20 million puts me in competition with private equity firms. No thanks.)
				
				
- 
					

Profit margin of 30 to 50 percent and a history of annual profits. (I’m not looking for a fixer-upper.)
				
				
- 
					

A simple business using a straightforward business model. I prefer digital businesses, professional or home service–based businesses, and real estate enhanced businesses that I can scale.
				
				
- 
					

I’m looking for a motivated seller—not someone solely interested in cashing out at the highest price possible.
				
				
- 
					

I want to be the owner, not the operator. I’ll either hire an operator or hire the seller to run the business. (Sidenote: I never do a deal if I don’t have the operator first anymore.)
				
				
- 
					

I need it to make AT LEAST $300,000 to $350,000 in profit because I want to take home a salary of $120,000, I want to pay my operator $100,000, and I want to have a financial cushion.
				
			
			

Now, my Deal Box is relatively wide. If you’re just getting started, keep it narrow. For instance: You have experience in the cleaning industry, so you’re looking for a cleaning business in your hometown of Austin, Texas. Keep it stupid simple. You want simple “boring” businesses that are easy to understand, with a documented history of profitability. You don’t want to acquire a human genome mapping company or an artificial intelligence lab. More on this later, aka how to not royally F up your first acquisition. Because that sucks.
			

Armed with these three categories—your Zone of Genius, Ideal Owner Experience, and Deal Box—you have successfully defined what you want. Congratulations, you are no longer a walking question mark. You can now start my favorite part of the process.
			

## Brainstorming Your Boring Businesses

			

This is the fun part. It’s time to brainstorm all the potential businesses you might want to buy. This is where you let your imagination roam free—list every boring business that ignites a spark of interest and could be a potential fit with your Zone of Genius, Ideal Owner Experience, and Deal Box.
			

Jot them all down, no matter how far-fetched some may seem. Scribble down anything that comes to mind. A niche bookstore? A bubbling car wash? An online store? Write them all down. No judgments, just write, until you have at least ten options in front of you.
			

In your search for the right business, it’s common to sift through a TON of options. My recommendation is the 100-50-10 to 1 rule. Look at 100 businesses at a high level for every one you buy; analyze 50 at a secondary level (request financials, do a nondisclosure agreement [NDA], look under the hood); do deep due diligence on 10 (send a letter of intent [LOI], review the private placement memorandum [PPM], do a formal due diligence process). Did you marry the first girl you met? Probs not. You went on dozens or hundreds of dates before you put a ring on one. Same for mergers and acquisitions (M&A).
			

In our community of a thousand-plus business buyers, the average buyer reviews thirty-plus deals and starts with a Deal Box that includes twenty-plus companies. You might need to explore as few as ten businesses or upward of a hundred before you find the one that’s right for you.
			

Don’t view this as a daunting task but rather as part of an exciting journey. Or as Warren Buffett said, “No matter how great the talent or efforts, some things just take time. You can’t produce a baby in one month by getting nine women pregnant.”
			

It’s the essential step to avoid careening toward a business divorce. Because the best way to determine if a business is a good deal or a bad deal is by comparing it to other deals. A very rich mentor of mine at Goldman Sachs said, “Nothing is ever good or bad except by comparison.” He was right.
			
				
					

100-50-10 to 1 Rule
				
				
					
				
			
			

Once you have your list of potential businesses, it’s time for your crash course in dealmaking. That brings us to the next step, where you’re going to do what most won’t in the age of the internet: shake hands and meet people in real life. Shocking, I know.
			

## Motivated Sellers: How to Find Businesses for Sale

			

## Your Quest to Find a Motivated Seller, aka Find Your “Carl”

			

Carl put the key in the lock, flipped on the lights, opened the doors, and put out his Open sign. He’d done it just like this for the last twenty-five years. It was another Tuesday. But today, the key wouldn’t turn as fast, the sign felt a little heavier, and the door harder to open.
			

Carl had run this coffee shop in his small town for his whole adult life. It had put his kids through college. It had sent them on vacations. It had even bought them two houses. He knew the names of his customers and usually got their “I’ll have my regular” right.
			

But thousands and thousands of coffees later, the smell wasn’t quite as sweet. So many years ago, when he’d put up the Roaster & Sons sign, he believed his kids would carry on the mantle. But they’d replaced their aprons with suits and ties. Of course, Carl was proud of them, but there was one rather large problem. Despite the money he made each year and the profits he stacked, if he had to look at one more F-ing cup of coffee, he might have to take a baseball bat to it.
			

This, my friends, is often why owners are ready to sell their businesses. You need to find your Carl.
			

## The Secret Seller Phenomenon
