I stood in a conference hall in Austin, Texas. It was my third presentation that month to business owners. Whenever I talk about buying businesses, the usual trumpeted response is something along the lines of “What kind of idiot sells their profitable businesses at zero dollars down?” And if the audience is online, it’s a bit less subtle: “YOU’RE A LIAR.” Fun times.
			

Then, because the internet is a real garden party, they like to call me names and tell me my deals are fake. Sigh. I wish people would listen more carefully to how buying small businesses helps both sides of the transaction. But as my father likes to say, “You can lead a horse to water, but you can’t make it drink.” Or another personal favorite, “Never wrestle with a pig because you both get dirty…but the pig likes it.” Save that one for later.
			

So, before the disbelievers sounded off, I decided to use a little parlor trick. I asked the audience a few questions:
			

How many business owners in this room are over forty?
			

About a hundred people raised their hands. Half of the audience.
			

Okay, how many of you are actively trying to sell your companies?
			

All the hands go down, except three or four raised tentatively.
			

Now…how many of you would sell if the right offer came along?
			

More than one hundred hands shot right back up again. Roughly 60 percent of the room. Here’s the secret: I have done this in hundreds of rooms, in hundreds of cities across this beautiful country of ours. The reaction is the same. Every. Damn. Time.
			

I call this the Secret Seller Phenomenon. At any given time, more than 60 percent of all business owners would consider selling for the right price, the right terms, to the right person.
			

Thank God for the trolls. They leave more deals for us.
			
				
					

Secret Seller Phenomenon
				
				
					
				
			
			

Somewhere out there right now in your city—in the industry you want and in the size of business you need—there is an owner waiting for you. There’s a Goldilocks soup that is just your temperature. I can pretty much guarantee it.
			

Imagine for a moment that you spent decades building a business. You’ve missed your kids’ soccer practices to close deals. You’ve served and employed hundreds of people. Then you turn sixty-five and you’re told it’s time to play bingo and canasta. You go from an “owner” to a “retiree” in a flash. Emotionally, that can be tough. We humans prosper on purpose. Losing it can be a hard pill to swallow.
			

Then again, if you’ve ever run a business, holy hell do you know the misery in it. Payroll is due every Friday. Employees quit on Christmas Eve. Vendors steal from you. Entrepreneurship can feel like a game best played by masochists, drunks, and the deranged. Including yours truly.
			

Many boomer business owners keep going because they haven’t saved enough for a comfortable retirement. Forty-five percent of boomers have no retirement savings at all, and even those who do find they haven’t saved enough. Getting old is surprisingly expensive.
			

That’s why sellers, in fact, are all around. They just need to meet the right person—someone who will respect the business and its customers, take care of its employees, and maintain the reputation the owner has established. And, ya know, pay them the right price.
			

Baby boomer business owners are ready for their next adventure. They’re ready to retire and sip mai tais on a $249 cruise ship special. Most are motivated to sell for that reason alone, but one in five is simply burned out. One in ten is sick. Most have no successors and no transition plan. They plan to just keep working till, well, they can’t.
			

We call these the Seven Ds of why sellers sell:
			
				
- 
					

Death
				
				
- 
					

Divorce
				
				
- 
					

Disease
				
				
- 
					

Distress
				
				
- 
					

Dullness
				
				
- 
					

Departure
				
				
- 
					

Disagreement
				
			
			

These are your windows to cash flow on Day 1. After all, when you are sick, tired, and ready to retire, an offer to buy your business sounds pretty sweet.
			

How do you spot these hidden sellers, who quietly dream of offloading their business to someone like you? As a matter of fact, we have a checklist right here for you to follow your way to Carl.
			
				
					
					
					
				
				
					
						
							

The Seller Financing Avatar Checklist
						
					
					
						
							

No.
						
						
							

Feature
						
						
							

✓
						
					
				
				
					
						
							

1
						
						
							

10+ years since the business was founded
						
						
					
					
						
							

2
						
						
							

Under $1 million in profit
						
						
					
					
						
							

3
						
						
							

Little competition—no one else is competing to buy the biz
						
						
					
					
						
							

4
						
						
							

Brick and mortar (not online)—or highly commoditized online
						
						
					
					
						
							

5
						
						
							

5+ years since the owner’s been in charge
						
						
					
					
						
							

6
						
						
							

Lack of successor
						
						
					
					
						
							

7
						
						
							

Owner is near retirement age
						
						
					
					
						
							

8
						
						
							

Business isn’t applicable for bank loans
						
						
					
					
						
							

9
						
						
							

Priced too high for its balance sheet
						
						
					
					
						
							

10
						
						
							

Business without clean financials
						
						
					
					
						
							

11
						
						
							

Business without set leadership team
						
						
					
					
						
							

12
						
						
							

Distressed business
						
						
					
					
						
							

13
						
						
							

Asset sale
						
						
					
					
						
							

14
						
						
							

Established relationship with seller
						
						
					
					
						
							

15
						
						
							

Owner wants the cash flow versus lump sum OR wants larger exit number over longer time frame
						
						
					
					
						
							

16
						
						
							

Owner has other sources of income outside of the business
						
						
					
				
			
			

Which leads to another common question I get:
			

How do I find these mythical owners who might be open to selling?
			

My answer: the cash goes to the door knockers, not the mouse clickers.
			

## “But Can’t I Do This on the Internet?”

			

Buying a business is not like buying a house, where you spend most of your time perusing properties on your laptop. While 90 percent of homes are sold through online advertising, only about 20 percent of businesses are. That means despite the growth and popularity of business-selling websites—like bizbuysell.com, loopnet.com, flippa.com, and acquire.com—they are not always the best places to find a good business to buy.[*4]
			

Most business owners are, let’s be honest, old. My dad sure as hell is not on Slack or inputting his valuation and uploading his financials onto an online platform. Also, they don’t want their employees to know they want to sell. They don’t want their customers to know. They don’t want to advertise it online because they don’t have time to field hundreds of calls from lookie-loos. They are too busy working. In fact, the idea of selling may not ever have occurred to them until you bring it up. This will change in the future as businesses come online, but for now…we go off market.
			

That means knocking on doors and flapping your lips. Asking around, consulting lawyers, accountants, real estate agents, chambers of commerce, and small-business incubators, and by popping into the companies themselves to shake hands and kiss babies.
			

What do you need to know before you go door-knocking? Keep in mind a few things:
			
				
- 
					

78 percent of small-business owners are male.
				
				
- 
					

55 percent are generational entrepreneurs; they grew up in small businesses. Sometimes they inherited the business.
				
				
- 
					

58 percent have owned a previous business.
				
			
			

What this tells us is that there is often an emotional component to the sale. The business may have been in one family for a generation or more. The owners may not be as interested in money as they are in their legacy, their family name. Again, they want the business to go to the right person—someone who will respect and take care of their employees and customers and maintain the quality the business is known for. Developing a relationship with them and convincing them you will do those things is crucial. That’s very hard to do through email.
			

Also—don’t be a millennial-splainer. Don’t tell them all the reasons you will run their business better than them. Don’t come in hot with the “facts” on what their business is worth. Don’t talk about the new tech and your superiority due to your financial wizardry. Your mantra is going to be shut up and listen. We’ll get into more detail on that later, but the difference in how you start the convo versus how every private equity company does will mean millions of dollars for you, not them.
			

## Jake and the Walking Billboard Strategy

			

“Codie, are you ready to tell me how much you love me?” Jake, one of my operators shouted excitedly at me into the phone.
			

“Jake, it’s six a.m. in Texas. You had better have a good reason for calling me,” I say back, because pre-coffee Codie is not pleasant.
			

“I have the perfect company for you to buy!” Again with the shouting. I’m annoyed just reminiscing about it. But to his credit, Jake had just found us a business to buy where he knew the seller (less risk); the seller was ready to retire (motivated); this was just one of his many businesses (open); he was willing to stay on, sell the business to us for 2.5X profits (average online biz trades at 5–10X), and defer 30 percent of the purchase price in seller financing[*5] (a Carl); AND it was an online business we already used as a vendor (my Deal Box). Jake is already one of the most excitable, gregarious humans I know. But then you give him a deal and he might as well be a kid in a Toys“R”Us with someone else’s Amex. He’s a vibe.
			

He was also right. We ended up buying a small online business for a cheap valuation, deferring some of the payment and integrating it into our other online businesses. The kicker? It all started with a handshake. Because that is the secret to deals; they are all around, and they look just as ordinary and unassuming as your dad. The seller of the company got introduced to Jake by his son, they had gone to school together. But turns out the son has no interest in taking over after dear old Dad. So we get to win, and his dad gets a nice, long, paid-for vacation to Southeast Asia.
			

What was the key? Jake uses a method I taught him that is so painfully obvious, yet never utilized, as to be comical. It’s what I call the Walking Billboard Strategy.
			
				
- 
					

Every time you meet someone, tell them you buy businesses.
				
				
- 
					

Every time you walk into a small business, compliment them on the biz and ask if they own the place.
				
			
			

Telling them you buy goes like this:
			
				

Hey, I’m Codie. I buy small businesses from owners in Austin that do less than $10 million in revenue and are in professional services—think accounting, marketing, et cetera—but are ready for an exit and some cash flow.
			
			

Talking to owners goes like this:
			
				

Hey, I love this place! Do you own it? Cool!! I love meeting owners because I actually buy small businesses like this…if you’ve ever thought about selling at the right terms, obviously. Love to stay in touch.
			
			

There are only two inputs: (1) the number of owners you meet, and (2) the number of people who know you buy businesses. Increase either of those (ideally both) and you will eventually have so many potential deals it’ll startle you. People overcomplicate growing their deal database. If you remember nothing else from this book, remember the Walking Billboard Strategy and you’ll go far.
			

But let’s say you want to go even deeper…
			

## Seven Ways to Find Motivated Sellers

			

I’m going to give you my top seven ways to find motivated sellers who want to sell even if they don’t know it yet. But before you jump in, I must warn you, please don’t do these all at once. Pick one that will work for you or in the area you live. Don’t bounce from strategy to strategy looking for the next shiny thing. Focus, people!
			
				
- 
					

Ask The 4A Club. That’s your accountant, attorney, agent (real estate), and adviser (financial). Call or email them and ask if they know anyone selling a business that’s in your wheelhouse. Then ask your friends for the contact info for their accountants, attorneys, agents, and advisers. Rinse and repeat each month with check-in emails. “Hey, Alan the accountant, just wanted to ping you because I am still buying businesses :) If anyone comes to mind I’d love to pay you a sourcing fee for any deals you send my way. Thanks!”
				
				
- 
					

Ask business owners you know. Fellow business owners are a great source for potential deals. Start with warm leads (people who are your friends) and end with cold leads (local owners you’ve purchased from before). Use the biz-buying script above.
				
				
- 
					

Find intermediaries. Intermediaries like bankers, business brokers, and mergers-and-acquisition advisers are greater sources for leads. My Contrarian Community member, Ryan, recently wrote on Facebook: “I’m looking for a business broker. Who have you worked with that is a 10/10?” Some of Ryan’s friends replied that they didn’t know what a business broker was, but three times as many had names and contact info of not just business brokers but SELLERS.
				
				
- 
					

Join your local Chamber of Commerce (uschamber.com/co/chambers). These people are literally steeped in local boring businesses. It’s all they talk about. Spoiler: the meetings may make you want to gouge your eyes out…. These ain’t ragers. But they are full of your target avatar nibbling shrimp cocktail and drinking house wine. Get to know these folks and tell them you’re looking into buying businesses.
				
				
- 
					

Visit Small-Business Development Centers (sba.gov). These are cobweb-filled gold mines of free resources, and they are all over the place. Oregon, for instance, has twenty regional centers and twenty-six satellite offices throughout the state. SBDCs offer training, market research, and planning. Their Capital Access team will help you find financing, and their succession-planning advisers provide confidential help to owners thinking about retiring.
				
				
- 
					

Scan your service providers. Pull together all your credit card and bank statements and transactions from the past couple of years into a spreadsheet. Delete the transactions from the big corporations like Amazon, Target, Costco, Home Depot, Walmart, and so forth. Focus on the small companies you paid recently. Maybe there is a landscaper you’ve used a few times, a plumber, a building contractor, or an accountant. Do any of these companies have a seller type that fits your Deal Box? Hint: you likely are already working with someone who is exactly the person you want to buy out. More on this in the Venmo Challenge.
				
				
- 
					

Follow up every forty-five days. Ask for permission to keep them updated, and then put a reminder in your calendar for a quick update every forty-five days. That’s enough to stay top of mind without becoming a pest. For instance, a member of our community buys insurance companies. He once made a multimillion-dollar deal with an agent he’d first contacted eight years before. They’d kept in touch because my friend used a customer relationship management system and committed to checking in with his contacts every two or three months. He’s been calling some of them for years. Being proactive this way is how you create deal flow. Over time, you can weed out the individuals who aren’t helping and get it down to a smaller group of five to ten people who consistently give you some actionable intel. Only rule? Don’t be a taker. Buy ’em a coffee, send ’em a cool relevant article, go wild and text ’em a meme.
				
			
			

Visualize this: Your whole life you’ve been going to a weight-lifting gym. The only other people in there with you are muscle-bound dudes and chicks who could beat you up. So you think that it’s really hard to find feminine single women who are into fitness. Then I take you to my hot yoga class. The room is full of beautiful, fit women and you are the only dude in sight. Perspective.
			

There are far more motivated sellers than meet the eye online. Yes, they want to be freed from the obligation of running their business, but it’s usually much more than that. As the buyer, you are carrying on their legacy. You’re helping employees keep their jobs. You’re continuing their relationships with the customers. Most important, you’re giving the owner a stream of income to help them ease into retirement. You just have to find your proverbial yoga class.
			

Once you have a list of potential targets, you’ll hit the pavement and investigate further to see if they are YOUR Carl. Ideally, you’ll physically go into their businesses and chat with them when they’re not busy. If you can’t do that, give them a call or shoot them an email to see if they’re open to the discussion. The bottom line is this: you will have to put in some face time and build real relationships with owners in order for this to work.
			

Below I’ll give you some key questions to ask, but take it easy. This is not the Spanish Inquisition. You have to warm up to them, and they’ll need to warm up to you. As the conversation unfolds, gently weave in some of these questions:
			
				
- 
					

How did you get started in the business?
				
				
- 
					

What inspired you to choose this line of work?
				
				
- 
					

What were you doing before this?
				
				
- 
					

What do you love about being in this industry?
				
				
- 
					

What’s your favorite part of running this business?
				
				
- 
					

If you had it all to do over again, what would you do differently?
				
				
- 
					

What’s the toughest part of being in the business?
				
				
- 
					

What is the most important thing for your customers to know about you?
				
				
- 
					

What’s a typical day like?
				
				
- 
					

Have you considered selling the business? How come?
				
				
- 
					

What are you hoping to do next?
				
				
- 
					

What is most important to you—Your legacy? Your employees? Your customers? Your sales price? Your reputation?
				
			
			

Keep in mind that the seller may be just as interested in your motivations and capabilities. So, be clear about them going in (that’s why you did those exercises earlier). If the owner asks why you’re interested in buying their pride and joy, have a good answer ready.
			

Be honest, of course, and while there’s no need for flattery, it’s not going to hurt your position to say you admire what they’ve built. The most underrated part of getting to know owners is actually getting them to like you. Who do we like? People like us. People who like the same things we like. Owners want to sell to someone who actually likes the thing they do for a living. Common sense, but not common practice.
			

Learning the owner’s true motivations may take some time. Their motivations are often nuanced, and it’s unlikely the seller will spell them all out for you. You may have to tease them out, and that will require some subtlety, emotional intelligence, and time. You may need several conversations getting to know the seller and building trust first. We’ll dig deeper into that in the negotiations segment.
			

Knowing your skills, passion, and level of expertise is valuable to you, but it’s crucial to selling your acquisition bid to the seller. Remember, these are small businesses, the product of someone’s blood, sweat, and tears, and most sellers want to know their offspring is going to a good family. When you’re a great fit to take over a company, the seller is more willing to negotiate or give you equity for your skills.
			

The best predictor of future behavior is past behavior. The seller is going to be interested in your work history. Tell them how you’ve won, examples of how you’ve leveraged your strengths, and times when you failed but learned valuable lessons that paid off later. This isn’t a job interview (so please do not bring a PowerPoint), but, in a non-braggy way, mention relevant accomplishments that prove you have an upward trajectory. Forget about ticking off all the duties you performed. Nobody cares. How did you win?
			

## The Venmo Challenge: Become an Owner in 30 Minutes

			

I know what you’re thinking:
			

This process is going to take forever! Is there any way to speed this up?
			

So glad you asked. In this section, I’m going to show you my method for quickly securing partial ownership of a cash-flowing Level 1 or Level 2 business—for zero dollars—with a few conversations. I know, it sounds ridiculous. Except it works. It’s called the Venmo Challenge. Here’s how to steal my homework.
			

Step 1: Open up your Venmo account. You’re going to look at where you are consistently spending cash with small businesses and service providers. For me, I quickly see that I’m regularly paying money to my:
			
				
- 
					

Cleaning lady
				
				
- 
					

Property manager
				
				
- 
					

Handyman
				
			
			

Before you proceed, check in with yourself and your desire to own a portion of their businesses. Do you actually want to help your cleaning lady grow her business? If yes, proceed.
			

Step 2: Tally up your monthly expenses for their services. For me:
			
				
- 
					

Cleaning lady: $2,000 a month (’cause I’m a dirty girl)
				
				
- 
					

Property manager: $6,500 a month
				
				
- 
					

Handyman: $500 a month
				
			
			

Step 3: Estimate their annual revenue. After doing some research, I get a feel for how many customers their businesses are likely serving throughout the year and come up with my estimates for their revenue:
			
				
- 
					

Cleaning lady: $90,000 per year
				
				
- 
					

Property manager: $350,000 per year
				
				
- 
					

Handyman’s company: $800,000 per year
				
			
			

Step 4: List ways you can add value to their businesses. At this point, you might still be wondering, “Why would a small-time business owner give me a cut of their business? Won’t they just want to keep all that money to themselves?” This is where you need to make sure you understand their desires. Desires usually fall into three buckets:
			
				
- 
					

They want to make more money.
				
				
- 
					

They want to do less stuff.
				
				
- 
					

They want to do the things they do smarter.
				
			
			

If you can help them do any of those things, you can provide real value. Now, remember your Zone of Genius? Well, mine is marketing. I notice my cleaning lady doesn’t have a website, a logo, or even a name for her company. She doesn’t market her services online at all—zero social media. Then I see the property manager and handyman have some of these problems too. It’s clear I can add value by helping them market their businesses.
			

Step 5: Have a conversation with them about their business. Now I’m going to give you an example of how you can speak with an owner about their business. Let’s start with my cleaning lady, who I’ll call Jane.
			
				

CODIE: Jane, we’ve never really talked about your cleaning business before. And I was curious, do you clean a lot of other people’s houses?
				

JANE: Yes, I do.
				

CODIE: Of course you do, cool. And do you have any other employees?
				

JANE: Nope, it’s just me.
				

CODIE: Very cool. The reason I ask is because I sometimes invest in small businesses and help them grow. And what that usually looks like is helping them with marketing so they can bring in more clients and make more revenue. I was looking online, and I couldn’t find your website or social media.
				

JANE: I don’t have those things. I just get referrals.
				

CODIE: Okay, cool. Are you looking to grow your business at all, or not so much?
				

JANE: Hmm, I’ve thought about it. But right now, I’m pretty much at capacity.
				

CODIE: Well, what I do is help business owners like you so they can grow their business. We make sure they can handle more business first, of course. But in exchange, I take a distribution—a part of the total sales they do each month. You do great work, I love your services, and if you want to keep growing, I’d love to help you. Would you be interested in doing that together?
				

JANE: Hmm…I think so, yes. That could work.
				

CODIE: I know this is kind of a personal question, but how much money do you make in this business each year? What’s the total annual revenue? (Note: I am always shocked how many people tell me this!)
				

JANE: Well, I made about $90,000 last year.
				

CODIE: Wow, congratulations! All right, let’s write down some numbers and see how many clients you have right now, and what your capacity is to take on more clients. And if it’s something you want, we can talk about how it might look if I help you grow your business.
			
			

Step 6: Do the math for how much you can grow their business. Jane charges an average of $300 per cleaning, and she does about three hundred cleanings per year.
			
				

CODIE: If we can increase your number of cleanings to five hundred per year, and you raise your prices to $400 per cleaning, you’ll be making $200,000. Does that sound doable to you?
				

JANE: Hmm. It could be possible.
				

CODIE: Okay, let’s see how many new people I’d need to refer to get you there. Doing the math, it looks like I’d have to send you four more clients a week. If I come up with a process to send you four more paying clients a week, would that work for you?
				

JANE: Well…I suppose I could hire someone to help me. Yes, I could make it work.
				

CODIE: Perfect. So, why don’t we do this: You keep all the revenue on your first $90,000 that you’re already going to make this year. That’s all yours, I get none of it. And then any money that you make over $90,000, I’ll get 25 percent of that. So what that means is, if I help you grow this year’s revenue to $190,000—that’s an additional $100,000 that you’re not making right now—I keep 25 percent of that. So you get an additional $75,000 and I keep $25,000 for the year, or roughly $2,083 per month. Again, this is only from the additional revenue you’re making from the clients I bring you—the ones you wouldn’t have gotten otherwise. Does that sound fair to you?
				

JANE: That sounds fair. How will we do it?
				

CODIE: Well, I’d set up your marketing systems first, at no cost to you. Then, once they start bringing in more clients, you’d pay me once a month. The only thing I’d ask is to be able to look at your payments. I could look at your Venmo, or better yet, I’ll come up with a payment system to automate everything so you never have to chase people’s money down again. Sound good to you?
				

JANE: Let’s do it.
			
			

Did reading that exchange make you feel uncomfortable? Think I’m taking advantage of my cleaning lady? Time to examine your beliefs around money. This is what’s known as a sweat equity deal, or a distributing equity deal. Consultants do it all the time. It’s a win-win for Jane because I’m building her marketing systems, bringing her clients, and giving her a raise of $75,000 per year. She’s not going to make that money without my help. And it’s a win-win for me because I’m not spending any money up front to get a 25 percent cut of her ongoing sales.
			

I’ve personally used this method to get paid between $8,000 to $15,000 per month from a podcast editing company. In that case, the owner of the business hated sales and finding new clients, who were each worth $3,000 per month. I referred a ton of my friends and followers to his business, and in turn he sent me a monthly check. These days, we do this with businesses that make millions. It’s all the same game, just different levels.
			

That’s the Venmo Challenge. You don’t have to put down a bunch of cash, and you don’t put yourself at risk of going bankrupt. Just a nice exchange of profits for value. Now go forth and have those conversations with your favorite service providers. Who knows. It could earn you a nice monthly paycheck for the rest of the year.
			
				
					
					
					
					
					
				
				
					
						
							

Step 1
						
						
							

Step 2
						
						
							

Step 3
						
						
							

Step 4
						
						
							

Step 5
						
					
					
						
							

Venmo Spend
							

Pull up your Venmo history for the last two months.
						
						
							

List All
							

List all the people you pay, how much, and what they do.
						
						
							

Desire to Own
							

Ask yourself whether you’d want to own part of their business.
						
						
							

Owner Convo
							

Have your first convo with them: Are they interested in growth, increasing profits?
						
						
							

Offer
							

Interpret the results and write your conclusion.
						
					
				
			
			

## Evaluation Essentials: Assessing Your Boring Business

			

## Miss Nevada’s Mailbox Master Class

			

Lisa Song Sutton has a whole vibe. She’s a five-foot-two bundle of pure Korean immigrant hustle who refuses to wear anything but red-bottom heels. She’s a friend who taught me more than a few things about finding reliable, replicable, cash-flowing small businesses. In the town of glitter, gold, and gyrating neon, Lisa found cash flow in a corner of Sin City that few gamblers ever experience: the mail.
			

I met Lisa at a small-business conference. Let’s set the scene: I showed up in my usual work boots, jeans, suit jacket coat, and attitude. Lisa did not get the same assignment. She showed up in a Hervé Léger (read: skintight) dress and six-inch stilettos clacking with each stride. Very Vegas, I thought. She looks like Miss Nevada. Shocker, later I learned she was.
			

As a recently reformed private equity finance try-hard, I doubted there was much I would learn from my fellow presenter. Turns out, I am a judgmental idiot.
			

Dolly Parton has a line I’ve come to love: “You know, I look like a woman but I think like a man. And in this world of business, that has helped me a lot. Because by the time they think that I don’t know what’s goin’ on, I then got the money, and gone.” Dolly, you minx.
			

Turns out that Lisa’s got a little Dolly in her. She worked as a real estate attorney and owned a slew of businesses. She’s got a real estate franchise, a bunch of multifamily and single-family homes, Sin City Cupcakes (alcohol plus cupcakes), and Liquid & Lace, a swimsuit line. But my favorite business of hers has none of her glitz on it.
			

Here’s what happened: in 2021, she was looking to invest in something that cash-flowed in any market. Think about it. When the economy goes down, I’d hazard a gamble that Don Julio 1942 Margarita cupcakes—one of Lisa’s Sin City flavors—take a back seat. Same goes for Lisa’s other ventures, real estate and swimwear.
			

The question was: What will people buy or use no matter what the market is doing? She started circling on self-storage. It was the top-performing market sector of all U.S. real estate for many years, after all. Yet Lisa thought the start-up costs were too high and that the market had become saturated. Still, the idea of people renting space, month after month, clung to her.
			

What if they weren’t big walk-in storage units? What if they were smaller? Like a lot smaller. What if they were…postal boxes? Aren’t those just mini storage units? Then she remembered that she had a postal box. She rarely used it, but they charged her card every month like clockwork. Hmm. Twenty bucks a month for a six-by-six-by-six-inch cave. If you had several hundred of those…
			

She called her friend Greg in San Diego, who had three UPS pack-and-ship franchises.
			

“If you could do anything different to make your store more profitable, what would it be?” she asked him.
			

“Add more mailboxes,” he said without hesitation.
			

Mailboxes, he explained, are your steady, recurring revenue. Shipping is ancillary and seasonal, but mailboxes are year-round. Unfortunately, UPS limits the number of mailboxes you can have to a few hundred. If he were to start over, Lisa’s friend said, he’d go independent and line every square inch he could with boxes. That’s the gold.
			

Lisa did some more research. What she learned:
			
				
- 
					

Postal business is increasing 5.3 percent a year.
				
				
- 
					

Demand for locations is tied to new businesses, all of which are up in Vegas.
				
				
- 
					

Competitors in the area had sold out their mailboxes and were running long waiting lists.
				
			
			

Thesis validated. Now this is where it gets interesting.
			

She arranged to meet with the owner of the shipping center she’d been using for years. As they were talking about his business, he dropped a bombshell on her: The store was doing $1 million per year in revenue, with just 1,200 square feet. Damn.
			

Then, Lisa made him a brilliant pitch:
			

“Let me shadow you for two weeks, document your entire operation, and then deliver to you a complete set of SOPs [standard operating procedures].[*6] I’ll also give you a check for $10,000, and a promise not to compete in your service area.”
			

He glanced down at those lethal-looking heels, the sprayed-on leggings.
			

“Like a trainee, you mean?” he said skeptically.
			

“Exactly, like a trainee. I want to learn how to do everything.”
			

The owner chuckled. “All right, I guess you’ve got a deal.”
			

For the next two rather bizarre weeks, Lisa “Fireball” Song Sutton clacked around his store, opening up shop at dawn, trailing her two little walking hot dogs (ahem, corgis), and documenting every step in the pack-and-ship process. The hilarity of this visual aside, this was a crucial part of Lisa’s due diligence. She would be there first thing in the morning to open up, and be the last person there at night to close. She would pack and tape. She’d greet customers and fill out box rental agreements. She’d sort mail. She’d learn how to run all the equipment. And she took notes on everything. If after a couple of weeks behind the counter she hated the experience, she would know this business was not for her.
			

She loved it. And now she knew how to hire and run it.
			

After getting her ten thousand hours of experience in two weeks, Lisa left the owner with her SOPs and set out to find some locations. She searched the “gentrifying neighborhoods” that had no competition, then used her real estate experience to negotiate reasonable leases. She rented space in a strip mall in North Las Vegas, then another thirty miles south in Spring Valley. Her build-out costs for two locations were about $40,000 for counters, mailboxes, registers, signage, promotions, and packing materials.
			

Her hiring strategy was just as bizarrely perfect. In the north, she discovered the value of military spouses. Efficient. A built-in network of military friends shipping stuff all the time. Only interested in work and no time for foolishness. Down south, she had a different talent pool. Think Vegas pool party cocktail waitresses meet swimsuit models. These ladies made some serious stacks of cash working the Vegas pools, but had to deal with handsy drunk gents and 120-degree heat all day. At some point, many wanted to push the unsubscribe button. So they did, and instead of heels and thongs, they got to wear sneakers, get equity and ownership, and leave the butt squeezes in the past.
			

In her first year of running her mini-mailbox business, she was profitable. By year two, she was making $300,000 in revenue. By year three, she was doing $500,000.
			

I share Lisa’s story because she did everything right that we’ve covered so far.
			

First, she defined what she wanted. She knew what kind of Owner Experience she wanted (recession-proof, recurring revenue, no drama), as well as specific criteria in her Deal Box.
			

Then, she went out and talked to an owner in her network and got the information she needed to make an informed decision. She didn’t expect to get a listing sent to her email or find opportunities on a website. She just walked straight into a UPS franchise, asked questions, and came up with an even better business model. She even convinced a successful operator to train her in the business, reveal his secrets, and hand her the keys to the castle. She didn’t repeat the mistake I’ve made before by re-creating the wheel. She just bought a set of tires and hit the gas.
			

Oh, and Lisa’s Zone of Genius? She knew what it was and she used it. She was able to negotiate favorable rents using her real estate skills. She took the time to find the right operators—young women eager to work with someone rather than for someone—and incentivized them to help make the business a success. She’s not behind a counter licking stamps all day; she’s out in the community, looking for the next deal that might dovetail with what she’s started.
			

Most important, she did her due diligence, the process of evaluating the business before buying it. That’s what you’ll learn how to do in this chapter. And don’t worry, it’s not as difficult as it sounds. It is critical, though.
			

Lisa’s story shows what’s possible when you follow the steps. As I’ve said, this is a recipe, and it works.
			

## How I Rapidly Evaluate Boring Businesses

			

When you buy a business, you are essentially buying a spouse. We don’t just swipe right and get on one knee, now do we? No. We find “the one,” and then 50 percent of the time we kick ’em to the curb. Ah, modern society. Jokes aside, having been through a divorce, I can tell you how to avoid its business equivalent—a shutdown.
			

You have a better likelihood of lasting success in business than in the average marriage. So long as you don’t pick a nutjob of a business to settle down with. Thankfully, there is a 20 percent that drives 80 percent of every business. Your goal is to find that 20 percent. First, I like to think of it as not just buying a business but buying an income stream. Replace a laundromat for a car wash for an accounting firm. Most businesses are songs, just different genres. They need to all have one thing in them: the ability to pay a cash flow amount consistently, as cash flow (income) is the lifeblood of a business. Before I buy, I like to ensure that my business covers four things:
			
				
					

Income stream must cover four things:
				
				
					
				
			
			
				
- 
					

Debt service/financing: Can the business comfortably afford its debts and financing costs?
				
				
- 
					

Operator salary: Is there enough margin to pay someone to manage daily operations without my constant involvement?
				
				
- 
					

Growth capital: Are there funds left over to reinvest for future growth?
				
				
- 
					

My earnings/income: After all expenses, does the business generate sufficient profit to contribute to my personal income?
				
			
			

If the business covers those, I move on to determining the level of risk. What is the likelihood that, once I buy it, this business will go out, find more dollars, and bring them back to me? How reliably will it do this consistently, day after day, year after year? Three tests have helped keep me honest:
			

## Test 1: Is It a SOWS?

			

My framework for finding great boring businesses is called SOWS. Like cows, but without all the methane gas. SOWS stands for:
			
				
- 
					

Stale: As in, minimal innovation has been adopted.
				
				
- 
					

Old: The business has been around for a while.
				
				
- 
					

Weak: The competition is lazy and uninspired.
				
				
- 
					

Simple: You don’t need to be a rocket scientist to run the damn thing.
				
			
			

If the word SOWS conjures up an image of an adult female pig, that’s fine. Pigs are smart. They’re valuable. They live a long time. They got a lotta fat on ’em. They make bacon. So, yeah, both the acronym and the metaphor work for me.
			

Stale, you ask? What do I mean by that and why is that a good thing?
			

“Stale” means it’s a business that hasn’t kept up with the times. Its website is circa 2008. The owners don’t use social media. They have no testimonials or customer ratings. They not only have a fax machine; they actually use it and require their customers to use it. They may send you documents by email, but they expect you to print them out, sign them, and mail them back.
			

Why do I find these archaic practices so appealing? Because less innovation means more potential upside for me, the buyer, and thus less risk. If they send you an email address from aol.com, still use fax machines, or write paper receipts, you should be getting all hot and bothered. Marketing 101 is rarely done in small businesses. With tech and social media done right, you can turn a boring business that you buy for pennies into a $10 million company.
			

Old, too? Yeah. I am not looking for the sweet young thing. Nor am I looking for the big disrupter who’s exploded on the scene and is capturing all the market share. Those kinds of businesses attract a lot of attention, a lot of competitors. I want companies older than five years that have been plugging along just fine, thank you very much. They have faithful customers, repeat business, and social equity in the community. People use their location as a point of reference when giving directions. (“Take your first right after that pack-and-ship store at the corner of Liberty.”) Old businesses can be hidden gems. As you’ll recall from earlier in the book, they operate on the Lindy effect: the longer they’ve been around, the more likely they’ll continue to survive.
			

Weak? Really? The company you’re buying isn’t weak, but its competition is. Your target company is old and stale, but its competition is even worse. When was the last time you got a plumber:
			
				
- 
					

On time
				
				
- 
					

With automated billing
				
				
- 
					

Who sent follow-up texts
				
			
			

Never, right? Those are three easy fixes to make your new acquisition better than competitors (and more profitable for you).
			

Simple? Simple, as in you can explain the business model to an eight-year-old. (“People with dirty cars come here and drink a cup of coffee while we make their cars look new again.”) There is nothing proprietary here. No biotech. No R&D. No industrial secret that you can scale nationally. Instead, you’re looking for businesses that are in high demand and have few inputs.
			

Only you, my brilliant grasshopper, will see ways to bring modern magic into their business, with futuristic advancements like Billing software! Customer relations management software! Outsourced employees! and other little tricks that build speed and capacity, allowing you to charge more and handle more customers.
			

SOWS: Stale, Old, Weak, Simple. That is your winning boring business formula and how you see businesses that no one else even realizes are there.
			

## Test 2: Can It BRRT?

			

Once you’ve found a potential SOWS, then what? You put it through the second test: the BRRT Method.
			

BRRT is just as simple, but its purpose is a bit different. While SOWS proves to you that a business is indeed boring, the BRRT Method ensures it has upside potential.
			

BRRT stands for:
			
				
- 
					

Buy: You want to buy a business that cash-flows, not cash-sucks. Think monthly subscriptions, maintenance contracts, or membership fees. The revenue is recurring, predictable, and spread across a diverse client base. On the flip side, cash-suck businesses are “work first, pray for payment later.” Think special-event services, seasonal stores, art galleries, consulting firms, and tech start-ups. The only time I’ll buy a cash-suck business is if I know I can successfully convert it into a cash-flow model in the first ninety days. Otherwise, I stick to cash-flow businesses only.
				
				
- 
					

Resist: You want a business that’s in a recession-resistant sector and can actually grow during an economic downturn. I think about it like this: If the economy tanks but your toilet is overflowing, you still gonna fix it? Yup. That’s a resist business. If the economy tanks but your custom frame breaks, are you going to buy a new one? Or just go to Target for a cheaper one? Custom framing = not a resist business.
				
				
- 
					

Raise: You’ll need a business that can increase its prices. Which, by the way, nearly every small business can do. Most small businesses are underpriced by 30 to 300 percent in our experience, and only about one-third of owners raise their prices annually.
				
				
- 
					

Tech: Can you add technology to the business in a meaningful way? Goes back to that fax machine and notebooks-as-ledgers model.
				
			
			

Here’s how I used the BRRT Method with a deal I did recently. I have a media company, so I decided to use my unfair advantage and look for some media prospects to acquire. I scouted the internet and came upon an online education business that looked promising. To me, it seemed like the business was underperforming and neglected—a sure sign the owner was running out of gas and might be open to selling.
			
				
			
			

I pulled out my trusty rapid-fire BRRT machine and did this:
			
				
- 
					

Buy: Check for cash flow. I estimated the company’s revenue and profit by checking their site traffic on Semrush and Similarweb. I examined their product set, applied a 2 to 4 percent conversion rate, and estimated their revenue. I figured they had about $100,000 in revenue with $20,000 profit. I could have gotten all this from the owner, but this is Due Diligence for Dummies, my friend. I was still deciding if I wanted to move forward.
				
				
- 
					

Resist: Check for ability to withstand recessions. People don’t usually drop out of school or abandon learning during economic downturns. In fact, they usually do more in a downturn. So far, so good.
				
				
- 
					

Raise: Check for ability to raise prices. Many small businesses never raise prices and many forget to charge more for inflation. This acquisition was overdue for a price hike. The company was selling its content for twenty-nine dollars a month, which struck me as extremely low.
				
				
- 
					

Tech: Check for whether they are already tech savvy and optimized. No social media? Perfect. No chat automation software? Great.
				
			
			

Feeling good about my BRRT results, I sent an email to the owner: “Would you be open to chatting about a sale (at the right price, obviously)?” I indicated I typically buy businesses for 2 to 5X profit.
			

Twelve emails, two phone calls, and one Zoom meeting later, we closed the deal with the condition that the owner would stay on for three months to transition the operation to my team. After we completed the deal, I redesigned the website materials, built out their email marketing system, revamped their sales funnel, and increased prices to $299 a month or $1,000 a year (72 percent discount to incentivize annual payments). We lost 3 percent of our customers, but our annual revenue went up.
			

## Test 3: Can I Pay an Operator First?

			

Hard truth: if there is not enough profit in a deal, you are buying a job, not a business.
			
				
			
			

How much is “enough”? The business’s profit margins cover the costs of a quality operator and still allow you to hit your personal profit goals. That’s enough.
			

I don’t like to buy businesses unless they put $100,000 in annual profit in my pocket. So, when I’m evaluating a business, I’ll estimate the annual profits, subtract $100,000 or so for hiring an operator, then subtract another $100,000 for my salary. That means the business needs to be making at least $200,000 in profit.
			

It’s crucial that you ensure there is enough profit in the company to hire a quality operator first, and then put cash in your own pocket. Unless, of course, you want to run the business yourself (which is how I started). You’ll learn exactly how to hire an operator in Step 3, the “C is for Command” section of the book, so stay tuned.
			

These three simple tests—SOWS, BRRT, and Operator First—will give most first-time boring business owners the ability to rapidly evaluate whether a deal is worth pursuing or not. If the business passes all three tests, then it’s time to begin the due-diligence process. That’s what we’ll cover next.
			

## Due Diligence 101: How I Lost $12 Million in 90 Days

			
				

Trust but verify.
				

—Russian proverb, ironically popularized by Ronald Reagan while negotiating nuclear disarmament with the Soviet Union
			
			

A few years ago, my partners and I considered investing in a cannabis company in Oakland, California. They sold buds in cute little packages, but their main thing was manufacturing edibles, pre-rolls, concentrates, and topicals. Although weed was still restricted to just a few states, this company had been around for years serving the medical marijuana market. When recreational weed started spreading, this company’s strong brand helped it develop a distribution network in California, Oregon, and Arizona.
			

You may have noticed I haven’t named the company. I’m not going to. In a minute, you’ll understand why.
			

The company was run by this cute little fellow. Nicest guy you’ll ever meet. You know the type. Young and straitlaced. Married with two kids. Super at home with spreadsheets. Loved the numbers.
			

So, due diligence was a breeze. We examined tax returns, inventory lists, and all their profit-and-loss statements. It all checked out. Its revenue doubled in one year, and the California cannabis sector alone was expected to grow into a $5.2 billion industry. This investment was about as close to a no-brainer as you get. We invested $12 million for a large majority stake. The money would help the business increase production, acquire expanded sources of raw materials, hire more staff, and launch a new vape cartridge line. A line of ice pops. High-grade pre-rolls infused with concentrate and terpenes. The company would also start a nonprofit to provide medical marijuana to veterans suffering from opioid addiction. We were excited.
			

Then, after a few months, we got a frantic phone call from the guy.
			

“Everybody needs to fly out here immediately,” he said.
			

Through the speaker I could hear a quiver in his voice. Was he about to cry?
			

“What’s up?” I asked.
			

“Well, umm, well,” he stammered. “There’s no easy way…”
			

“Spit it out, we’re pros,” I said.
			

“We’re two weeks from running out of money.”
			

I fell silent, but thoughts were racing through my head. The big question, of course, was: How’d Opie from Mayberry blow through $12 million in three months?
			

Needless to say, we sent our best fixers to converge on the Bay Area to find out what was going on. What had we missed?
			

What we’d failed to notice in our due diligence was that our CEO was not who he appeared to be. As we reexamined the books, a few surprises emerged. Surprises. Understatement. These were more like unabridged WHAT THE HOLY F***? moments. For starters, he was using company funds to rent apartments for his mistresses. Not one, but two. He was paying family members large salaries for jobs that didn’t exist. And then there were the hookers and the drug parties, all funded by his naive investors.
			

Wait. It gets better.
			

We sat down with him to go over all that we discovered. When we told him his termination was immediate, he slammed back into his fancy leather office chair with a look of astonishment.
			

“What do you mean I’m fired?” he said. “I’m the CEO!”
			

“Dude,” I said. “You stole more than a million bucks from the company. You don’t have a job!”
			

He continued to stare at us with an open mouth.
			

“Not only do you not have a job, but we’re coming after you for the money you stole!”
			

So we fired him and sued him. He sued us back, claiming wrongful termination and slander. It’s a funny story unless it’s happening to you. He lost on both fronts.
			

As it turns out, the company survived. Someone else was put in charge of it, and the company went on to do well, winning awards, introducing new products, and eventually being purchased by a larger company. I got out of it and washed my hands of all cannabis investments.
			

Here’s the moral of the story: the bigger the investment, the deeper your due diligence should go. Higher levels, higher devils. Don’t take anything for granted. Trust but verify.
			

## Phase 1: Truth Telling

			

Due diligence should be conducted before starting any negotiations to buy a business. This involves a preliminary evaluation before even extending an offer, where you officially test what the seller has told you about their revenue, profit, customers, competitors, employees, and operating procedures.
			

You’re not trying to do a full colonoscopy but you are going to determine whether the seller is giving it to you straight about the health and future of the business. You want to know if the seller is telling the truth, but you also want to ferret out what the seller isn’t telling you. There are four ways for sellers not to tell the truth: lie, omit, obfuscate, and not even know the truth themselves. For instance, did they just lose their biggest customer due to some poor performance? Are they using undocumented workers? Do they think they are profitable but their accounting is wrong? Is what they’re telling you about their income the same as what they report to the IRS? Spoiler: doubtful.
			

Let’s be clear that there are typically two levels of due diligence for every deal. The first level is really called preliminary evaluation and occurs when you’re first evaluating the business and haven’t decided whether to make an offer. At this stage, you are asking for a simple set of documents:
			
				
- 
					

Three to six years of financials
				
				
- 
					

Expenses broken down annually (monthly is ideal but not always likely)
				
				
- 
					

Revenue broken down annually (monthly is ideal but not always likely)
				
				
- 
					

Asset and equipment list (valuations would be ideal but you’ll likely get them later)
				
				
- 
					

Photos of location
				
				
- 
					

Lease
				
				
- 
					

Overview report or summary
				
			
			

Remember, the seller is still running their business, and they likely won’t have a lot of time to track down reams of documents for you. You’re still just kicking the tires here. So keep your requests relatively light and work with the seller if you can to make it easier for them to produce what you need. You want to balance not spending a ton of time and money evaluating every single deal before offer with doing just enough to continue to the next stage. In our community and accelerator program we have an entire list of pre-offer and post-offer questions I like to ask. I won’t give you all one hundred of them now, but if you’re serious, go check out contrarianthinking.co.
			

Another awesome resource: Bizminer (bizminer.com). This site offers a trove of statistical data and industry trends for when you start doing due diligence. Their platform gives you access to the most frequently updated granular data so you can research, analyze, and prospect across nine thousand unique markets and ten thousand–plus companies.
			

We use flow charts for everything at Contrarian Thinking, so in our community we created them to allow for easy decision-making. It might go something like this:
			
				
					
					
					
				
				
					
						
							

Stage 1: Outreach
						
					
					
						
							

Decision Point
						
						
							

Criteria/Action
						
						
							

Next Steps
						
					
					
						
							

Evaluate basic information or listing online.
						
						
							

Is the deal in your Deal Box? (Key components/main criteria)
						
						
							

Yes: Proceed to sign NDA.
							

No: Pass on the deal.
						
					
				
			
			
				
					
					
					
				
				
					
						
							

Stage 2: Evaluation
						
					
					
						
							

Decision Point
						
						
							

Criteria/Action
						
						
							

Next Steps
						
					
					
						
							

Review financial information.
						
						
							

Enter as much as you can into your deal calculator.
						
						
							

Continue with detailed evaluation.
						
					
					
						
							

Assess business stability and location.
						
						
							
								
- 
									

Is it a good business? (Stable/rising sales, margins, seller’s discretionary earnings [SDE], net operating income [NOI], earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation, and amortization [EBITDA])
								
								
- 
									

Good market/location?
								
								
- 
									

Any gross defects?
								
							
						
						
							

Yes: Continue evaluation.
							

No: Pass on the deal.
						
					
					
						
							

Evaluate the asking price and financial health.
						
						
							
								
- 
									

Is the asking price reasonable?
								
								
- 
									

For small businesses: Is SDE multiple reasonable?
								
								
- 
									

For commercial real estate: Is cap rate > loan rate + 1%? Is cash flow sufficient?
								
							
						
						
							

Yes: Move to offer/negotiation.
							

No: Pass on the deal.
						
					
				
			
			

## Phase 2: The Full Monty

			

If after Phase 1 the business looks promising, you will want to move on to a more in-depth process of due diligence. At this point or prior, the seller may ask for a letter of intent (LOI). That’s fine, as long as the letter of intent is nonbinding. It may include the seller’s asking price, but the letter can make it clear that is not binding either. You can check out our Gold Standard LOI Template (see the appendix), but make sure to get yours from an attorney. I don’t play one in this book, on TV, or in real life.
			

In the in-depth round, you are looking at a variety of financials beyond the income-tax returns. Now you’re looking at cash-flow statements, contracts, business structure and bylaws, insurance, competitor information, cash management, company credit score, professional services agreements, legal issues, and employee data. A key thing to determine is what your break-even point will be. In other words, based on the financials you’re seeing, how long will it be before you get your investment back?[*7]
			

Now this is where most buyers start to get nervous. What do I know about bylaws? It’s going to be okay. I’ve given you a lengthy checklist, but you probably aren’t going to need all this material—many things listed are likely to be irrelevant in your deal. The key is to verify that the financials are accurate and that you have a full sense of the risk and liabilities involved. The smaller the business, the less complex they get. Right about here it’s really useful to have a group of accountability buddies. That’s why we built our Contrarian Community to guide people through buying these businesses in real time. But whether you join ours or find your own…get in the room with other dealmakers.
			

Every deal is different, so the items on your due diligence list will change. For instance, if you’re buying a laundromat where 80 percent of the revenue is in coins, you might want to do a random count of how many coins are deposited in the machines, two days per week, for a month. You’d want to look at the energy bills. How often are the machines serviced and does the business have a service contract with an appliance repairman that ensures timely responses when a machine breaks? In addition to checking the rent, check the length of the lease and compare what this business pays to what its neighbors are paying.
			

Look, too, for red flags, such as:
			
				
- 
					

Cash-flow discrepancies: In a cash-based business, like a laundromat, the owner reports less income on his tax return than on the financial statements he provided you. He explains that he uses cash sometimes to buy supplies. It doesn’t take a forensic accountant to see that the owner is skimming from his own business. When you evaluate the business, go with what he reports to the IRS, not what he claims to make.
				
				
- 
					

Consistently bad reviews on Yelp or Google: These aren’t deal killers, but you can use them as a bargaining chip in negotiations. Multiple complaints to a state licensing board? Possible deal killer.
				
				
- 
					

Missing licenses: The state tells you landscapers should have a certain license. When you ask to see the seller’s, he claims he “never needed one.” No bueno.
				
			
			

## Phase 3: Hired Guns and Experts

			

Remember that board you built in your mind? Now is your time to call on them. I often have my attorney and accountant review due diligence documents. For example, I’ll ask my accountant to review financials and tell me if they find any discrepancies. Or they might do a quick overview of the seller’s tax returns, receipts, and future inventory to see if any irregularities pop up. I might have my attorney review contracts or employee clauses. He’ll check to see if the company is or has been involved in any lawsuits and review any customer or supplier agreements.
			

I also recommend you get a subject matter expert in the sector you’re buying and pick their brain—much like how Lisa Sutton found that pack-and-ship operator to train her. You don’t always have to pay the person. You’d be amazed how thrilled an owner of a roofing company is to talk to a young gun about the biz. Cost = the price of lunch. If you’re not the best at making friends and mentors, no sweat. You can also offer to pay them fifty or a hundred bucks an hour for a short consultation. That hour might save you tens of thousands of dollars on a deal. Just do me a solid: do NOT do a deal without some sector-specific expert in your corner. You’re going to thank me for this later.
			

I call these folks my deal team, and there are others who rotate in and out. If the deal involves real estate, I’ll consult a realtor or a real estate broker and ask them to pull some comparable sales. Industry trade associations are also a good source of subject matter experts and advice on entering that sector. You can also use social media. I’ve done that several times. If I’m thinking of buying, say, a plumbing company, I’ll post the details of the deal and ask plumbing professionals on Reddit if it’s a good purchase or not. I did the same thing on Facebook for a laundromat I bought, and I got several great ideas for boosting profits. Experts and specialists love to share their knowledge. Think about how much free information I share with people. We humans get off on relevance.
			

Some elements of due diligence take a broader view. What kind of risks do operators in this sector sometimes face? How are those risks typically mitigated? What market forces or new technology could change how the business is run? Do sales come from a concentrated, discrete set of customers or from a broad base of customers? Does the business rely on a single supplier? Are the geographic boundaries of the business changing in any way? What kind of nearby development might affect future sales?
			

It may also make sense sometimes to meet with key employees to talk about their roles and future plans. Do they see themselves continuing with the company? Are they happy or are there other roles that might appeal more to them? If they were running the company, what changes would they make to improve the bottom line? These conversations can be valuable, but the seller may be reluctant to have employees know that he’s thinking about selling.
			

## Red Flags and Land Mines: Warning Signs to Look for During Due Diligence

			
				

The first rule of making money: don’t lose money. Second rule: don’t forget rule number one.
				

—Warren Buffett
			
			

When you start looking at a potential deal and doing your initial due diligence, it’s easy to become locked in and obsessed with this one deal. You’ll have to fight that natural tendency. Falling in love with a single business will only hurt you. You must be willing to walk away without feeling like you failed. Lesson from my father: “Never fall in love with something that can’t love you back.”
			

The truth is that buying a business is just like any investment: there are winners and there are losers. Even if you love the deal, a few red flags and land mines should send you running. You have to set aside your emotions and avoid businesses with the following characteristics, with the same fervor politicians have for avoiding taxes.
			
				
- 
					

 Unprofitable: ’Nuff said. Never buy a money-losing operation because you think you can turn it around. That’s for pros. You are not a pro yet. Once you are a pro, you can buy the ugly house on the block, fix it up, and flip it. For now, you wouldn’t even know how to stucco a kitchen. So keep it easy for yourself. Buy a nice house (aka biz) in a nice neighborhood (that has cash flow), and wait.
				
				
- 
					

Don’t bankrupt yourself: This is controversial, but for me at least, I would never do a recourse, personal-guaranteed loan on my first deal unless it was tiny. There is nothing worse than the weight of debt you cannot repay. As you get more sophisticated, you can do debt well. But in the beginning, be careful.
				
				
- 
					

Thin margins: Margins vary by sector, but generally speaking, a 30-plus percent margin is good, and 20 percent is average. Anything less than 10 percent should be a concern. Aim high, miss high.
				
				
- 
					

 Asset heavy: Take a page from the private equity firms that prefer nimble, asset-light companies. Laundromats are okay, but in the beginning, you want to avoid giant industrial production facilities.
				
				
- 
					

Not your ideal seller type: Personally, I like the older buyer who is willing to help with transitioning or managing the business while they ease into retirement. Avoid the thirtysomethings with hypergrowth companies who want a VC-style exit. Not a good dancing partner.
				
				
- 
					

 Not enough cash flow for an LBO: When beginning buyers like you borrow part of the purchase price against your new company’s assets or cash flow, that’s called a leveraged buyout (LBO). But the banks won’t lend if the cash isn’t flowing.
				
				
- 
					

Not enough cash flow to pay yourself: Remember, cash flow must repay the primary debt, repay whatever down payment you made to get the loan, pay for a manager to run the business, and then pay you, the new owner. This is simple math. You get paid what’s left.
				
				
- 
					

 Not enough cash for rainy days: Do you know most businesses don’t even know how much cash they have on hand, or how long that cash would last if their sales stopped or slowed? You will not be most people. You’ll ask yourself, “Self, do I have enough cash for hard times?” Have enough cash and you’ll sleep well.
				
				
- 
					

Have to operate it even if you don’t want to: Unless you’re buying yourself a new job, make sure there is enough profit in your new company to hire an operator who reports to you. If there isn’t, you may find yourself running the business every day rather than working on your next deal.
				
				
- 
					

Not completely transparent in due diligence: If the seller is not forthcoming with tax returns and their ledgers, drop the deal. Math wins.
				
				
- 
					

 Declining sales and too much competition: You may love the business, but if the books reveal declining revenue in a competitive market, walk away. Downside scenario planning will save you. What if your worst year happens this year? Are you still profitable? If not, pass. What if the sales fall by 50 percent? Still profitable? If not, restructure the deal so you don’t lose your shirt.
				
				
- 
					

Inability to scale or do add-ons: Your role as owner is to grow the business: attract more customers, get existing customers to spend more, or raise prices. If none of those is possible—either because the market is saturated or because the cash flow isn’t high enough to fund these endeavors—keep looking.
				
				
- 
					

Promoting EBITDA (gross): Earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation, and amortization is just a fancy way of calculating cash flow. It’s tossed around like it represents something greater than what it is. Cash flow is simply the amount of money coming in and leaving the business. It is not net profit. Good investors scoff at EBITDA at this level of business buying. If a company has a large amount of depreciable equipment, the cost of maintaining those assets isn’t accounted for. We want net cash flow. That’s it.
				
				
- 
					

If they say “proprietary patent pending”: Run. Odds are high that they are overstating their value and misleading you OR it’s more complex than you may want on your first deal.
				
				
- 
					

Unethical Seller: You can’t do a good deal with a bad guy. Do your homework on the people involved in the deal.
				
				
- 
					

no exit plan: How long will it take you to sell this business if you need to? Start knowing who would buy you up early. Ideally, I’d know before buying, so if I got in and hated the deal, I’d have someone to flip it to.
				
				
- 
					

Partners? Proceed with caution: Imagine every partner you have will leave you high and dry. It’s certainly happened to me. I didn’t always have a lot of rules for partners, but now I do.
				
				
- 
					

Seller Rushes the Deal: A super-pushy, antsy seller is a red flag. Deals take time. Just like starting a new job, you are on a new venture. Don’t make the mistake of signing before you’re ready.
				
				
- 
					

Don’t fall in love: Remember: “Never fall in love with something that can’t love you back.” This is the kiss of death. You’re too far into a deal, you really want to close, and the seller knows it. So right before you go to close, he adds last-minute terms that are unfavorable to you. Tiny paper cuts can bleed you out. Hold the line. There are too many deals to fall in love with one.
				
			
			

One last tip: Don’t keep the details all to yourself. Run your terms by experts before you sign. We have group members in our Contrarian Community who did not share the final deal terms and analysis before they bought a business. WHY? Terrible idea. What often happens: Deals move fast. You get caught up in it, you feel good about it, and it sucks to share your baby and have people call it ugly. Do the opposite of what feels good. Find a wet-blanket friend, have them review, and keep whittling down until even they get on board.
			
			• • •
			

As you come to the end of this chapter, you’re now at a critical point in your boring business journey: You’re officially ready to set the stage for negotiations. Congrats!
			

Now, throughout the dealmaking process, I want you to create a fun challenge for yourself: Could you do a deal with as little money down as possible and with as creative financing as possible? I call this the Transfer Risk Strategy. Of course, you may have to put some money down, and you may not be able to get complete seller financing. You may have to do a revenue share, sweat equity, or an SBA loan. That’s okay. But if you start out with a zero-down, seller-financing goal, you’re more likely to land closer to that ideal. M&A manifestation at its finest.
			

Your next assignment: Get on the same side of the table as the seller. This means presenting the seller with an informal proposal containing a realistic valuation of their business and an offer to buy it with seller financing. In other words, you’re going to name your price before the real negotiations begin.
			
				

Skip Notes
				
					

*1 Earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation, and amortization.
				
				
					

*2 This is a standardized service, packaged like a product. It has set tasks, clear pricing, and a repeatable process.
				
				
					

*3 Because a few of you restaurant-owning psychopaths are going to yell at me, show me the lie here. My rule with restaurants is: they are hard. Ask any restaurateur. You only open restaurants because you love them, are a top-level operator, and a bit of a masochist. I’ll invest in the top operators (and I do have stakes in three), but you couldn’t pay me enough to stop waiters from copulating on top of the croissant baskets and breathing Jack Daniel’s on customers. I’ve been a server—we wild. Eat at restaurants, don’t buy ’em.
				
				
					

*4 There is one online tool I use: bizscout.com. What makes it uniquely useful is that it creates lists of off-market businesses, and they push to get those businesses online. Those are what I’m looking for, so I can go in and propose my own price to the owner. (Disclaimer: I used bizscout.com so much that I ended up buying it.)
				
				
					

*5 This basically means that the seller agrees to receive a portion of the payment over time, instead of all up front. Seller financing is explained in detail in Step 2 (“I is for Invest”), where I break down how it works, when to use it, and the benefits for both parties.
				
				
					

*6 An SOP is a a step-by-step playbook that lays out exactly how to do something, every time. SOPs keep your operations consistent, efficient, and scalable—no guessing games, just clear instructions anyone can follow.
				
				
					

*7 For details, check out the Due Diligence Checklist at msmbook.com/tools. Many things on that detailed list won’t apply, but you can use it to design a due diligence checklist for the business you’re buying.

---

Step 2: I Is for Invest, Main Street Millionaire
		
		
		

	
	
		
			
			

##

---

The Life-Changing Magic of Profit Paybacks*, Main Street Millionaire
		
		
		

	
	
		
			

## The Life-Changing Magic of Profit Paybacks*

			

*Or How to Legally Buy Businesses for Zero Dollars (and Have the Seller Thank You for It)
			

David Foster Wallace once told a joke about two young fish who passed by an older fish one morning. The older fish said, “Morning, boys. How’s the water?” The two young fish swam on for a bit, and then looked at each other and said, “What the hell is water?”
			

We are the fish. Access and belief in the availability of money is our water. It’s all around us, and yet we spend our lives never really seeing or understanding it.
			

At least that was what it was like for me. I grew up in a household where my debit account was more overdrawn than it ever was full, where my mom counted every purchase I made, asking if I really needed it. I didn’t have a credit card until I was well into my twenties, and I thought debt was for poor, bad, or rather idiotic people.
			

One of the most tragic ways we conform is through our belief that money is scarce. Money is limited. Money is, in fact, all around us like water. In the United States, we’re long on wanting money but short on financial literacy.
			

Today, we are going to change that. I’m going to teach you how my understanding of money allowed me to buy cash-flowing businesses worth millions, in exchange for…no money at all. How did I do that? I Robin Hooded this sh*t. I stole the secrets from private equity and applied them to Main Street. And you can do the same thing when you buy your businesses too.
			

But we’ll get to that in a moment. First, let’s talk about debt.
			

How bad is debt, really?
			

The difference between you and the truly wealthy—the Rolls-Royce–driving, Hamptons-summering, multi-mansion-owning global elite—is not simply their bank account. It is their knowledge of how to turn their money into even more money. There is an entire cabal of humans who not only have money but have money that “works” for them. That is the dream we are pursuing.
			

Some of the methods I talk about in this chapter require loans, and I want to make sure you do not have a little voice in your head telling you it is always a bad idea to ask for money with the promise you’ll pay it back. That’s all debt is, essentially—an IOU with repercussions.
			

Debt has historically been turned into the bad guy. When I wrote a tweet about the power of debt, it garnered 150 trolling comments like these:
			

Shame on you, Allah says debt is sin.
			

You just want to control the poor with debt as shackles.
			

Ah, the internet, you are special. But this time I was genuinely shocked. Some noted that the Aramaic word for sin and debt are identical (chovah). Therefore, if you use debt, you are a sinner. That’s a new one for me. Another said Islam forbids taking interest payments. Who knew? Even Buddha asserted that interest-bearing, centralized, privatized debt is harmful, unethical, and delusional. Bottom line, debt users: forget Nirvana, we’re all going to hell.
			

I’m no religious scholar. Maybe all those things are true. But I have a different view. In my mind, debt can be incredibly dangerous, but if used properly, it’s a lever that lifts the world from poverty. When you remove the emotion and insert rationality, debt makes a lot of sense. (Thank God, Allah, and the Buddha that you’re reading this in a book and not on Twitter.)
			

## OPM: Other People’s Money

			

No matter how much wealth you’ve accumulated, there is a saying in investing: “I like to use cash, just not mine.” That’s why private equity firms do giant leveraged buyouts using the bank’s money to accelerate growth. That’s why we, and even Taylor Swift, use mortgages to buy houses. And that’s why you will use the Profit Payback method to do your deals. You’re inside the cool kids club now.
			

## The Profit Payback Method

			

This is my not-so-secret secret weapon. Sixty percent of all small businesses change hands using the Profit Payback method, more commonly known as seller financing or creative financing. This means that they get to pay the seller for their business over time, using the future profits from the business. Don’t let the name “seller financing” fool you. It’s not the seller paying for it, it’s the business’s profits that are paying for the purchase.
			

Now wait a minute. You may be thinking, Why would a seller give you their future profits so you get the right to buy their business? So many reasons. Once you see this, you can’t unsee it. Let’s walk through an example.
			

My friend Matt wanted to buy a business for $500,000, but he didn’t want to put up all the cash and didn’t want to deal with a bank or the SBA office. His seller agreed to finance the deal. It looked like this:
			
				
- 
					

Price of the business: $500,000
				
				
- 
					

Annual profit: $200,000
				
				
- 
					

Monthly profit: $16,000
				
				
- 
					

Down payment: $50,000
				
				
- 
					

Monthly payments to owner: $4,000 over 120 months (10 years) plus interest
				
				
- 
					

Monthly profit to Matt: $10,000 after payout and interest
				
			
			

Why did the seller do this deal? Because no one else wanted to buy his business. He was located in a small town, and he was ready to move and retire.
			

Members of our Contrarian Community have done millions[*1] in seller-financing deals, just like that one. No debt. No high-risk collateral (like putting up your house). None of your cash to buy a profitable business. The Profit Payback method—aka seller financing—is hands down the most common approach I use when buying a business.
