## Why I Love Profit Paybacks

			

Everything is negotiable. Everything. Do you want a lower monthly payment and a longer loan length? Ask. Do you want to include some assets in your terms? It’s on the table. Would you rather put more cash up front? That’s an option too. This is the exact opposite of bank loans and the SBA. There, everything is set. No wiggle room. With seller financing, nothing is set, and the only thing that’s standard is wiggles.
			

The best part is that you can adjust the terms to fit the business. You’ve seen all their financials during the due diligence process, so if you want lower payments over a longer period, it’s totally doable. You can also incentivize the owner by giving them a higher payout if the business makes more than anticipated, or if the seller extends the terms. The ceiling is only as low as your creative structure. The more you know, the better deals you can do.
			

## What Is Common in Profit Paybacks?

			

Terms vary widely with seller financing, but the baseline is typically:
			
				
- 
					

Average term length: 3 to 10 years
				
				
- 
					

Average percentage down: 10 to 50 percent (sellers will almost always do 50 to 70 percent down and finance the rest)
				
				
- 
					

The seller might:
					
						
- 
							

Request financial statements during the term of the loan
						
						
- 
							

Want to remain on the lease during the note
						
						
- 
							

Require the buyer meets certain financial benchmarks, like keeping a minimum in a bank account, etc.
						
					
			
			

You might still be wondering: But why would a seller do this type of deal? Many reasons. But sometimes it’s easier to show you than tell you.
			

## More Money and an Annuity

			

Here’s how you get the seller to finance your business with a profit purchase…and thank you for it. Real life example: You find a business to buy. The seller wants $1 million. So you take it to the bank to get a loan. The bank says, “Well, the cash flow can support a purchase price of $750,000, not $1,000,000, and it’ll be an 8 percent interest rate per year we’ll charge you. That means another $60,000 a year you’ll pay in interest.”
			

You think about that and say, well, if the business does what the seller says it is going to do, I’d pay $1.15 million for it, not $1 million. So you go to the seller and say, “Hey, Carl, there are two options we can do. Option 1 is I use the bank and pay you $750,000 for your business plus interest to the bank on the loan. OR I pay you $1.15 million, plus a 5 percent interest rate, which means another $50,000-ish a year to you and you loan me the money from the business. We use the profits of the business to give you more money, and you make all the money the bank would have made. As long as the business does what you say it has and will keep unlocking more money.” Game, set, match.
			

More cash for the seller, and the seller (not the bank) gets an annuity.
			
				
					
					
				
				
					
						
							

Description
						
						
							

Value
						
					
					
						
							

Purchase price
						
						
							

$1,000,000
						
					
					
						
							

Number of years over which seller financing is paid
						
						
							

10
						
					
				
			
			
				
					
					
					
					
				
				
					
						
							

Category
						
						
							

Bank
						
						
							

Seller Financing
						
						
							

Notes
						
					
					
						
							

Cash
						
						
							

$750,000
						
						
							

$1,150,000
						
						
							

25% discount to their offer versus 15% more than their offer price
						
					
					
						
							

Interest rate (given to the bank versus interest paid to seller over ten years)

$57,500
						
						
							

8% bank versus 5% seller financing
						
					
					
						
							

TOTAL TO SELLER
						
						
							

$750,000
						
						
							

$1,207,500
						
						
					
				
			
			

Now dear Carl, how about taxes? Do you like paying them? Me neither. What if we could spread out taxes, defer them, and likely by that time your income would be lower and your tax bracket could be lower? You have two options. We could do a lump-sum payment and you could pay the total amount all at once. Or you could pay taxes over ten years. I know which I’d prefer. Maybe we even keep you on in a consulting 1099 capacity so that you have more write-offs to use on a go-forward basis.
			

## Tax Savings: Long-Term Payouts over Years, Not a Lump Sum

			
				
			
			

So Carl, we’ve gotten you more money and less taxes. How about we also do the deal faster? Let’s look again at two options. Option A: We get a loan from the SBA; their website says they take ninety-plus days on average. When has a bank ever been faster than average for you? Me neither. Also, Carl, the world is tough, who knows what could happen in ninety days. Deal could fall apart, and banks could stop lending. Or, with Option B, Profit Payback, we can close as fast as you, me, and our attorneys can move. We’ve even closed deals in a week once all the due diligence is done. Time kills deals. Let’s not kill ours, eh?
			
				
					
					
					
					
					
					
					
				
				
					
						
							

Day 1
						
						
							

Day 2
						
						
							

Day 3
						
						
							

Day 4
						
						
							

Day 5
						
						
							

Day 6
						
						
							

Day 7
						
					
					
						
							

Share seller financing terms
						
						
							

Joint attorney call to walk through offer
						
						
							

Negotiate and confirm terms with attorneys
						
						
							

Sign terms
						
						
							

Transfer down payment
						
						
							

Transfer ownership docs
						
						
							

Business sold
						
					
				
			
			
				
			
			

The amount of opportunity will always be limited by your ability to recognize it.
			

After all, seller financing is not a one-sided transaction at all. The seller gets:
			
				
- 
					

Cheaper closing, including no bank fees or appraisal or mandated costs.
				
				
- 
					

A done deal. Some businesses are hard to sell or don’t have many buyers, and seller financing attracts interest.
				
				
- 
					

Paid over time at a lower tax rate.
				
				
- 
					

Cash flow like an annuity, allowing them to maintain a lifestyle they want to keep.
				
				
- 
					

An interest payment. The buyer is going to pay bank loan interest, so why not pay the seller instead?
				
				
- 
					

More money. Seller financing can help sellers increase their price.
				
				
- 
					

Faster closing. The buyer isn’t waiting for loan approval, underwriting, and legal.
				
				
- 
					

More potential buyers. With seller financing, the seller can entertain offers from those who can’t get a loan.
				
				
- 
					

To leave a legacy. A seller who wants to transfer to an employee or someone they like can use seller financing to make it easy on them.
				
			
			

## What Cons Are There?

			

Honestly, not many. However, there are a few worth considering:
			
				
- 
					

Higher price: If you can get a killer business at a lower price that you feel 100 percent certainty about, then maybe it’s cheaper in total price to go to the bank.
				
				
- 
					

Tied to the seller: Sometimes it’s cleaner to just cut and deal with a third party.
				
				
- 
					

Higher interest: Occasionally the interest rate with seller financing may be higher (I’d fight that).
				
				
- 
					

Seller approval: You have to talk the seller into this transaction. They may not want to be your bank.
				
			
			

Let’s walk through one of my deals.
			

## How I Used Profit Payback to Buy My First Laundromat

			

One day, I was sitting in my finance job, spectacularly miserable making money for other people I didn’t even like. It was maybe my tenth or twelfth consecutive weekend in the office until 9:00 p.m. under fluorescent lights, painfully making someone else millions. Ever been there?
			

I realized, Ya know what, if these jagaloons in braided belts can do deals, why can’t I? Thus my humble beginnings with buying laundromats and making money with quarters. The balance sheet of the laundromat looked about the same over the five-year period I requested, showing a consistent profit.
			

When I reached out to the owner, he said he wanted roughly $200,000 and would finance a good chunk of it through seller financing. Given that the seller really wanted to sell, there were no other buyers, and it had been listed for over a year, I was able to negotiate him down to a much lower buying price. A perfect storm for me to get a deal.
			

But let’s say I couldn’t get that deal done at that lower price but could still make it work at something higher, say, around $125,000. I’d give him a small down payment, something in the ballpark of $25,000, and then structure a Profit Payback to do the rest of the payment over three years. Let me show you why this is so intriguing, even at the higher price point:
			

For this deal, I would be making a solid profit each month. Then, I would be at breakeven in three months for my initial cash down, and my cash-on-cash return in year one would be downright impressive. Close to 3X!
			

Once the deal was settled, I could use the proceeds as cash down on another business.
			

My friend Brandon of Investment Joy did this exact process:
			
				
- 
					

Total deal size = $675,000, but 94 percent was seller financed. Brandon put in $40,000 of his own money for the down payment and the other $635,000 was negotiated as seller financing with 3 percent interest.
				
				
- 
					

Of his three main businesses, they are more than 90 percent seller-financed.
				
			
			

Honestly, the businesses I bought back then weren’t even great deals at that price. Not enough profit, too small, operators I couldn’t rely on. It was a ton of work and I wish I had done a thousand things differently. Those first deals were jobs, not businesses; I was too scared of failing and going bigger on a real deal. I also made tons of mistakes but still made money. So don’t for one second think I’m some kind of genius, or lucky, for buying these. I was not, and still am not. I’m largely allergic to spreadsheets and get hives looking at forward projections. If you’re willing to roll up your sleeves, the Profit Payback method is the way.
			

## “But I’m Still Scared of Debt!”

			

If it scares you to take on debt, good. It should. The first deal you do is the most important deal you’ll ever do; get it wrong, and you’ll think deals are bad. Get it right, and your life changes forever.
			

There are really two main questions to ask yourself about debt before you take it on:
			
				
- 
					

Will it pay for itself?
					

I only take on debt when the return on what I’m using it for will pay me more than the debt service. I don’t buy boats or fast cars. I do deals. Example: Buy a business with a stock loan and use the profits to pay for the debt service.
					
				
- 
					

What if it doesn’t pay for itself?
					

Chaos theory reigns. If something can go wrong, it probably will. So, what happens if I am totally wrong and I end up not being able to cover the debt service? What will I do? How do I cut my losses? First, I never do a deal so big it can bankrupt me. Please don’t do that. Get investors, use seller financing, don’t mortgage your house. Learn, earn, then invest. Eventually, if you do this right, your first deal will look dumb in retrospect. You’ll realize it was too small, too far, too whatever. So you make sure that it’s sellable. Then you sell it. That’s what I do. You can too.
					
			
			

## The Truth about Debt

			
				

Smart men go broke three ways: liquor, ladies, and leverage.
				

—Charlie Munger
			
			

Charlie’s partner, Warren Buffett, famously corrected him later to say, “My partner Charlie says there are only three ways a smart person can go broke: liquor, ladies, and leverage. Now the truth is, the first two he just added because they started with L—it’s leverage.”
			

Leverage is just another word for debt. Debt is neither good nor bad. It is simply a tool. However, it’s a tool that separates the rich from the poor. Use it wisely to create millions. Use it poorly to bury yourself under its weight. The wealthy understand debt. They use debt to buy cash-flowing businesses that will, in turn, pay off the debt. They use it to fund those businesses’ growth (lines of credit). They use it to buy real estate (mortgages). Average people don’t understand debt, so they do dumb things with it. They use debt to buy liabilities (things that don’t make any money) and then wonder how they’re in such a deep hole. Remember, leverage done wrong can bury you.
			

Just remember the old Warren Buffett–ism: “Never risk what you have and need for what you don’t have and don’t need.”
			

If you read the above and say, “That won’t work for me,” you are probably right. It won’t. If you read the above and think, “How could that work for me?” it probably will.
			

I’ve built broke. I’ve built scared. I’ve built alone. And let me tell you: It’s never the right time to buy a business. But it’s also always the right time.
			

So, as you move forward with the negotiations process, I want you to remember these two words: seller financing. That is the debt strategy that wealthy people use, which will allow you to buy your first business with little money down, bringing you one step closer to financial freedom.
			

## Five More Ways to Buy a Boring Business without Relying Only on Cash

			

There are many paths to the promised land. After doing hundreds of deals and helping thousands of people, we’ve found several other ways to buy a business with little or no money down. I’ve seen all of them work again and again, depending on the situation.
			

I am often asked, “Which is best?” The annoyingly true answer: it depends. So in this section, you’ll see the pros, the cons, and some examples of five more strategies we’ve used to buy businesses using smart financing.
			

## 1. Loans (SBA, Asset Loans, Equipment Loans, etc.)

			

This is probably the only love letter to our government you’ll ever see me write. My favorite three-letter word: SBA…well, SBA loan.
			

Think of it like this: dear old Uncle Sam is giving you up to 90 percent of the purchase price for you to buy a small business. Wild. Basically, the government works with banks to set guidelines for loans to be qualified under SBA requirements. It’s like having the government back your loan, so if you go belly-up, the government covers some of it, which means lenders lend more freely. It’s genuinely a phenomenal program.
			

What’s the catch? There are a few parameters (also these change, so hit up your local SBA bank):
			
				
- 
					

The business has to be profitable.
				
				
- 
					

It has to have been around for at least two to five years.
				
				
- 
					

There are limits on how much money they’ll give you.
				
				
- 
					

You have to qualify, as does the business.
				
			
			

What does it take for you to qualify for an SBA loan? Specific requirements can vary, but you’ll typically need the following:
			
				
- 
					

Credit score of 680 or higher
				
				
- 
					

Minimum 10 percent down payment
				
				
- 
					

No criminal history
				
				
- 
					

No ongoing federal debt
				
				
- 
					

Industry or managerial experience
				
				
- 
					

Bankrupt-free record from the past three years
				
			
			

Now, the most common loan I’ve used is an SBA 7(a) loan. That is the most flexible and basic loan, and the interest rate is based on the amount you want to borrow up to $5 million (there are nuances here and this varies, so go talk to your local bank). You can use it to buy a business or get working capital, expand a business, purchase equipment, and improve facilities. The loan term can be up to ten years for working capital and up to twenty-five years for fixed assets.
			

There are many other types of SBA loans, so I recommend going to sba.gov to find an SBA loan office that can give you options on which type of loan you qualify for. They have options for veterans, minorities, and more. Let’s not turn this into an encyclopedia, eh?
			

Your loan application will require (at least):
			
				
- 
					

A purchase agreement
				
				
- 
					

A letter of intent to buy the business
				
				
- 
					

The past three years of business tax returns
				
				
- 
					

Reporting of any outstanding business debt
				
				
- 
					

A business lease agreement (if leasing)
				
				
- 
					

Reporting of long-term business contracts
				
				
- 
					

Incorporation documents and business license
				
				
- 
					

Documentation of business assets
				
			
			

You’ll also typically want a short business plan to show that you are not completely inept and are capable of running the business. If you Google “SBA sample business plans,” you can steal other people’s homework.
			SBA Benefits:
			
				
- 
					

Lower down payment: Banks typically want 20 to 30 percent down, but you can (sometimes, not always) do 10 percent with the SBA.
				
				
- 
					

Longer terms: You can get loans anywhere from ten to twenty-five years as opposed to the standard three to ten years.
				
				
- 
					

Easier qualifications: It’s infinitely easier to get this loan.
				
			
			Cons:
			
				
- 
					

Time: It usually takes 90 to 120 days. (Is the government ever speedy?)
				
				
- 
					

Rates: You can get lower rates than an SBA loan if you have a good relationship with a local bank. Interest rates are often variable, which can be very problematic.
				
				
- 
					

Inflexible payments: There are set terms, prepayment penalties, and variable interest rates.
				
				
- 
					

Personal guarantee: You are on the hook if you can’t repay. THIS IS BIG.
				
				
- 
					

Decent credit: The loan is assessing the business, but you can’t have bottom scores.
				
			
			

Bottom line: It’s found money. It’s got strings attached, but it’s a source nonetheless. The government certainly takes enough of your taxes, and this is an opportunity to take some back.
			Who gives you the loan?
			

Below is a list of some of the top-ranked SBA 7(a) lenders in the United States. Call them, or you can hit up the SBA direct. It has local centers across the country ready to help you.
			
				
- 
					

Live Oak Bank (liveoakbank.com, 866-518-0286). They’re my favorite. We do deals with them and we do get a commission, FYI. Use the code word “Codie” for them to hook you up.
				
				
- 
					

Huntington National Bank (huntington.com, 800-480-2265)
				
				
- 
					

Newtek Small Business Finance (newtekone.com, 800-749-8707)
				
				
- 
					

Celtic Bank (celticbank.com, 801-363-6500). We also get a commission here, as we use them a lot too.
				
			
			

## 2. Customer Acquisition for $0

			

Brittany ran a small gym during the height of the pandemic. Walking down her town’s main street, she could almost hear the snap, snap, snap of closed signs going up in windows as she walked by. Of the ten fitness companies she competed with, only two were left standing by the end of 2020. She called me one day to ask about buying equipment from the closing gyms at a discount, aka an asset acquisition.
			

“You are missing the boat entirely,” I said. “The opportunity is in helping them sell and you buying them.” Here’s how Brittany did it:
			
				
- 
					

She reached out to all the gym owners who had closed or seemed close to closing. She said she wanted to help them recoup some of their costs through creative acquisitions.
				
				
- 
					

She told each owner she would give them 50 percent of all the revenue for six months from any client they transferred to her business. Now Brittany’s former competitors had skin in the game of building Brittany’s business. (Brittany could have structured this as a percentage payout over a multiyear period based on future sales so the former owners had an income for a few years and Brittany could have deferred payment.)
				
				
- 
					

She brought in the former owners to her club to do a farewell class and handhold their clients over.
				
				
- 
					

She helped former owners draft email/social/marketing messages for all their former clients to explain how they didn’t want to leave them without a home, so they found them one.
				
				
- 
					

She gave former owners lifetime memberships to her business and welcomed them to the family.
				
				
- 
					

She created an email and marketing sequence that dripped on the new customers for a few weeks to close them.
				
			
			

Brittany acquired $100,000-plus in revenue for her business with two acquisitions, for a total cost of zero dollars. She gave those customers a home and the dejected owner a win. Hell, the owner could even say they sold their business instead of closing it. You cannot underestimate what that means to an owner.
			

Let’s say a business is closing in your area. What if you reached out to the owner and repurposed their clients to your business? You propose a deal to refer their clients to you, they get a percentage of the revenue of clients who sign up, and you help the owner craft a “goodbye or transition plan.” In one fell swoop, you’ve allowed the owner to get an annuity, given those clients a new home, and cut yourself into a revenue stream or a new business line.
			

## 3. Customer Acquisition for Referral Fees

			

The average restaurant doesn’t last longer than a year. But when they go under, they get nothing and all their customers have to find a new spot. Hold my beer, let’s fix that.
			

Peter, a member of our Contrarian Community, reached out to a local restaurant owner who was going out of business. He asked if they would send their customer list a final goodbye email with a discount code that was good for a year at another restaurant nearby. Here was the deal:
			
				
- 
					

Anytime a customer bought something with that code for 25 percent off, both the previous owner and the acquiring restaurant made money.
				
				
- 
					

That restaurant owner, who had a list of fifteen thousand customers, made $2,250 in two weeks following his email.
				
				
- 
					

Meanwhile, Peter made $1,000 off that same email and continued to make more than $100 a week from that deal.
				
				
- 
					

As for the acquiring restaurant owner—that customer list he essentially bought into for $0 has netted him more than $20,000.
				
				
- 
					

Once he realized that this strategy would be useful, the old owner switched his email list to being a recommendations list with his “best of” picks. So that is almost $23K from a business that previously was going to evaporate for $0.
				
			
			

The playbook is simple: Find businesses that are closing or closed, email their owners to see if they want to do a referral fee for any customers transferred, then find an adjacent business that will pay you for those warm leads in perpetuity. Voila, you’ve bought yourself a revenue share in a business.
			

## 4. Revenue Share Acquisition for $0

			

My friend Drew Sanocki made a career out of paying one dollar for big and small websites that were losing money. He turned AutoAnything into a nine-figure company by buying up money-losing websites for a dollar each and growing them. He even bought businesses doing millions in revenue for one dollar. How did he get something for nothing? By promising the owners a percentage of future revenue as he turned the companies around.
			

Why would anyone do this? These are often called zombie companies by private equity and venture capital funds. They barely exist, careening along costing them either money or time. They’ve tried to grow them and failed. All of a sudden an expert with a track record of success comes along and says, “Your business can keep bleeding, or you can let me take it over, turn things around, and take a percentage of my upside. Which do you prefer?”
			

## 5. Employee Acquisition for $0

			

There is a uniquely terrible feeling of being on video, with your own face, and those videos being awful…and all over the internet. That was me, and I was drowning in misery. My editors were chopping up videos so poorly that one got memed. My ego was bruised AF. The problem was we couldn’t find good enough editors, producers, and videographers in Austin. So I hired a vendor. They were expensive, but they were AWESOME. Then I did what I always do, I talk to the owners. His name is Tanner. I was like, “Tanner, how’s this going? Do you like the agency model? Seems like a lot of work?” These are leading questions. Tanner proceeded to tell me it all. Not fun. Tough partners. Not working. Hmm. Insert maniacal, calculated laughter. “Young Tanner, what if I bought your company?” He was like, umm yes. “But here’s the catch: I don’t want your partners and I don’t want the assets. I really just want you and your team. So I’ll do what’s called an acqui-hire. I’ll buy out your company but just for the cost of you and your team’s salaries, plus a signing bonus if they all come on and stay on for a certain period. You get a sale on your résumé, and I get great team members.” Win-win.
			

In VC land, this is called acqui-hiring—a combination of acquisition and hiring. We also did this with instructors at Brittany’s gym and added ten new employees who brought hundreds of new clients with them.
			

After all, does anyone think it’s easy to hire right now? Ya, me neither. Well, how about this: A business that is closing refers employees to you, a competitor who is staying open. You pay either a finder’s fee for all employees who stay past a certain period or a profit revenue share for any clients brought over. Here’s how you can play too:
			
				
- 
					

Do the same process with employees of the other gyms or businesses in your sector.
				
				
- 
					

Ask the owner to help transfer their best employees or contractors and client list.
				
				
- 
					

Give the owner and the employee a percentage share of revenue or profits for their clients.
				
				
- 
					

Give new employees off-hours that you currently don’t have filled to do their classes.
				
				
- 
					

Bring them in to co-teach a class with one of your other instructors.
				
				
- 
					

By acquiring employees through revenue or profit share, you are growing inorganically with zero dollars out of pocket.
				
				
- 
					

Keep only those who work to offset their costs.
				
			
			

The moral of this story is, if you run a business and you aren’t out there trying to buy competitors who are closing, you are missing a huge opportunity. Plus, it’s fun as hell.
			

## Prepare for Purchase: Getting Your Documents in Order

			

## The Blank Page: How to Begin the Negotiation Process

			

## People > Terms

			

When most people do deals, they try to turn it into a full-blown audit. But I like to steal Buffett’s homework. Buffett is famous for executing deals on a single-page contract. Why? There are only two things that matter in analyzing deals.
			
				
- 
					

Will I make money? (the business economics—revenue, profit, expenses, assets)
				
				
- 
					

Do I want to work with these people? (the team—employees, clients, contractors, sellers)
				
			
			

So before we get into how we do deals, I want to give you a little secret trick I call the Blank Page Start. Why? Because lawyers are awesome…at complicating things. So when I’m negotiating and structuring the deal, before I bring in an attorney, I take a blank piece of paper and write out something like this:
			
				

Hey, seller, we’ve agreed there might be a deal here. I want to buy your business for X amount, using Y financing, based on Z terms and conditions.
			
			

I do this on an actual blank page and use a pen. Last thing you want to do is scare off a seller, so keep it stupid simple. Personally, I make the deal basic and nonbinding, then I sign and date it. Pro tip: once you get the first yes, the next one is always easier.
			

I scan the page and email it as an attachment to the seller, with a brief message: “If these terms sound good to you, let’s both sign this and I’ll get it over to my attorney to paper the deal.” I don’t bring in the attorney until both sides have signed the blank page.
			

If you haven’t realized it by now, this book is not normal. We have three main tenets here: fast and simple financing, fast and simple signing, fast and simple negotiating. You get the idea. If time and complexity kill deals, we try to eliminate deal killers.
			

## Negotiating Price (without Shooting Yourself in the Foot)

			

What’s the old adage? Never say your price first? That’s good advice, usually. But when you’re buying a small business from an owner who has never done a deal before (which is most owners), they will almost certainly have an inflated idea of what their business is worth. They have put their lives into it, so letting them set the floor can often send you to fantasyland. If their business is making $200,000 a year and the owner says they believe it’s worth $20 million, walking them back down to a realistic figure is hard. The seller now has to lose face, and they have what is called price anchoring. They are anchored to an unrealistic price.
			

What do I do differently than almost all other buyers? I name my price up front. By naming my price first, I get to avoid these emotional pitfalls. Besides, I am buying something that I’ll need the seller’s help with in the future. I always want to pay a price that is a win-win. Let one seller lose, and you’ll never buy from them (or their friends) again.
			

How do you set a price on a thing when you have no idea what it’s worth? Let the market decide. Small businesses typically sell somewhere between 1 and 5X their net annual profit. I also like to say to the seller:
			
				

By the way, we’ll get into how profit is truly calculated because it varies. Small businesses sell for 1 to 5X their annual profit at your level. It’s not my price, it’s the market price. Given there are a lot of companies, transitioning and buying is hard, and the seller is always at an information disadvantage, we just can’t go above those numbers.
			
			

In other words: it’s really out of your hands. Now you’re on the same side of the aisle as them. Dang market. You wish you could pay them more. You just can’t.
			

Then, just as a realtor will show market comps when buying a house, I will fortify my point by handing them a white paper from Contrarian Thinking’s Annual SMB Report (see the appendix) showing the average purchase price. The final number will be determined by several variables—from inventory and equipment to location and revenue history—but giving them a range will tamp down their emotional investment and tendency to overvalue their business by 10,000X. Now you’re in the game.
			

Finally, always leave them with a mint. You know how you go to a nice restaurant and you get a complimentary mint with your bill? The last moment is minty and magical. You should do the same. They may not remember your first words, but they’ll remember your last. So you wrap up this initial conversation on a positive note, reaffirming your interest in their business and your respect for what they’ve built. Emphasize this one point: you only win if they win. You need the business to continue and them to be aligned with you. By taking the lead, you’re laying the groundwork for a smooth and respectful negotiation process.
			

Once they’ve signed your page and sent it back, it’s time to lawyer up.
			

## How to Find the Right Attorney

			
				

If you think hiring a professional is expensive, wait until you hire an amateur.
				

—Red Adair
			
			

Before you obsess about whether to set up a C Corp, LLC, ESOP, or some other baffling acronym, you need to obsess about finding a good attorney who knows how to structure deals. A smart attorney with extensive M&A experience can make you ten times what you’d make using a $200 online legal service. Trust me. This is your “Who Not How” moment.
			

In other words, the only way to boost your wealth and productivity is to find out who can help you, not how to get things done. The wrong question is “How do I create a great letter of intent?” The right question is “Who can make my deal structure bulletproof?”
			

Where do you find your attorney? Three methods:
			
				
- 
					

 Pros refer pros: Ask your mentors or accountants who they recommend. Pros only refer pros. Amateurs can’t tell the difference.
				
				
- 
					

Rich people: With money comes risk. Where there is risk, there are attorneys. Ask your rich friends; rich people often have good attorneys. And yes, even if you’re broke, you do know some rich people. Everyone is six degrees from Kevin Bacon.
				
				
- 
					

The Who’s Who lists: Consult the local “luxury” magazines for their annual best-of results and see who makes their lists in the legal field. You can also go to Vault Law 100 (vault.com), which ranks the most prestigious law firms based on assessments of lawyers at peer firms. BizScout also has a list of our favorite vendors.
				
			
			

Once you’ve got some leads, interview your candidates. Here are some questions to ask:
			
				
- 
					

How many deals have you done?
				
				
- 
					

What size deals do you specialize in?
				
				
- 
					

In what sectors?
				
				
- 
					

What geographic region are you familiar with? (It usually helps to find someone familiar with your local turf.)
				
				
- 
					

What price points?
				
			
			

When in the company of attorneys, always remember every conversation comes with a price tag. If you can, get a flat-rate quote from your attorney for drawing up the papers and advising you on the deal. They’ll always give you an hourly rate, but I say, “Hey, here is what I want. I want an LOI, a purchase agreement, and an operating agreement for a company worth less than X amount. What is the cost to get those things accomplished?” Then comparison shop.
			

The right questions make you money. You’ll want to get a time estimate from them. How fast can you get these documents back to me? How many revisions do we need to have? The biggest killer of deals is time, so speed is important. You should take your time to find the right deal for you, but once you’ve found it, you want to move.
			

Lawyers have more specialties than birds have feathers. You may find that you can use the same attorney for similar deals in the future. But if you execute a completely different type of deal, it could make sense to find another lawyer. For example, I tend to use one attorney for many of my purchases, but recently when I was acquiring some intellectual property, I switched to a person who specializes in that area of the law. This is when using a midsize firm might be to your advantage. Midsize firms often “got a guy” for just about everything, so it might be easier and cheaper to stay within the firm you’re already using. Just get a new guy.
			

After you’ve found your attorney, the next step is to focus on the three essential documents you’ll need to get this deal done.
			

## The Three Documents You Need from Your Attorney

			

Those who write the blueprint control the structure. As the buyer, you should always do your own documents—or, in all likelihood, your attorney should. Do NOT let the seller or the seller’s broker do the documents; whatever draft you get from them is inherently biased. You want your attorney to come up with clean documents—your documents.
			

I’ve had many, many sellers say, “Oh, the broker gave me the offering docs and I’m going to review them and make sure the terms look good.” Nope. No, you’re not. So you say, “No, let’s start with my documents because I’m the one buying the company. Then your attorney can review them and make sure you like them.”
			

Contracts cost. Expect to pay $3,000 to $25,000, with the complexity of the deal increasing the overall cost. Apart from your college degree, these documents will likely be the most expensive pieces of paper you’ve ever purchased. The only difference is that these can actually make you a lot of money.
			
				
- 
					

Letter of intent (LOI), nonbinding or binding: Think of the LOI as an engagement ring. It’s your official proposal to buy their business. It’s also a framework that aligns both of your expectations going into the deal. A nonbinding LOI indicates that both parties are serious about the transaction but are not legally committed to proceed. A binding LOI has legal consequences and may require both parties to complete the deal under the terms specified. There is nuance here, a lot of it. So get with your attorney before you move forward on this stage.
				
				
- 
					

Purchase agreement: If the LOI is the engagement ring, the purchase agreement is your marriage certificate at the courthouse. This is the main document in the acquisition process, which details all of the final terms and conditions of the business sale. Unlike the LOI, this document is legally binding. For the buyer, it ensures that they receive what they are paying for. For the seller, it provides assurance that they will be compensated as agreed upon.
				
				
- 
					

Operating agreement/shareholder agreement: There is no document like this one in a marriage, so let’s kill that analogy now. The operating agreement outlines the business structure, management roles, profit-sharing mechanisms, and other operational details. This document will dictate how the business is run post-acquisition, including decision-making processes, dispute resolution, and profit distribution. You and your lawyer will want to ensure this is aligned with your vision and management style for the business.
				
			
			

A key thing for your attorney to know is whether you think you’ll eventually sell the business or keep it forever. Here are other key questions to know about yourself:
			
				
- 
					

Do you want to buy more than one business and add it to your company, or are you interested in buying just one business?
				
				
- 
					

How big do you think this business will get? For example, if it’s a laundromat, you’re probably not going to sell for $10 million. Therefore, you do not need a complex C Corp structure. But only you, your attorney, and the business can tell you that.
				
			
			

This leads us to another common question I get:
			

What type of company structure should my business have?
			

Never take marital advice from a single person, and never take legal advice from a non-attorney. Your lawyer is the best person to answer this for you, but I’ll give a few examples here that might help with this decision.
			

Let’s say you are looking at buying an e-commerce company doing a couple hundred thousand in revenue annually. An LLC structure might be sufficient. But if you’re planning to grow the company and sell it for several million dollars, a C Corp structure would make more sense. You pay a little more in taxes for a C Corp than for an LLC, but with the C Corp you can use the QSBS (qualified small business stock) exemption, which lets you take no capital gains on a C Corp structure that you sell.
			

By the same token, if you buy a laundromat and you’re never going to sell it for millions, a C Corp structure is overkill, and an LLC may make more sense. Repetitions at this game of business will steer you well eventually, but perhaps not yet. In the meantime, pay the pros.
			

There are a couple of key things I want you to talk to your attorney about. The first is asset purchase versus stock purchase. An asset purchase involves buying only the assets of the business (like equipment, inventory, and real estate), whereas a stock purchase involves buying the entire company, including all its assets and liabilities. The most common I’ve seen in small Main Street businesses are asset purchases, due to wanting to limit liability and just buy the assets, not intellectual property (IP). That said, there are many reasons why companies may want to do a stock purchase instead, such as if the IP and name is needed historically for government contracts, or if you need the credit history of the business. Talk to your attorney. It’s not overly complicated but it is decided on a case-by-case basis.
			

Next is the allocation of the purchase price, which is a process in which both parties agree on how to allocate the purchase price among different assets. These typically fall into four categories:
			
				
- 
					

Furniture, fixtures, and equipment (FF&E): Office supplies, trucks, computers, and more.
				
				
- 
					

Inventory: Products and goods on hand to sell.
				
				
- 
					

Noncompete agreement: For tax reasons, buyers normally want some of the purchase price allocated to a noncompete to help bolster its enforceability. There is value in not having the seller compete with you.
				
				
- 
					

Goodwill: This is an intangible asset that accounts for the excess purchase price of another company. Items included in goodwill are proprietary or intellectual property and brand recognition, which are not easily quantifiable.
				
			
			

Essentially, what you need to know right now is if you buy a business for $600,000 you can be taxed very differently depending on how you allocate it. So just keep that in your back pocket for when you close.
			
				
					
					
					
					
				
				
					
						
							

Example: $600,000 Purchase Price
						
					
					
						
							

Item
						
						
							

Tax Treatment for the Buyer
						
						
							

Allocation Option 1
						
						
							

Allocation Option 2
						
					
					
						
							

FF&E (furniture, fixtures, and equipment)
						
						
							

Depreciated
						
						
							

$500,000
						
						
							

$200,000
						
					
					
						
							

Inventory (goods on hand)
						
						
							

Deducted as COGS
						
						
							

$50,000
						
						
							

$50,000
						
					
					
						
							

Noncompete agreement
						
						
							

Ordinary expense
						
						
							

$5,000
						
						
							

$5,000
						
					
					
						
							

Goodwill (brand and anything in excess of the above in purchase price)
						
						
							

Amortized over 15 years
						
						
							

$45,000
						
						
							

$345,000
						
					
				
			
			

There are two things an attorney can’t tell you: whether the deal is good, and whether your partner is gonna work out. Only you can answer those questions. All I can tell you is forgoing a business partnership agreement comes at your own peril. This next section will tell you why.
			

## So, You Want a Business Partner…

			

Your most expensive mistake will not be an action taken, it will be a human chosen. And one of the biggest mistakes I see first-time business buyers make is partnering with whoever is in front of them.
			

But it’ll be more fun! I won’t be alone! They’re one of my most talented friends! They bring so much to the table! This will allow us to get rich together!
			

Maybe, but probably not. When it comes to partners, I like to reference a rather off-color article by Ben Franklin. It is called “Advice to a Young Man on the Choice of a Mistress,” written in 1745. While I’m not in the business of needing a mistress, if you substitute the word “partner” for “mistress,” Franklin is rather sage. He writes that if you must have a mistress, you should pick an older one. Why? Because “they have more Knowledge of the World and their Minds are better stor’d with Observations.” Which is prescient. And perhaps my favorite reason: “They are so grateful!!”
			

Buffett and Munger often reference these two things about their partnership. One of them with more experience, and both immensely grateful. If I leave you with no other advice, experience and gratitude would likely be a good place to start. When it comes to partners, I prefer that they not learn on my dime and that I smile when I see them.
			

Think of it this way: Even when people marry their dream partner, they still have a 50 percent chance of failure. A business partnership is similar but without all the fun of makeup sex and the glue of mini humans. It is a commitment to another imperfect person through difficulty, disaster, and dullness. This is not for the faint of heart and not to be taken lightly.
			

My advice has largely become: Don’t partner on your first business. Don’t give away your equity. Yes, a good partnership can be powerful. But are you so sure you know what good looks like? Do one-off projects, work alongside others, but be careful whom you marry. Just because you can doesn’t mean you should.
			

You want to win with another? Prepare for what winning looks like. Late nights. Frivolous lawsuits. Money stolen. Banks withholding your pay. Missed date nights. Firing employees. Hard choices. There is no other way, there never was, and most humans cannot take it. I wish someone had told me that your success will likely be driven by your partners, and that bad ones look very different from good ones.
			

## Bad partners:

			
				
- 
					

History of losses
				
				
- 
					

No long-term partners
				
				
- 
					

Need you to win, while you don’t need them
				
				
- 
					

Say they do “strategy”
				
			
			

## Good partners:

			
				
- 
					

History of winning
				
				
- 
					

Long-term games and friends
				
				
- 
					

Will win regardless, but choose you
				
				
- 
					

Say they do whatever is necessary
				
			
			

When I first started, I didn’t have a lot of rules for business partners. I confused friendship with partnership. This was a mistake I wish upon no one. I’ve lost count of the money I’ve spent learning lessons on choosing the right people (for me). I do know that I’ve cut a $1 million check, I’ve spent multiple six figures on attorneys (plus a year of my life), and I’ve lost friendships because I wasn’t willing to have hard conversations up front. Fear up front meant payment later. I’ve come to believe that your success is a direct result of the number of difficult conversations you are willing to have. Now I ask myself a few questions and do a little hard-hitting homework before I ever do a deal with another human. Here are my rules for having a partner in an acquisition:
			
				
- 
					

 Trust but verify: Run a complete background check. Chat with ten others who have done business with them. Yes, ten. Review their business’s reviews and BBB files. If they’ve ever raised capital, talk to their previous investors. Humans leave trails.
				
				
- 
					

Start with the end in mind: Imagine you will get a divorce. How would that change things for you today?
				
				
- 
					

No fifty-fifty partnerships ever: At least to begin with. Someone needs to be in charge.
				
				
- 
					

No one gets equity on Day 1 for future potential work: You either put in money or wait and vest over time.
				
				
- 
					

Always do vesting cliffs for partners: If you want to give away 20 percent to your operator, give them 5 percent the first year, 5 percent the next year, and so on. (Typically my operators get 10 percent as CEOs of the business, and that can go up as they provide value.)
				
				
- 
					

Take-backs in business are okay: Add clawback clauses for work or milestones. If the operator of a million-dollar business does poorly and only makes $200,000, ensure that they don’t get their equity.
				
				
- 
					

 Set the price now, or pay the price later: Add in triggers. If they want to leave, you get to buy their equity at a set valuation or multiple.
				
				
- 
					

If it’s not written, it didn’t happen: Add an operating agreement and an employment agreement for your operator with very specific expectations and triggers if they don’t hit benchmarks.
				
				
- 
					

Beware of shadow influences: Protect yourself from anyone who could get in the middle, such as your operator’s partners or family members.
				
				
- 
					

Make your pro the bad (but smart) guy: Attorneys review all contracts prior to doing the deal.
				
			
			

I wish I could tell you this will solve all your potential problems, but it won’t. You can’t do a good deal with a bad guy. So proceed on partnerships with caution.
			

A few final words of wisdom on partnerships:
			
				
- 
					

Partnerships are easy to get into and hard to get out of.
				
				
- 
					

People change; expect it. Expect people to turn into the worst versions of themselves when money’s involved. For many, money creates monsters.
				
				
- 
					

Incentives drive actions. If they earn their money regardless of their performance, expect them to do less.
				
				
- 
					

When people show you who they are, believe them. One partner I had YELLED at two young and inexperienced designers who pitched us, bringing one to tears. Assume you will one day be the one they turn their worst on. He sure proved that right.
				
				
- 
					

Most people won’t want it as bad as you. They won’t be willing to put in what it takes. Set the agreement accordingly.
				
			
			

Let’s prepare for the shift from diligence to dealmaking.
			

Think of it as the calm before the storm—a moment to breathe before you officially become a business owner. Because what comes next is the pivot point of your journey toward financial freedom: negotiating the deal.
			

In the following section, you’ll step into the ring, equipped with tips I’ve picked up (along with many scars) on how to negotiate and close the deal.
			

## How to Make Your First Business Deal a Slam Dunk

			

Most things in life come with instructions, safety measures, and guardrails. Dealmaking has none of that.
			

Think of closing a real estate deal, where you go into a title office and sign a towering stack of documents without reading any of them. You just sign, sign, sign, initial, initial, initial. The industry is so standardized you sign with blind faith and with no concern for what lurks in the pages.
			

When you’re buying a company, there are no bumpers. There is no normalized structure. There are no standard terms. The two parties can go back and forth, making any changes they want to those legal documents. This can be done in person, over the phone, through email—over and over, through the end of time.
			

What does that mean for you? It means your dealmaking process can either go really well, or it can completely fall apart. No one wants the latter, but in a world where there are no rules, the ones you don’t even know about are the ones that will get ya.
			

I’m going to lay out the dealmaking and negotiation principles I’ve learned over the years. I wish I could take credit for all of these, but you’re going to learn from some of the giants who helped me not absolutely lose my shirt and make a bit of money along the way.
			

## Tip 1: Pros Control Terms

			

My friend David Osborn can count his wealth in the nine figures. He’s a no-nonsense man who, before our very first meeting, gave me a sixteen-page personality test to fill out. Despite all that, I love the guy. Few people these days tell you things you don’t want to hear and why you should believe them. In that vein, I’ll never forget when, on the first of our many walks, he told me a truth I’ve come to use too many times to count. I was negotiating an eight-figure deal and, candidly, royally screwing it up. The deal had become a monster—crazy fighting on the price, me combating every which way to lower it, an entrenched counterparty. But David told me this line:
			
				

You can have my price and your terms, or my terms and your price…but you can’t have both.
			
			

Then he told me I was being rather like a stubborn donkey fixating on price. Control the terms and you actually control the price. He used an example: “Codie, let’s say you have a business that does one million dollars a year and I ask you to buy that business for one billion. You’d probably say yes, right? Well, what if after you sign, I tell you that I’ll pay a dollar a day until I get to a billion. Does that change your mind? Sure should. That is the power of terms.”
			

There are only two notes in any negotiation: price and terms. There are countless ways to arrange those notes, but they always belong to one of two categories. Play the chords wisely. Pros control the terms.
			

Here’s a good example of using terms in one’s favor. A friend of mine was negotiating to buy a business that made $200,000 net profit annually. He wanted to pay 2X ($400,000) for the business, but the owner wanted 4X ($800,000).
			

To bring the two sides closer, my friend proposed some milestones:
			
				
- 
					

He would pay the 4X valuation, provided the business made enough each quarter of that first year to keep it on pace to hit that $200,000 profit.
				
				
- 
					

If it went above that $50,000-per-quarter milestone, then the price would go up.
				
				
- 
					

So, for instance, if the company only made $40,000 in the first quarter, the price my friend paid would fall by X amount. Conversely, if the business made $60,000, the price would increase by Y amount.
				
			
			

Spoiler: nine times out of ten the business will do less than the seller thinks. So in this case the price of $800,000 for above $200,000 in profit wasn’t actually hit. The final purchase price is more like $450,000 for slight outperformance. Win-win. Control the terms and you control the deal.
			

## Tip 2: Be Likable

			

Sometimes the key to good negotiating is donuts. Never underestimate the power of a sugary treat, a strong cup of joe, and zero suits, spreadsheets, or numbers in the vicinity.
			

One of my deal partners likes to say, “Being likable beats being good nine times out of ten.” He’s right. We were supposed to pay a guy $70,000 down for a business we were buying, and I accidentally sent the guy only $60,000. My partner told me about six months later that we still owed him $10,000. I was red from my cheeks to my feet. How embarrassing! His response, “This is why you make sure they like you.” We paid the owner but he never even raised a peep. The power of affinity.
			

In a society of self-inflated asshats who come in swinging to negotiations, being likable is pretty simple. Just be honest, set realistic expectations, and show respect for the seller. And when you can, treat ’em to donuts and a thank-you card. In the deluge of texts and emails, a handshake and a pen go a long way. Warren Buffett knew this so well that his thank-you notes have become legendary—a bit tongue-in-cheek, overly transparent, and always handwritten.
			

## Tip 3: Be Patient

			

Those who can wait, win. There’s a famous story of Wall Street negotiators who would pull the competing negotiation team into their office conference rooms, give them plenty of water, coffee, and other beverages, and then, when they needed to use the bathroom, apologize that the bathroom is “sadly” under construction. Since leaving the deal table meant blowing up the deal, the parched team had all the power. The longer the water guzzlers had to wait, the more anxious they became, until they finally signed a deal and, despite the hard terms, smiled in relief. Not my style, but the sentiment carries. My mentor used to say, “Model the Buddha: I can think. I can wait. I can fast.” Who would have thought the Buddha would have a place in negotiations? But he’s right.
			

One of the best deals I ever did was with a team that wanted it done so badly they would call and email me constantly. I was getting antsy and thought we were going to lose the deal since they were pushing so hard. My attorney, Todd Phillips, told me to call him and said simply, “The lady doth protest too much, methinks.” A famous line from Hamlet where the Player Queen’s protestations of love and fidelity are too excessive to be believed. What was he saying? They’re bluffing. Calm down, pretend to not be rattled. Loud ones aren’t usually the issue; be wary of the quiet ones, they are usually the adversaries to beware of.
			

## Tip 4: Flinches and Questions

			

Sometimes it’s not what you say, it’s how you say it. A little game I like to play is when the seller names a price that’s higher than I want, I simply wince. Without your saying a word, the seller is now compelled to explain how they arrived at their price. That gives you vital information and puts them on the defensive. Sometimes the seller will quickly start negotiating against themselves and lower their price. If that doesn’t happen I like to revert back to what the deal is worth and use the old Chris Voss–ism, “How am I supposed to do that?” It’s a no, but it pushes on the problem, not the person.
			

To get technical: When it comes to price, you have a few additional, smaller levers you can use. If the seller wants an inflated price, let them know that the bank won’t loan you that kind of money for a business at their profit level. Or come in with recent sales of similar businesses. Shift the blame to market forces that are outside your control as you make your case for a lower price. But never underestimate a flinch and a question.
			

## Tip 5: Meet Them at Home

			

Many people hold Warren Buffett as their favorite investor. I, however, hold a special place in my heart for Sam Zell. Zell was a real estate magnate who earned a multibillion-dollar fortune and a reputation as “the grave dancer” for his ability to bring companies and properties back from the dead, but not before buying them for pennies on the dollar. Zell wrote this unforgettable piece of advice:
			
				

Today I could probably get just about anybody to come to my office for a meeting, but that wouldn’t tell me much. Instead, I spend over a thousand hours a year on my plane traveling around the world to meet with people. I want to see what they are like on their home court, how they treat their people and the examples they set.
			
			

Most people want to do deals behind the comfort of their keyboard. I want to shake hands and meet them in their warehouse. Show me, don’t tell me.
			

## Tip 6: Be Ready to Walk

			

Never fall in love with something that can’t love you back. The kiss of death is getting too far down the road on a deal and being struck with a healthy dose of sunk-cost fallacy. This means that because you spent all this time and cash on a deal, you think you still have to do the deal, even if you’re nicked with tiny paper cuts (bad terms and details) that’ll bleed you out. Know what your walkaway number is and stick to it. Remember, in the small-business arena, there are a LOT of deals available. Hours into a deal do not equal hours you’ll get out of a deal. Sam Zell liked to reference the old Peter Drucker quote, “We suffer from knowing the numbers.” He meant that no matter what emotions or beliefs come along, we look back to the math. When emotions soar, intelligence plummets. Trust in the numbers. When the math doesn’t math…walk.
			

A line remembered can sometimes be a dollar saved. Here are truisms I wish I would have known sooner in doing deals:
			
				
- 
					

He who wants it least wins.
				
				
- 
					

Choices. Give yourself options.
				
				
- 
					

Mirror back what the seller is saying. Label and summarize.
				
				
- 
					

Negotiations don’t happen between nine and five o’clock.[*2]
				
				
- 
					

Nothing is good or bad, except by comparison.
				
				
- 
					

If it’s not in writing, it never happened.
				
				
- 
					

Follow-up questions are your secret weapon.
				
				
- 
					

He/she who talks most loses.
				
				
- 
					

He/she who asks the most wins.
				
			
			

Negotiation is an art, not a science. It takes time to become a skilled artist.
			

## Closing the Deal

			

Price and terms do not make a deal locked. The magic and the mayhem in doing deals come from the devil in their details. I can’t take you through every if-then statement you will come across, but I can tell you a couple of ways things start to get wonky.
			

## Valuation Segmentation

			

When you’re structuring the documents, you want to pay close attention to the price, of course, but also to what is associated with that cost. How much of the price is driven by the equipment and how much of it is driven by the goodwill you’re buying? The seller is going to want to assign as much as they can to goodwill because the higher that is, the less they’ll pay in taxes.
			

But that is not a good deal for the buyer. The buyer wants as much assigned to equipment as possible. You do not want to be buying a lot of goodwill, unless you love paying the IRS. You can accelerate the depreciation of equipment, but you have to amortize your tax write-off on goodwill, and that takes longer. Big words that mean: get with your accountant before you close. But sellers might (read: will) try to sneak a high level of goodwill into the deal if they prepare the docs.
			

We politely pass on that. This is an example of the sellers saying, “Okay, we agreed on terms, and we agreed on price!” and then they sneak in their number for goodwill in an attempt to lower their taxes at your expense. Nice try.
			

## NDAs, Noncompetes, and Guarantees

			

There are other red flags you should watch out for. Most deals have noncompete clauses that prevent the seller from immediately opening a new business in direct competition with you. But states have different noncompete allowances, and you and your attorney have to be aware of those. For instance, California allows a seller to compete a lot sooner than other states, and if you put in a fifty-year noncompete clause, the state might revoke your entire agreement.
			

You also want to make sure to add clauses that free you from historical liability. For instance, if a customer sues the laundromat for an incident that occurred before you became owner, you want to ensure that you have no liability. You don’t want liability for outstanding lawsuits the seller didn’t tell you about and that didn’t come up during your due diligence.
			

In general, though, keep an eye on the main terms in the purchase agreement:
			
				
- 
					

Is the asset purchase list complete?
				
				
- 
					

Is the inventory list complete?
				
				
- 
					

Do you need a non-solicit clause that prevents the seller from stealing your staff to work in their next venture?
				
				
- 
					

Do you have contingencies for when X, Y, or Z that were supposed to happen didn’t happen?
				
			
			
			• • •
			

Some moments are worth remembering. This is one of them. I remember the first time I signed my name onto a document as an owner. It was both terrifying and exhilarating. No one else to blame, no one else in charge. Just me. Yet, having skin in the game is something every human should try at one point. What else is the point of life except to push your capacity to its limit? Now you will do what most won’t. Because you know: Acquisitions are hard. Start-ups are hard. Employment is hard. So, choose YOUR hard.
			

Today, you could officially be at the cusp of ownership. There is magic in that moment—savor it.
			
				

Skip Notes
				
					

*1 The results mentioned above were achieved by people who hustled using the methods taught in this book and in the community. Not every participant gets these exceptional results. Business success varies significantly depending on individual expertise, effort, market conditions, and good ol’ fashioned luck. This method involves risks, just like anything else, and not everyone will succeed.
				
				
					

*2 Deals often get done over late-night emails, weekend meetups, and off-the-clock conversations. Be ready to move whenever the opportunity strikes.

---

Step 3: C Is for Command, Main Street Millionaire
		
		
		

	
	
		
			
			

##

---

Hiring an Operator: The Key Player of Your Business, Main Street Millionaire
		
		
		

	
	
		
			

## Hiring an Operator: The Key Player of Your Business

			

At this stage of the game, the most common question I get from new owners is this:
			

After I buy the business, how do I manage the damn thing?
			

If this is how you’re feeling, don’t worry. Most new owners don’t know how to run a new business they’ve acquired. And they can’t fathom how entrepreneurs like me oversee anywhere from ten to thirty cash-flowing businesses at a time.
			

The secret rule of ownership is to hire a team that runs your entire business better than you. In business, unlike sports, we don’t perform to the level we practice, we perform to the level of those we hire. Managing multiple businesses ain’t easy, so please start with one. As a beginner, it will feel like an oddly fun mixture of bull riding meets tequila shot drinking.
			

Here, the captain and all-star of your team is your operator. The one who runs the plays day to day while you fund it and find your next deal. Before you get all excited, with dreams of others doing your work and growing your bank accounts while you lounge poolside, let’s talk about the tale of two operators…
			
				
			
			

## Tale 1: The Dark Side

			

The keys to the business were ours, finally. We had just bought a twelve-year-old business that would profit each of us a few hundred thousand dollars a year. Or so I thought.
			

My operator found me the business and promised to run it as well. Perfect, right? Months pass and the business is growing slightly slower than we thought, but hey, that happens. We’ve asked for a few updates, we’ve had a slight push and pull on the business, but we give the operator a lot of rope. It’s his first business takeover with me, and I love him. He’s basically family.
			

Then one night, on our annual family summer vacation, I get an odd email from my accountant. Turns out our operator was on vacation too. The accountant had started running the numbers in his stead. Small problem: the math was not mathing. After several late-night phone calls (which the operator wouldn’t pick up) and many more texts (left firmly on unread), we realize, Holy hell, we’ve been taken for a ride.
			

Our little business was running on fumes, likely funding our operator’s European vacation from the profits of a business that was apparently far from profitable. It took long sleepless nights, months of work, and lost friendships to get the business back from him and turn it around. Turns out, you should always use the word “family” carefully.
			

## Tale 2: The Bright Side

			

What does a good operator look like? You have conversations like this:
			

“Chris, what is this deposit for $14,850?” I ask while checking one of our bank accounts.
			

“Oh, that’s PortCo payout. Hunter just sent it through,” he replies.
			

Mailbox money. ’Tis possible. Hunter runs one of our portfolio companies. He works hard, treats the business like it’s his, does what he says he is going to do, is BETTER at running this business than we are, and has fully aligned incentives. This is the power of a good operator. You do what you promise, they do what they promise, and the checks get deposited while you’re living life.
			

Is it always perfect? Sadly, no. Does he make mistakes? He’d be the first to admit, yes. But once you have worked with pros, you’ll never allow for another amateur.
			

Many want you to believe that business ownership and operations is impossibly difficult. You, the average person, can never do it. How dare I simplify, and how dare you try. My response to them is always, “Okay, if it is so hard, why didn’t you go back to being an employee?” At which point they all chuckle. Well, yes, it is better than that.
			

In this chapter, I’m going to give you a framework that’s allowed me to own my businesses, instead of getting owned by them. (Spoiler: the key is hiring a great operator.)
			

## Do You Really Need an Operator?

			

Short answer, no. I have bought many businesses to replace a job, then ran it myself to start. Long answer, it’s a heck of a lot more fun than doing it all yourself. Consider my own experience with a laundromat. This might shock you, but I do not know how to fix a washing machine. My husband doesn’t even allow me to wash our own clothes for fear of my ineptitude with our residential machine. So you can imagine, the first day I walked in as the owner, I would’ve rather swum with sharks than be tasked with laundry operations. Every task—from mixing soap for large wash batches to opening coin collectors—was foreign to me. Operating this simple, boring business required skills and knowledge I didn’t have. I was an embarrassment.
			

Thankfully, I had a guy who brought the expertise needed to keep the business running smoothly. His role was essential.
			

This brings us to the crux of the owner-operator relationship. Owning a business where your daily involvement is minimal can feel…strange. You might wonder if you’re even necessary to the whole equation. Before you ask, the answer is YES. Your operator often relies on you as much as you depend on them. This is a symbiotic relationship, where your investment and their expertise converge. Together, you create a pathway to success for both parties.
			

In my case, hiring an operator was mission critical. The business became a well-oiled machine under his daily supervision, which allowed me to find more deals, add them on, finance them, and build toward our future growth. We brought different skills to the table but worked together harmoniously to create more profits. That’s the name of the game.
			

## How Much Should You Pay an Operator?

			

Follow my “Six Figures to Thee & Me” rule. Your business needs to make enough in profits to pay six-figure salaries to both you and your operator. I won’t buy a business unless it has $200,000 in annual profits. That’s because I want to make at least $100,000 (six figures) and pay my operator $100,000 (six figures). Why? The three most important words in investing are margin of safety. Buffett said it best: “Margin of safety, which means, don’t try and drive a 9,800-pound truck over a bridge that says ‘Capacity: 10,000 pounds.’ But go down the road a little bit and find one that says ‘Capacity: 15,000 pounds.’ ”
			
				
					

Six Figures to Thee & Me Rule
				
				
					
				
			
			

My rule is always be generous in paying those who make your life easier. Coups start with shallow pockets. I’d rather pay myself less than my operator to start with. Let’s say you’re happy with making $50,000. You buy a local auto-repair shop, with annual profits of $120,000. According to Glassdoor, a fair salary for an experienced auto-repair operator is $60,000. That means you have $60,000 left over for your annual salary. Nice! Just one small issue: if ANYTHING goes wrong in the business, you will either need to pay yourself less or find a cheaper operator. Margin of safety. Magic words.
			

If you don’t want to run the business yourself, you need profits. Those profits will let you hire a quality operator while still reaching your financial goals. How do butchers stay fat? They keep a little meat on the bone for droughts. Same goes for investing.
			

Money is just one carrot to dangle. If you’re hiring top talent, it should not be the only carrot you offer. Here are my top three options to make an operator’s compensation package more attractive and aligned with you and the business:
			
				
- 
					

Performance bonuses: Offer bonus incentives based on the business hitting certain milestones or key performance indicators (KPIs). For example, if they can increase revenue by $100,000 within the fiscal year, they get a 5 percent bonus (i.e., $5,000). The operator’s performance is now tied to the business’s success. They have skin in the game. Nothing aligns behavior better than that.
				
				
- 
					

Equity: Consider offering an equity earn-in or buy-in option. Maybe after one year of meeting or exceeding set KPIs, they earn a 2 percent equity stake. Or they could be awarded a 5 percent equity stake if they hit 20 percent year-over-year growth for three consecutive years. This method can be particularly effective with proven talent. It aligns their long-term interests with the health and growth of the business.
				
				
- 
					

Longevity payouts: Craft your compensation plan to reward both longevity and performance. For instance, set different payout tiers for three-, five-, and ten-year milestones. This motivates your operator to not only stick around but consistently perform at their best.
				
			
			
				
					

People Work for You:
				
				
					
				
			
			

Naval Ravikant said it best: Play long-term games with long-term people. This is the key to success. But success can be engineered with win-win scenarios. Your operator is incentivized to grow your business, all while creating a culture of ownership and accountability. Their success is your success, and vice versa.
			

## Don’t Chase Talent, Draw Them to You

			
				
			
			

The problem with most job listings is that they’re starting a love letter with “To Whom It May Concern.” Amateurs. You are not going to make that mistake. You are going to serenade your people.
			

The best job listing of all time is often attributed to Sir Ernest Shackleton, and it supposedly read like this: Men wanted for hazardous journey. Low wages, bitter cold, long hours of complete darkness. Safe return doubtful. Honour and recognition in case of success.
			

Your job listing should read like an advertisement for a thrilling opportunity. Those who want safe harbor will stick to cubicles and 3 percent annual raises. Give yourself permission to be creative and go beyond a list of responsibilities. Like my friend Shaan Puri, who posted this job listing for an intern on Twitter:
			
				

Internship Contract
				

I’ll pay you, but this won’t make you rich.
				

It will be hard, and you will learn a lot.
				

Sometimes I’ll forget that you’re young and have never done this before.
				

I’ll ask you what you think. Not as a pop quiz. Just to know.
				

If I say “can you do that?” Just say yes & figure out how to do it later.
				

I’ll invest in you as much as you’re investing in yourself.
				

I’ll push you into the pool, but I won’t let you drown.
				

I will be brutally honest with you. I may be the first to do so.
				

You will get 3 years of experience in 1 year.
				

I won’t give you any work that I haven’t done myself.
				

You will end up in meetings you don’t belong in, so that one day you belong in them.
				

I’ll judge you on your results, not your age.
				

I don’t drink coffee, so you’ll never have to make me one.
				

Sound good? Let’s do it.
				

-Shaan
				

P.S. If I forget to do one of these things, remind me.
			
			

## Seven Tips to Write a Job Listing That Attracts Amazing People

			

Here are my golden rules for attracting talent so good they’ll make you question whether you should, in fact, work for them instead.
			
				
- 
					

 Write like you talk: I once hung out with a guy who spoke like a Macy’s ad: monotone, bizarre sentences like “for a limited time only” and “we look forward to your presence.” It was the opposite of fun. It felt like being too close to your weird, pale uncle. When you write, write like you’re talking to a smart, curious, cool friend. You kind of want to impress them, but if you used a bunch of big words, they’d think you were a snob. Less important to sound sexy here, more important to be honest.
				
				
- 
					

Role title: Use something that is SEO-friendly. Don’t use any “Ninja/Unicorn/Badass” whatever-type-of-operator title. No one is searching for that.
				
				
- 
					

Role intro: Draft a compelling introduction to the role. Remember that you are basically marketing the company, and this position, to the world. What would catch this person’s attention? What pain have they experienced before in relation to their work? What have been their frustrations with the role? How can we help solve that?
				
				
- 
					

Position overview: Here’s your general overview of the role and responsibilities. The more specialized the required skill set, the more specific you should be.
				
				
- 
					

Company overview: Explain what your company does (aka how you make money). Bonus if you can include a founder story.
				
				
- 
					

Company mission: Tell them what the long-term vision is for the company. Leave them feeling inspired and wanting to join you on the journey.
				
				
- 
					

Logistics:
					
						
- 
							

Where it’s located
						
						
- 
							

Salary (job postings with salary ranges are significantly more likely to get applications than ones that don’t offer a range—just be transparent)
						
						
- 
							

Benefits
						
						
- 
							

Instructions on how to apply for the job
						
					
			
			

Here’s a real job listing we used to find and attract an operator.
			
				

Title: GM/Operator of Contrarian Thinking Capital Portfolio
				

Contrarian Thinking is seeking a motivated GM/Operator to build and run the next generation of business acquisition. This person will build the team in underwriting, investing, and managing new investments. This is not your grandparents’ holding company, we’re using media and money to revolutionize acquisitions.
				

The ideal candidate for this role is intellectually curious; able to analyze complex data to identify risks and opportunities; has the capacity to work in a fast-paced, collaborative team environment; and is driven to help improve and innovate the team’s approach to investing. Responsibilities include performing due diligence analysis of potential investment opportunities and assisting with day-to-day monitoring and reporting of the investment portfolio. The catch here is this is nothing like your traditional private equity companies. We use seller financing heavily, we do deals in the micro SMB space, we use our audience as a magnet for deal flow and investment, and we have a focus on using as little capital as possible up front to do deals.
				

Specific Responsibilities:
				
					
- 
						

Provide hands-on leadership for all aspects of investment activity—from origination to exit—with the goal of growing the portfolio into a significant asset base and high-quality opportunities
					
					
- 
						

Develop and execute an acquisition strategy that drives revenue and growth
					
					
- 
						

Source proprietary investment opportunities and create an investment deal flow pipeline
					
					
- 
						

Validate the business opportunity, establish an investment case, and structure and negotiate terms for potential investments
					
					
- 
						

Develop a complete understanding of an emerging company by evaluating its market opportunity, competition, product, sales and growth, operations, financial statements and projections, management strength, equity needs, and capital structure
					
					
- 
						

Work with and support portfolio companies in tandem with the Head of Portfolio Management, including attending board meetings and monitoring performance to ensure returns and strategic objectives are achieved
					
					
- 
						

Analyze and report on portfolio performance, and make recommendations for future deals
					
					
- 
						

Due diligence includes but is not limited to: reviewing detailed company information, conducting in-depth industry analysis, completing reference checks, running debt models, and performing valuation analysis (comparable and DCF) in order to evaluate the overall attractiveness of a transaction
					
				
				

Qualifications:
				
					
-
