Minimum of 10 years of experience including significant experience at a top-tier venture capital firm or major corporation venture capital group, with a deep understanding of the small-business acquisition space
					
					
- 
						

Proven experience from sourcing to exiting investments, including deal analysis, due diligence, reviewing and negotiating all documentation, serving as deal interface, and driving deal processes to completion
					
					
- 
						

Highly developed analytical skills with ability to define issues, collect and analyze pertinent data, identify alternative solutions, project consequences of proposed actions, draw valid conclusions, propose sound recommendations, and implement approved solutions in support of specified goals; attention to detail is critical
					
				
				

About Contrarian Thinking:
				

Contrarian Thinking is a digital media company, education business, and an investment firm. We are one of the fastest-growing business-focused media companies with 100 million views a month and 5 million social followers. We have a holding company of businesses that comprise our enterprise and are launching several new channels and businesses this year. We are led by Codie Sanchez as a creator-driven company.
				

The Perks—Why Work on Our Team:
				
					
- 
						

Competitive salary
					
					
- 
						

Flexible vacation policy and paid company holidays
					
					
- 
						

Company-provided technology package
					
					
- 
						

Relocation for those coming in from outside the area including travel and company-provided housing for the first 30 days
					
				
			
			

## Beyond the Listing: Six More Ways to Find Potential Operators

			

Not every great operator will be found through a job listing. You’ll need to actively seek them out and ask around. Over the years, I’ve honed several methods to attract what I call “10X hires.” Here they are:
			
				
- 
					

 Poach proven talent: Let’s say you’re buying a car wash. Start getting to know, visiting, and calling all the top competitors in your area. The best talent already does this job, but for someone else. Don’t sleep on proven talent working at other businesses.
				
				
- 
					

Hire Ex-military: Former military members often make great operators. They’re disciplined, they establish hierarchy, and they can make decisions under pressure. Programs for veterans are a fantastic place to scout for potential operators. Most of the veteran hires I’ve made were through friends, or the SEAL Future Foundation, Goldman Sachs Veterans Integration Program, and the Veteran Career Transition Assistance Program (VetCTAP). You can also get the word out on veteran job boards, such as Hire Heroes USA (hireheroesusa.org), VetJobs (vetjobs.org), and Recruit Military (recruitmilitary.com). I may be biased (I’m married to one), but I believe veterans are far more capable and deserving of ownership than most.
				
				
- 
					

Use Recruiters: Imagine you could pick up the phone and within fifteen minutes call every top car wash operator in your local area, have them answer your call, and know you by name. That’s what recruiters have—well, at least the good ones. These people do this for a living for a reason. Don’t be short-term greedy and long-term stupid. Whenever I can pay to steal someone’s ten thousand hours, I do so. Google “top recruiters in X industry and X area.” Now borrow their network.
				
				
- 
					

Competency collector: I sniff out competence like a German shepherd on a cocaine trail at the airport. Once you know the scent, you can’t help but jump on it. People say business cards are dead. They’ve never hired operators for blue-collar businesses. Keep a few of your cards on hand. Then every time you see someone go above and beyond, give them your card and ask for theirs. Collect competence—you’ll never know when you could need it next.
				
				
- 
					

The friend flywheel: We all occasionally get that person who asks us if we know anyone for XYZ position. Be the person who takes ten minutes each time someone asks to try to connect them to someone. Small actions of giving ripple over time into big waves. Be the friend people go to for talent and they’ll be the same for you.
				
				
- 
					

Social media = hiring superhighway: Your social media is a walking billboard. Use it as a hiring funnel. When you build in public, your billboard stands on people’s commutes for free. Done right, this attracts competent candidates who are more likely to understand your mission and believe in it. I’ve spent years building up a big social following, and because of that, one job posting leads to hundreds or thousands of potential hires. Recently, we received over five hundred applications for an operator job listing. Those applications came from my social channels alone.
				
			
			

The amount of money you make will always be limited by the level of talent you can attract. Don’t shortchange yourself. Do whatever it takes to find a great operator.
			

## How to Short-List the Best Candidates

			

The next step is to not suck at picking the right operator. We all have that friend who cannot help but date the wrong person. They get their heart broken and stomped on every time. Here’s how we avoid bad operator syndrome. The key is finding someone who:
			
				
- 
					

Has the right moral compass
				
				
- 
					

Has a history of winning
				
				
- 
					

Leads instead of waits
				
				
- 
					

Takes work off your plate
				
				
- 
					

Has team members who will follow them
				
				
- 
					

Is better than you
				
				
- 
					

Is not going to F you
				
			
			

That combination is an individual who will add significant value and leverage through their role in your business. When it comes to selecting these individuals from a pool of candidates, there are three filters I value above all others: Known, Experienced, and Proven Talent.
			
				
- 
					

Known Talent: These are people you already know. I prefer people from my existing network whom I already know, like, and trust. Why? Winston Churchill told a story I will never forget. He was touring a young new Member of Parliament (MP) around the building. He showed him how one party sits on one side of the chamber, the other party on the other side, motioning behind them. The young MP starts pointing at the other side of the chamber, saying enemy this and enemy that. After a moment Churchill stops him and says, “Young man, the opposition is on the other side. The enemy? Why, the enemy is behind you,” as he points to where their party will sit. The point is, your biggest risk is inside your business, not outside. I like knowing the risk I choose.
				
				
- 
					

Experienced Talent: Pick people who have done the role before. This means looking for folks who have managed and grown a similar business. Why let them learn on your dime when you can find someone who already knows how to turn the wrench (and can tell you where the bodies will be buried).
				
				
- 
					

Proven Talent: The best predictor of future behavior is past behavior. Leadership potential is nonnegotiable, and it leaves warning signs everywhere. These signs include: leading groups, managing significant projects, playing on sports teams, enrolling in the military, or managing previous business ventures. My favorite hack is asking, “If I hire you, who will you want to bring from your past jobs?” Why? If the answer is no one, then were they leading anyone after all? Remember: Proven talent comes with a history of success but at a higher cost. Potential talent is more affordable but requires a major time investment in their training. Choose your own adventure.
				
			
			
				
					

Hiring Filters
				
				
					
				
			
			

## Build Your Candidate Hiring Matrix

			

Here’s the hiring matrix I use for every interview. It’s amazing how many people this screens out. And it lets the likely winners rise to the top.
			
				
					
					
					
					
					
				
				
					
						
							

Known Candidate
						
						
							

Proven Experience
						
						
							

Proven Industry
						
						
							

Proven Problem Set
						
						
							

Proven Scale
						
					
					
						
							

Has someone on our team previously worked with this candidate and is personally willing to vouch for them?
						
						
							

Has the candidate done this exact job before (e.g., hiring a COO to be a COO)?
						
						
							

Has this candidate worked in this exact industry or type of company before?
						
						
							

Has this candidate solved the exact problem we are solving for?
						
						
							

Has this candidate worked at companies of varying sizes and scales, or one close in size?
						
					
				
			
			

It’s like a grading rubric from school. You score candidates 0 to 5 for each category, and they can get between 0 and 25 total. You can edit the categories to be relevant to your company, but these are the five categories I’ve found to be the most important.
			

Here’s what it looks like when it’s been filled out:
			
				
					
					
					
					
					
					
				
				
					
						
						
							

Known Candidate
						
						
							

Proven Experience
						
						
							

Proven Industry
						
						
							

Proven Problem Set
						
						
							

Proven Scale
						
					
				
				
					
						
							

Mary, the Perfect Candidate (24/25)
						
						
							

Codie previously worked with Mary and recommends her. (5/5)
						
						
							

Mary was a head of ops before, and we are hiring a head of ops. (5/5)
						
						
							

Mary worked at a media company, and we are a media company. (5/5)
						
						
							

Mary is being hired as a first ops hire at CT to clean up and install all-new SOPs. She did the same thing at company X. (5/5)
						
						
							

Mary worked at a start-up and scaled it to $50 million (slightly bigger than us but not huge). (4/5)
						
					
					
						
							

Simon, the Average Candidate (17/25)
						
						
							

Simon worked with a friend of Codie’s who recommended him. (3/5)
						
						
							

Simon worked as a head of ops previously. (5/5)
						
						
							

Simon worked at an education company, an adjacent industry. (3/5)
						
						
							

Simon worked as a head of ops but the company was already established. (3/5)
						
						
							

Simon worked at a company doing $50 million already, then grew it. (3/5)
						
					
					
						
							

Pam, the Bad Candidate (1/25)
						
						
							

No one knows the candidate, all cold references. (0/5)
						
						
							

Pam has never done this role before at any level. (0/5)
						
						
							

Pam has never worked in a similar role at a related company, like being a head of ops in a legal firm. (0/5)
						
						
							

Pam maintained existing processes without driving change or taking on a leadership role. (0/5)
						
						
							

She’s only worked at a massive company like Google, or a tiny business like a restaurant. (1/5)
						
					
				
			
			

## How to Interview the Top Operator Candidates

			

You’ve curated a list of the best candidates. The next step is to screen them. Here you will assess the skills and compatibility of your future operator. At my companies, we make sure every candidate gets a high-standard interview. What is a high-standard interview?
			
				
- 
					

It uses the same questions every time.
				
				
- 
					

It follows our process for documenting and sharing your notes with other team members.
				
				
- 
					

It ranks each candidate on the five core components.
				
			
			

TL;DR—don’t wing it. It leads to poor notes from hiring managers and poor experience for candidates.
			

## Top Questions We Use in Operator Interviews

			

We ask questions that directly translate to values we embody at Contrarian. You should do the same.
			
				
- 
					

We value speed of execution. Tell me about one of the last projects you did? What was it? How’d you start? What happened? How do you compress timelines?
					
						
- 
							

In the answer, you’re looking for them to move fast. Look for examples of where they went from start to finish in twenty-four hours.
						
					
				
- 
					

We value good employees. What will past bosses say are your three biggest strengths and weaknesses?
					
						
- 
							

There’s always a real possibility that you WILL call these people. You’re more likely to get an honest answer by mentioning the possibility.
						
					
				
- 
					

We value people who have a history of winning. Let’s walk through your résumé together, starting with your last three jobs. What were your most significant achievements in each role? How did your contributions directly impact the success of your team or company? What challenges did you face, and how did you turn them into wins?
				
				
- 
					

We value experts. What could you give a five-minute presentation on with no prep? (Then hand them a whiteboard and let them do it.)
					
						
- 
							

You learn their thinking process, communication skills, and creativity.
						
					
				
- 
					

We value follow-through; we work the problem. Walk me through your last project. What should have been different?
					
						
- 
							

Do they jump to the blame game? Are they self-aware? Can they implement a feedback loop in their own work?
						
					
				
- 
					

We value leaders. Who would follow you here? What do they do? Who would you absolutely want to hire alongside you? What is your philosophy on managing teams? How do you handle a problem team member?
					
						
- 
							

How are they perceived in their network? Can they self-assess that? This is especially important if you want this person to help build with you.
						
					
				
- 
					

We value Archimedes finding their levers. What strategies or tools did you use to prepare for this interview?
					
						
- 
							

Let them show their work. It reveals how they think.
						
					
				
- 
					

We value a Relentless Obsession with Excellence. What do you think we are doing wrong as a company right now?
					
						
- 
							

Will show you how much they care, what work they’ve done, and if they are ready to hit the ground running.
						
					
			
			Green-Light Answers:
			
				

Detailed descriptions of roles
				

Mentions specific achievements or improvements they brought about
				

Demonstrates a clear understanding of their impact in each role
				

Clear alignment with your company’s values
				

Examples of past behaviors reflecting these values
				

Enthusiasm about your company culture
				

Goals that align with the role and your company’s direction
				

Clear and realistic career plans showing ambition and foresight
			
			Red-Flag Answers:
			
				

Vague descriptions
				

Inability to articulate specific responsibilities or achievements
				

A focus only on negative aspects of past jobs
				

Misalignment or unfamiliarity with your company’s values
				

Responses that seem rehearsed or superficial
				

Goals that are unrelated to the role or your company
				

Vague or absurdly ambitious plans that don’t seem achievable in the role
			
			

## Making It Official: How to Hire Your Operator

			

Once you’ve chosen your candidate, it’s time to make an offer. Craft a compensation package that motivates and aligns interests with success. I like to give two types of offers:
			

Offer 1: Bonuses and Salary. We humans like things nice and simple. So I usually like to give three bonus opportunities, one related to profit, one related to revenue, and one related to people.

Typically I’ll add a profit share for revenue earned above and beyond what is currently being operated on at the business.

Once they’ve accepted your offer, the onboarding process begins!
			

How to succeed at business: Hire smart people. Pay them well. Set them up to win. Then, leave them alone.
			

This is more than just a welcome tour; it’s about integrating your operator directly into the business as efficiently as possible. You can steal our New Hire 100-Day Game Plan if you want to (see the appendix).
			

## The Four Meetings You Need with Your Operator

			

Most problems stem from bad communication. Here are the key meetings you’ll want to immediately get on the schedule to avoid that:
			
				
- 
					

Business Overview: Dive into the history, current standing, and future direction of your business. This sets the stage for their role.
				
				
- 
					

Team Meetings: Set up meetings with any other key team members (group or one-on-one). This helps in kick-starting their relationships and understanding team dynamics.
				
				
- 
					

Weekly Meeting: Set a regular meeting schedule for the two of you to discuss progress, challenges, and strategies. An open communication environment means they can safely share their ideas, concerns, and feedback.
				
				
- 
					

Monthly Performance Reviews: Regularly review their performance against the set goals and KPIs. This helps in maintaining focus and adjusting strategies as needed.
				
			
			

## How to Coach Your Operator in 90 Days

			

You don’t want a liability. You want a partner in crime. This is where your operator transitions from a candidate to a driver of the business. And who is the best person to coach them? The previous operator (i.e., the former owner).
			

This is a big reason why I’ll rarely do a deal unless the owner stays on for a few months after I take over. I need to have future access to them. That way, my operator can learn directly from the person who knows the company best. Here’s how it works:
			
				
- 
					

Days 1–30: Shadow stage. Your new operator follows the former owner (like a shadow), from opening to closing, observing how to run the business. Their focus should be on developing the company’s standard operating procedures. What is the previous owner doing from one moment to the next? Track and write it all down in a notebook. Follow the rule of three: If an action is repeated three times, or involves more than three steps, it goes in the playbook.
				
				
- 
					

Days 31–60: Mimicry stage. Your operator will start doing the work alongside the former owner. They’ll continue documenting and developing the business SOPs. The main focus will be on gaining hands-on experience running the business. If you want to learn, watch—then do.
				
				
- 
					

Days 61–90: Activity stage. Your new operator will perform the business tasks all by themselves. The previous owner will show up part time to oversee their work, answer questions, and point out potential mistakes. You will make sure you like what you see, then finalize the documentation.
				
			
			

By the end of this period, the new operator should (1) feel confident in performing the tasks for their role, and (2) have a documented Operator’s Playbook for the business. Together, you can both refine the playbook so that it covers all SOPs, decision-making processes, and strategies. This will be a living document, so encourage continuous updates and improvements.
			

Whether you’ve hired an experienced operator, or an operator you trust to FITFO (figure it the F out), having an Operator’s Playbook is invaluable. Your operator can use it daily to guide their decisions. It will also be important if they leave, or if you decide to sell the business. You are one good hire from cutting your problems in half. Two good hires from a completely different company.
			

## Tools and Resources

			
				
- 
					

Notion (notion.so): My preferred tool for documenting standard operating procedures (SOPs) and internal company resources. My team and I use Notion every day.
				
				
- 
					

Entrepreneurial Operating System (eosworldwide.com): More than 190,000 companies around the world use EOS tools to get their teams aligned with a singular vision.
				
				
- 
					

How to Buy and Scale a Business for $0 (see the Appendix): We also have a course run by my friend Ayman Al-Abdullah on how to hire, scale, and grow seven-to-nine-figure companies. He should know: he built one from $5 million to $85 million a year in revenue.
				
			
			

## The New Owner’s Guide to Leadership and Culture

			

Can you really run a business? Will you fail? Will you become another statistic on the wall of those who tried and didn’t make it? Every move you make in life comes with a risk. Now we’re going to do everything we can to help you win the long game.
			

At this point, you have a trained operator in place to run your business. The next step is to truly make this business your own. This is where buying a business fundamentally differs from starting one from scratch. You’re stepping into an established business with its own heartbeat and its own way of doing things. You are fast-tracked by coming into something cash-flowing and established. But if you think this is a four-hour workweek on Day 1, you are nuttier than Mr. Peanut. Challenges? You will face many of them. But sometimes in life, hard things are a feature, not a bug.
			

Please just do me one solid: Don’t assume you’re smarter than the previous operator. You’re not. Consider yourself a beautiful little idiot who knows nothing. That’s actually better. You’ll win more battles asking questions, not giving feedback, and documenting up front than you will telling everyone how stupid they’ve been these last ten years in business. Bulls wreck china shops, newbies break businesses. “You’ve got to know it before you grow it.” If you try to grow the company before you’ve got your feet under you, your likelihood of winning decreases.
			

In this section, I’m going to give you the process I’ve used to manage ownership transitions for more than one hundred acquisitions. If you are the one operating your business, use this chapter as your playbook. If you are a passive investor with an operator, give them this playbook. Having a plan before things go sideways solves 99.9 percent of daily issues.
			

## The Takeover Process: How to Go from Handoff to Harness

			

## Days 1–60: Transfer and Transition

			

This period is where you follow, document, note, and insert. Your entire purpose here is largely to learn who does what, where the passwords and keys are, where the bodies are buried, and what you REALLY bought.
			

## Days 60–90: Stabilize and Systematize

			

This period is where you start stabilizing what is broken and wrapping processes around everything in the business. At the end of it, your goal is to have SOPs, dashboards, KPIs, and humans responsible for running them. You are setting the standards.
			

## Days 90–120: Power Up

			

In this stage, we care about three things more than anything: accelerating how fast we get cash in the bank, finding the people inside our business who are most critical, and then beginning to insert small ideas for changes in the business.
			

## 120+ Days: Harness

			

You have a handle on the business, you know where the pots of gold and the monsters that guard them lie. This is your time to start executing on all the notes, ideas, and growth plans you’ve accumulated.
			

A word of warning, based on thousands and thousands of acquisitions I’ve seen from my funds, my holding company, and our Contrarian Community: The next year is the Death Zone. But if you stick to the guidelines in Step 4 (“H is for Harness”), we shall hold hands through the valley of business death and emerge some rich bitches. At least, that’s the plan.
			

## Days 1–60: Transfer and Transition

			

## Your First and Last Words Get Tattooed: Connecting with Your New Team

			

Remember how Maya Angelou said, “People won’t remember what you say, they’ll remember the way you make them feel”? Scratch that in acquisitions. They’ll remember everything.
			

It’s 2023 and there’s a new CEO at the helm of Clearlink, a company in Utah that’s been sold, taken over, resold, and taken over again. The new CEO thought his company had a lot of room for improvement. So he fired up the old Zoom, got his thousand-plus employees to log in, then began a nine-minute tirade so cringeworthy even BuzzFeed picked it up. The CEO started by telling everyone how hard he works, challenging “any one of you to outwork me.” Then he quickly added, “But you won’t.” Brilliant move in empathy. Then he went on to say the company’s employees are quiet-quitting and checking out. But not to worry, one employee “went and sold their family dog” to honor the new requirements. He then added that it “breaks my heart as someone who’s been, you know, at the head of the humanization of pets movement in other businesses that we’ve built.” Everyone is dumbfounded by his tone-deaf speech, including hundreds of thousands of nonemployees on LinkedIn, Reddit, and YouTube, where his Zoom speech has gone viral.
			

Moral of the story: you can do your first meeting very wrong.
			

To start off on the right foot (without shooting it), you’re going to schedule an initial meeting with the entire team and the previous owner. This will be an opportunity to have the owner officially hand off the torch to you and their team. Here’s my two cents: have the owner be good, complimentary, excited, brief, then gone.
			

From there, you and your operator can control and run the rest of the meeting. Keep it as short as possible and have your team members reach out with one-on-one questions afterward. Here are the key points I hit:
			
				
- 
					

Hard work: What kills businesses? Apathy and time. You are bringing fire and vinegar to the business and are excited to help them all achieve big things.
				
				
- 
					

Humility: As the new owner, you’re there to learn. Assure them you have no plans to change anything right away. Then emphasize that you’re eager to grow the business while carrying on its great tradition.
				
				
- 
					

Excitement: Outline why you bought the business, why you’re excited about the future, and where the company is heading. Paint a picture of their roles in it. A little trick is to get to know as many employees as you can in advance and interject their names and roles.
				
				
- 
					

Gratitude: You’re grateful to them for staying on. Each of them has a key role to play, and you’ll be leaning on their skills and knowledge. You’re looking forward to having a free and open dialogue with them. Your door is always open.
				
			
			

Even if you follow the above formula for a great initial meeting, you ultimately don’t have control of how people will emotionally respond. So I want to prepare you for an unlikely yet still possible reaction.
			

Nolan Gore was thrilled. He had just bought a company to add to his landscaping company. As Monday morning’s announcement rolled around, he was equal parts excited and WTF did I just do. Time to call the first meeting with the team.
			

To say the juxtaposition in attitude was a chasm bigger than the Grand Canyon would be an understatement. Nolan and the former owner were thrilled. The new team was not. One of the guys cut him off in his first minute of talking: “I don’t like this. I quit.” Then he stood up and left. Fun. By the end of the day, three more people quit and he had to fire another. Every day for the first two weeks, something went wrong. It was tough.
			

It turns out we humans don’t always love change. Often we go through what’s called S.A.R.A.H. Shock. Anger. Resentment. Acceptance. Hope. Turns out after about two to three paychecks, things settle down. The hopers hope and the angry ones move on.
			

Nolan is now motoring the team firmly in hope. They are feeling good, but just so you know, the chick at the bar isn’t always thrilled when you ask to buy her a drink the first time. Same goes for acquisitions. Don’t let it sting ya. But to figure out which are hopers versus angrys, I like to give them a little assignment. It’s a way for you to show them you are actually here to help, not hurt.
			

Ask each employee to write down their Roadblocks and Rockets.
			
				
- 
					

Roadblocks: What do they need to be more effective in their role? What is holding them back?
				
				
- 
					

Rockets: What would they do, if they were owners, to improve the business? What gets them excited?
				
			
			

Depending on the size of the business, here’s how I do this: I give them one week to submit their Roadblocks and Rockets. Let them know that the reports will be between you, your operator, and them, and that you and/or your team will meet with each employee to review their report. Let them know you expect them to honor your deadline for the report.
			

When you review their reports, take note of great suggestions and important changes that will improve the business. Their reports will reveal who’s optimistic about the future and who’s ready to jump ship.
			

## 30-60-90 Plan: A Plan to Ensure Your Operator’s Success

			

The next step is for your operator to gather all the data. This includes the SOPs, Operator’s Playbook, and employee reports. In the first few days, you become a mixture of a detective and an admin. Do we have correct:
			
				
- 
					

Structuring entity
				
				
- 
					

Bank accounts
				
				
- 
					

Transitioning (transferring all passwords, accounts, etc.)
				
				
- 
					

Data collection and organization
				
				
- 
					

Methods to shadow the operator and key team members
				
			
			

Then you will create a 30-60-90 Plan for their next three months in the business. Simple plans are the best. Your plan will focus on gradual integration, achieving clear goals, and documenting everything. At the end of each thirty-day period, you’ll review your operator’s progress, and then they’ll repeat the cycle again. To get the 30-60-90 Plan as a printable worksheet, go to msmbook.com/tools.
			
				
					

30-60-90 Plan: Days 1–30
					

Focus:
					

Priorities:
				
				
					

Learning Goal 1:
					

Metric:
					

Learning Goal 2:
					

Metric:
				
				
					

Performance Goal 1:
					

Metric:
					

Performance Goal 2:
					

Metric:
					

Performance Goal 3:
					

Metric:
				
			
			

Here is an example of the 30-60-90 Plan filled out:
			
				
					

30-60-90 Plan: Days 1–30
					

Focus: Orientation. Get familiarized with the company’s culture, team, and daily operations.
					

Priority: Learn and absorb as much as possible about the business. Everything I learn, I will write down and document to review and tweak later. Aim to have a big-notion template finished by the end of my first 30 and 90 days.
				
				
					

Learning Goal 1: Understand the company’s key products and services.
					

Metric: Successfully describe the products/services, people, and needs in my own words.
					

Learning Goal 2: Get to know the team.
					

Metric: Number of team meetings and one-on-ones attended.
				
				
					

Performance Goal 1: Efficiently handle basic operational tasks.
					

Metric: Successfully complete assigned tasks with minimal supervision. Then apply the rule of three: document every process, have someone else follow those steps, and ensure they can achieve the same results consistently.
					

Performance Goal 2: Show initiative in understanding and contributing to business processes.
					

Metric: Create a list of improvements or new ideas related to the business that we need to implement sometime in our next 365 days. Categorize impact and difficulty.
					

Performance Goal 3: Begin to build relationships with key customers or clients.
					

Metric: Have introductory conversations with at least two major clients or customers.
				
			
			

You can guess or you can game-plan. You choose which one will work more consistently.
			

## Days 60–90: Stabilize and Systematize

			

## Your North Star: Where to Set Your Team’s Sights

			
				

If you want to build a ship, don’t drum up the men to gather wood and give orders. Teach them to yearn for the vast and endless sea.
				

—Antoine de Saint-Exupéry
			
			

Oh, Donald Park. He’s one of those guys who makes you feel like your résumé could be written on a fingertip by comparison. He is a buyer and seller of businesses, a black belt in jujitsu, an owner of restaurants and clubs around the country, and often the smartest but quietest person in a room. At the time we met up, my business was struggling, wobbling around without a clear direction. So we sat down and I word-vomited all my problems. He said something so wise and yet so simple:
			
				

You’ve listed a bunch of problems, but one single problem ties them all together. You can’t guide a ship without knowing where north is. A company is the same. You don’t know where you are going, so neither does your team. Find your North Star. Your entire job as a CEO is to remind people which direction to row.
			
			

It reminded me of Amazon’s North Star: customer satisfaction. I remembered reading what Jeff Bezos said:
			
				

The number-one thing, by far, that has made us successful is our obsessive-compulsive focus on the customer, as opposed to the competitor. I constantly remind our employees to be afraid, to wake up every morning terrified. Not of our competition, but of our customers. Our customers have made our business what it is, they are the ones with whom we have a relationship, and they are the ones to whom we owe a great obligation. And we consider them to be loyal to us—right up until the second that someone else offers them a better service.
			
			

I went home and thought about it. Emails were my North Star. Everything else would come together if we kept growing our own audience list. So I went online, bought a tech sign that looked a bit like a clock, and every time we got a new email, that sign changed. 100,997…100,998…100,999…101,000…All day long, it would tick tick tick. I sent one to all my executives at the company. On every call I began with our number. Every day I had an auto-report post it in Slack. Wouldn’t you know it, emails started to climb higher and higher. The team rows a hell of a lot faster when they’re all rowing the same way.
			

What number is your North Star? One of my favorite KPIs to use for a North Star is a weekly growth number. Let’s say you use 3 percent weekly growth. That means your business will usually grow 351 percent over the course of a year. Obsess over this number and your team will follow your lead.
			

Your North Star goes beyond picking a number. If you want to grow your business, sell your dream. Owners and CEOs are dream salesmen. They create dreams that feel so big, beautiful, and real that other people can see themselves in them. Keep selling your dreams to the people you work with. That vision will beget the mission, and the mission will create obsessions. Obsessed people win.
			

## The Compass: How to Communicate

			

The 24-Hour Rule: Shrink weeks to days. If you want to move fast, communicate often. The greatest muscle you can build is urgency. Shrink your timelines. When you say next quarter, push for the next sixty days. When you say next month, push for two weeks. When you say next week, push for twenty-four hours. When you say critical, check in hourly. Decrease the time between having an idea and getting it done. Everything changes.
			

“Ah, there’s Sauron’s eye again,” jokes my COO, Chris Piper. He talks about the relentless focus and intensity my team feels when my eye is on something. I like to say, “If you don’t want the eye, communicate well and often.” There is a huge difference between “We have a growth target” and “My team is obsessed with tripling our annual profits.” To achieve the latter, you have to take them to church each week.
			

For me, I do something I call “the Compass.” Every Monday morning, I call together our teams across companies and I speak to them for ten minutes. I sell those I pay. It’s a ten-minute sermon (or as I call it, “the Extreme PowerPoint”). Here’s what we cover:
			

## Minutes 0–2: Mission Statement

			

At Contrarian Thinking, our mission is to “Free Minds and Build Bank Accounts.” Each week, I cover an incredible testimonial from our users that embodies that mission. It’s a small reminder of the change my team is making in people’s lives. If you believe your people are only motivated by money, you do not understand what it means to be an owner yet. But you will.
			

## Minutes 2–6: Highlights and Insights

			
				
- 
					

Our wins and fails
				
				
- 
					

Business problems solved
				
				
- 
					

What the team achieved
				
				
- 
					

The team’s insights from the week
				
				
- 
					

Products shipped, or actively being built
				
				
- 
					

Revenue and customer acquisition numbers
				
			
			

From this segment of the presentation, we can determine:
			
				
- 
					

Is our team aligned?
				
				
- 
					

What isn’t working?
				
				
- 
					

What should we double down on?
				
				
- 
					

What are our inefficiencies?
				
				
- 
					

Are we making quality decisions?
				
				
- 
					

Are we working on the right things?
				
			
			

## Minutes 7–8: What Is Coming

			

My friend Shaan Puri worked at Twitch with its billionaire founder, Justin Khan. He tells how in the pitch deck the founder would say, “We are a steamroller in a field of flowers. Unstoppable.” I like that analogy. I’ll ask my team: Where can your actions and our unfair advantage be so superior that our competitors feel like wildflowers under our wheel? Sometimes it’s social media done so well that no competitor would be able to get the reach we do. Sometimes it’s an amazing product that we give away for free. Steamrollers. Be one.
			

## Minute 9: Make Your People Better

			

People do not work for companies just to make money. People work for you to make their lives better. Help them. I like to wind the meeting down by sharing wisdom from a book or resource. Sometimes I’ll give away copies of life-changing books, like Extreme Ownership: How U.S. Navy SEALs Lead and Win.
			

## Minute 10: End High

			

How does a pastor conclude a church service? On a high note. He ends his sermon with words to make you think and lift you up as the pipe organ booms and the choir sings. The lights come up, and you leave feeling empowered to be a better person. That’s how you end a team meeting. A joke, a story, a quote, or some self-deprecating humor so that no one dreads your next meeting. Leave them feeling better than when they came in.
			

## Friday Finances: How to Finish the Week

			
				

Don’t tell me what you “think,” just tell me what’s in your portfolio.
				

—Nassim Taleb
			
			

A successful multi-business-owner friend of mine, Matt Aitchison, was walking with David Osborn, who later became his mentor (and who I mentioned earlier in the book). David is worth hundreds of millions of dollars and has the kind of life many entrepreneurs dream of. Matt thought his first encounter with David would involve general questions and life lessons. He was wrong. David went deep and specific:
			
				
- 
					

What’s your net worth?
				
				
- 
					

How much do you earn a month?
				
				
- 
					

What’s your monthly spending?
				
				
- 
					

Your portfolio breakdown?
				
				
- 
					

The acceleration of each income stream?
				
			
			

Easy bud, I scheduled the colonoscopy for later. Matt, not surprisingly, had few of these answers dialed in. Then David said something Matt will never forget:
			
				

You will never attract more money if you don’t understand it. Your finances are your foundation. If you don’t know them, you’re building a house on sand.
			
			

Matt vowed never again to be caught off guard like that. So he created a system. Every week, Matt holds something he’s dubbed “Financial Fridays,” where he goes over the finances of all his businesses. This also includes an assessment of his personal finances and an overview of all his investments.
			

It was a brilliant idea. So, I do it now too.
			

Every Friday for the past several years, I’ve reviewed the financials for all my businesses. Sometimes all this means is reviewing a simple spreadsheet, like the one that follows. Your job is to simply not run out of money. The best way to do this: Have your accountant track the money every week and send a report your way.
			

When I first started, my spreadsheet looked something like this:

As you can see, it doesn’t have to be fancy. My P&L (profit and loss statement) is hosted in a basic spreadsheet. By showing the different COAs (courses of actions) you can tweak, you see what your income and expenses could be. I can’t recommend enough doing this early on. We now give copies of this template to new members of our business-buying group. You can get the template for free at msmbook.com/tools.
			

Money is a cruel mistress; if you ignore her she’ll leave you for someone else. Financial independence can only stem from being financially aware. After all, where your attention goes, your energy flows. So track your business’s money.

Happiness is positive cash flow.
				

—Carl Icahn
			
			

Do you know the number-one reason for small-business failure? Running out of money. My friend Ayman Al-Abdullah, former CEO of AppSumo, said it best: “It’s simple, yet hard: Don’t run out of money.”
			

It’s also true that money is a game. The goal isn’t to make money for its own sake. The goal is to do great things. Make positive changes, give back, invest in more businesses, and employ more people. All of it requires a foundation of green. So implement Financial Fridays in your business.
			

## Days 90–120: Power Up

			

In this stage, we care about three things more than anything: Cash, People, and Change. It’s about accelerating how fast we get cash in the bank, finding the people inside our business who are most critical, and then beginning to insert small ideas for changes in the business.
			

## From Cash Suck to Cash Flow

			

Most businesses you look at buying will be cash-suck businesses to start. Your entire goal is to either find one that is already a cash-flow business or, in the first ninety days, move the business to a cash-flow business.
			

## Cash-Flow Businesses:

			
				
- 
					

Get paid, then provide service
				
				
- 
					

Make monthly recurring revenue
				
				
- 
					

Have lots of clients
				
			
			

## Cash-Suck Businesses:

			
				
- 
					

Provide service, then get paid
				
				
- 
					

Make money a few times a year
				
				
- 
					

Have one big client, and if they leave, we’re in trouble
				
			
			
				
			
			

You want to imagine throwing out a boomerang (product or service) and having it come right back to you (getting paid). I call this the Cash-Flow Boomerang Process. Research your Cash Conversion Cycle and look at ways you can shorten it. That means how fast you feel that cash boomerang back at you.
			

How important is this? Well, Dell Computer nearly went bankrupt because they could not collect quickly. They had an average A/R (accounts receivable) of sixty-three-plus days. That meant you bought something, they delivered it, and they didn’t get paid until sixty-three days later. That’s all changed. You now have to pay up front when you order a computer online.
			
				
					

Cash-Flow Boomerang Process
				
				
					
				
			
			

Here’s a list of ideas to get your brain rolling:
			
				
- 
					

Take more up-front payments—50 percent, for example.
				
				
- 
					

Change payment terms to be shorter. As opposed to doing the service and then getting paid thirty days later, we take payment the second you click buy.
				
				
- 
					

Give a discount for cash payments. Take more cash up front and decrease credit card fees.
				
				
- 
					

When you book a service appointment, allow your team to auto-run the card. You have the customer sign a doc that says they allow their credit card to be run upon completion automatically.
				
				
- 
					

Have a line of credit (LOC) for every single business. I like having backup money from the bank in case I need it in the form of a letter of credit.
				
				
- 
					

Have an LOC on every single bank account you can. Allows for multiple LOCs and better financial flexibility.
				
				
- 
					

Delay payments to some of your suppliers and get better terms from them.
				
			
			

## The Big Scary: Cutting Costs

			

It’s spring, your closet is stuffed. You can barely see what’s in it. What happened? Bloat. Time for spring cleaning.
			

Last fall, all of our businesses did an expense analysis with a goal of cutting 20 percent of costs. Empires aren’t felled from without, they are drowned from within. Bloat happens naturally and costs will kill your business.
			

Here’s how we cut costs by one sixth across most companies (steal my homework):
			
				
- 
					

 Cost-savings goal: I set up a meeting with the team leaders and asked: Can we still hit our goals and cut expenses? We set our sights on a number (I said 20 percent as a baseline), and we ended up at around 16 percent. That is actually a lot. In one business, we have monthly expenses at $600,000, so that’s $96,000 less we spend now monthly. That equates to $1.15 million a year in savings. Or you could look at that and say I added another $1.15 million to my profits a year. At a multiple of 3X,[*1] it’s another $3.45 million in my pocket. Nice.
				
				
- 
					

Show them the cut: Take each business unit and look at their P&L or budget. Then show them the total number they would need to cut to hit their desired decrease in expenses. Secret tip: If you have managers, let them be the ones to do the first cuts. They’re closest to the business. Let people clean up their own closet.
				
				
- 
					

Dues and subscriptions: Subscriptions and recurring costs are the first things to bloat. We cut three ways:
					
						
- 
							

Number of seats
						
						
- 
							

Number of tools
						
						
- 
							

Renegotiate rates
						
					
					

One business had multiple dead users in ServiceTitan. Another had Jobber and ServiceTitan. Another had Mailchimp, ConvertKit, and HubSpot (which all basically do the same thing). We cut unnecessary costs, and then with what is left, we negotiate every single piece of software’s price. On average, I’d say 30-plus percent of our service providers decrease our rates just for asking. Found revenue: $5K to $20K.
					
				
- 
					

The walking dead: Almost every business has underperforming ideas and zombie units that need to be cut. The best businesses focus. Google has killed hundreds of business units since it started.[*2] We have killed thirteen-plus in my businesses. One example was an agency that had customizable offerings (too much work), another was a newsletter (not enough money), another was a physical product line (too much work and not enough money).
				
				
- 
					

Contractors: Everything from vendors to contract workers to consultants is the next area to negotiate, cut, or change. We ended up taking in-house ads for one business that saved us $4K-plus of ad spend. We rolled off one “nice to have” consultant. We cut a few underperforming contractors, then gave 50 percent of that to current team members to take on more work.
				
				
- 
					

Employees: This I do very sparingly. I pride myself on never (at least not yet) having layoffs in any of my portfolio companies based on the market. We grow but not too aggressively, exactly for that reason. We did end up letting go of a few underperformers faster in down markets, which is actually something I wish we’d handled better. Sad fact: if you feel like you should possibly fire someone, you already know the answer.
					

One way to determine who to cut is by asking managers. Whom do you routinely find yourself having to “fix” things for and wishing they would pick things up? If the name comes up more than once per month (it’s usually once per week), you have your answer. I call it the “splinter effect”—a niggling annoyance that’s not the right fit.
					
				
- 
					

Cut, automate, delegate, outsource: We have a process called CADO. We analyze what we can cut (stop doing) entirely because it doesn’t drive revenue; what we are doing manually that we can automate; what we can delegate to those who earn less; and finally what we can outsource. For instance, we had people manually posting on social media (face-palm), then started using an automation tool. We also delegated someone’s manual review work, which cost $80K-plus a year, to an outsourced virtual assistant that costs $12K to $20K. “We’ve always done it this way” is not an excuse.
				
			
			
				
					

The Cado Process
				
				
					
				
			
			
				
- 
					

Turn it into a game: We offered a $1K bonus in each of our companies to anyone who came up with an idea to use AI to cut a manual task. We paid the bonus, implemented the idea, and cut $100K in annual expenses.
				
				
- 
					

Reinvest the savings: The benefit of positive cash flow is we get to give more love to companies we are actively incubating and growing. Those businesses need more resources, and now we can offer them up.
				
			
			

## Hire Slow, Fire Fast: How to Create a Culture of A-Players

			
				
			
			

Remember this: Winners want pressure. You can use almost the exact same process for hiring and analyzing post-acquisition A-players as you do for hiring an operator. But there’s one more thing you can do for a potential employee before you hire them: give them a tiny trial project.
			

After the first interview, send them this in an email:
			
				

Before making a decision, we give potential hires a short project. The project is role-specific and helps us understand their skills. Are you open to participating?
			
			

Once they agree, you can email them your trial project, which should consist of tasks similar to what they’ll be doing on the job. Why put them through this? Because you need a sneak peek into how they would handle real job challenges before you commit to working together.
			

Here’s a sample of a trial project I sent to a potential hire after their interview:
			
				

Hey there Scott,
				

Thanks so much again for the great interview! As mentioned, I’m going to have the top candidates do a few tasks to make sure we’re a mutual fit. If interested, tackle these items and email me back your responses. If not, no worries!
				

Project 1: Edit a blog post
				

Here is a sample blog post (download link). It needs to be edited, grammatical issues fixed, links fixed, with proper formatting. How do you do it?
				

Project 2: Customer service email response
				

You receive this email in our support email box:
				

Hey Codie & team,
				

I joined Contrarian Thinking but don’t find the value to be worth the price. Can you please refund me?
				

How do you respond? How do you know the correct way to respond?
			
			

Review their trial project. You can grade them based on the following criteria:
			
				
- 
					

How quickly they complete it
				
				
- 
					

The quality of their answers
				
				
- 
					

Clear signals that they’re genuinely excited to join your company
				
			
			

For a current employee, I’d do the same process but ask them to create an SOP for their current role and then follow it. Again, the best predictor of future behavior is past behavior. And the best predictor of performance…is performance. Operate on show, not tell.
			

## How to Fire Fast

			
				

It’s not what you preach, it’s what you tolerate…. If substandard performance is accepted and no one is held accountable…that poor performance becomes the new standard.
				

—Jocko Willink
			
			

Now for some bad news. When you take over your business, I can almost guarantee that someone will sabotage your efforts. It may be subtle, or it may be obvious. Whatever the case, this person is a cancer that will slowly spread if you do not cut them out. Just like cancer, they are not evil—they might not even realize what they are doing—but they have the power to kill your business. You have one job: protect the house.
			

You must hold yourself and your team to an incredibly high standard. Low standards are like weeds in your garden. High standards are the pruning that lets everything else grow. If you want to grow, you need to be on the lookout for these types.
			

I can’t take the sting out of firing someone, but I can help you find the words to do it. I love our friend Nolan Gore’s mantra, “Remember the 3 Fs. Fair—don’t be an a**hole. Firm—no wishy-washy. Final—they will try to get another chance.”
			

Here’s a script that’s helped me before:
			
				

YOU: Hi, [employee’s name], thanks for coming in. I have some bad news, so I’ll get straight to it. After careful consideration, we’ve decided to end your employment. This decision is final and takes effect immediately.
				

THEM: Why am I being let go? I’ve been with this company for years.
				

YOU: This decision is based on how we’re restructuring the business. What I can say is that I will help you as much as I’m able to with your next job. If you need a reference, a letter of recommendation, or even just resources that can help, I’m here. All you need to do is ask.
				

THEM: Okay…so what happens next?
				

YOU: You’ll receive your final paycheck on [date], which includes [severance, unused vacation days, etc.]. Please make sure to return any company property before you leave today. I wish you nothing but the best, so please let me know if I can be of service to you moving forward.
			
			

If you have any heart at all, it will pain you to let someone go. But you (or your operator) have to do it. I know it’s difficult. But I try to remember that it’s possible that our company—or even just me—could be holding this person back. I say this from personal experience, after witnessing several former employees go on to do bigger, better things. Getting fired can redirect someone to their true path.
			

After his departure from Apple, Steve Jobs said:
			
				

Sometimes, being fired is the best thing that could happen to you. It can be a catalyst that propels you to a greater destiny, like a star being launched into a new orbit.
			
			

I’ve always believed that no one should be surprised by getting fired. You hand-hold up, or you coach out. But I can promise you I’ve never met an owner that didn’t have to do it at some point. So gird your loins.
			

You must protect your team and protect your house. THAT is your first responsibility above all else.
			

## Operator Tech Stack: Tools to Help You Run Your Business

			

All right, now you are allowed to show your technological brilliance. Well, a bit at least. Tech can make you love or hate your business. Without it, it’s very hard to scale, and it will make you an employee of your business. I’ve put together my Day 1 checklist of the tech I add to every small business I acquire. (Note: These are not affiliate links, and I do not get a cut for any sales.)
			
				

Deel (deel.com). This is hiring, HR, and payroll in just one system. We use Deel for most of the hiring at Contrarian Thinking. It’s brilliant for full-time employees and contractors. This will also allow you to hire top talent across the world easily, because they will act as your employer of record. It’s not cheap for a small business, but the systems they use can save you millions in potential contractor or employee lawsuits by doing things right.
				

Jobber (jobber.com). Jobber allows you to quote, schedule, invoice, and get paid, all in one place. If you have a service-based small business, you’re crazy not to use this.
				

Bench (bench.co). Bench is idiotproof, easy, fast, and relatively cheap accounting, but it’s not sophisticated as you scale past a few million. Once you reach that point, switch to QuickBooks.
				

OpenPhone (openphone.com). If you’re still using your personal cell phone for your SMB…please stop. OpenPhone will create a second phone number on your existing phone, but it goes through a different interface so you don’t need two phones.
				

Google Workspace (workspace.google.com). Great for hosting your business’s email. No need to church it up, Google Workspace does it all. Keep it cheap and consistent.
				

Notion (notion.so). We used to document our processes and SOPs in Google Drive, and it was a major headache. Now we have all our SOPs, processes, and most of our tasks here.
				

Slack (slack.com). Astounding to me how few small businesses use this. Get off email entirely and have all company communication go through Slack. Keep email for inbound.
				

Upwork (upwork.com). Wherever possible, we ask, “Can this job be done for one fifth the cost by someone overseas to increase the margin?” If it can, take time to find the right contractor. It may take a few tries to get the right hire, but once you do, it’s golden. We probably spend $1 million–plus on Upwork a year in hiring freelancers and contractors, especially overseas talent.
				

Trinet (trinet.com). We run most of our businesses’ insurance through TriNet (previously Zenefits), which connects to Deel for ease when we need employee benefits.
				

Zapier (zapier.com). We use Zapier to automate workflows across most of our organization. We use it for operations, marketing, sales, and practically everywhere. You can hire Zapier specialists on Upwork to help with automating more specialized tasks.
				

Loom (loom.com). This is the best tool for cutting down on meetings. You can record your screen to share a message with your teammates or customers, then easily share a link to the recording. You’ll save your own time and keep your employees more focused this way. Loom is also the best way to onboard your team. You can explain things to remote team members and catalog every process in your business. I also like that the videos can be sped up to 2X. Time is money.
				

Superhuman (superhuman.com). Superhuman makes responding to emails and inbox management 10X easier with shorthand signals and smart segmenting. It overlays on your current email as well.
			
			
			• • •
			
			

Congratulations, you’re in a great position. You’ve taken command of your business. You’ve hired and trained your operator. You’ve turned a job into a business. Now it’s time to harness that business. That means installing marketing systems that boost your profits automatically, stepping back to manage it (without losing your mind), scaling up its valuation, and preparing for an exit. That’s what’s next.
			
				

Skip Notes
				
					

*1 A 3X multiple means that buyers are willing to pay three times the business’s annual profit, reflecting what they expect to earn in the future.
				
				
					

*2 Check out killedbygoogle.com.

---

Step 4: H Is for Harness, Main Street Millionaire
		
		
		

	
	
		
			
			

##

---

Growth Tactics: How to 10X Profits in Year One, Main Street Millionaire
		
		
		

	
	
		
			

## Growth Tactics: How to 10X Profits in Year One

			

Now we are in go mode. This is your time to start executing on all the notes, ideas, and growth plans you’ve accumulated. The best way to learn? By example. So, let’s start with power washing as a business example of what I like to do when I take over.
			

Why power washing? Because…I love watching porn. Okay, easy, this is a special type. Specifically, r/powerwashingporn on Reddit, a very addictive community with two million followers. All they do is post videos and before/after pictures of powerful water hoses cleaning up dirt and grime.
			

You might think this boring business doesn’t make much money. But with the right growth tactics, it has the potential to increase profits tenfold.[*1] If you don’t believe me, that’s understandable—10X’ing profits can sound unrealistic, especially if you’ve never done it before. But my team and I have done it, with many boring businesses in many different industries. So, right now, I’m going to prove to you that it’s not only possible, it’s actually simple. With a few marketing tweaks, we’ll grow a modest pressure-washing business into a multimillion-dollar enterprise.
			

Let’s pretend you’re the new owner. You’ve been running this business now for a full year as a solo owner-operator. It’s going well and you’re making six figures. Here are the numbers from the first year of your pressure-washing business:
			
				
- 
					

Total leads: 500
				
				
- 
					

Conversion: 35 percent
				
				
- 
					

Paid jobs: 175
				
				
- 
					

Average job price: $400
				
				
- 
					

Annual revenue: $70,000
				
				
- 
					

Annual expenses: $5,000 (yes, it really can be that cheap to start)
				
				
- 
					

Annual profits: $65,000
				
			
			

Let’s also say you’re located in a city with one hundred thousand high-income households (for reference: Austin has more than four hundred thousand households). Even though there are two well-established competitors in the area, it’s safe to say there’s still room to grow. So what do you do to grow your 10X?
			
				
- 
					

Pricing—Raise, Layer, and Iterate: Most small businesses are three to five years behind in price increases. You will not be. Raise your prices. Add a three-tiered price structure (low, medium, high). Add discounts for up-front payment, annual plans, and institutional versus retail pricing. Let’s elevate your business by raising your prices 20 percent. This results in a lower conversion rate for sales (down from 35 percent to 33 percent), but your average order price increases from $400 to $480.
				
				
- 
					

Upsells: The most underrated business truth of all time is this: it’s far more profitable to sell more stuff to your current customers than it is to find new customers. Charging for pressure washing is like presenting a menu: the more you offer, the more you’ll make. A driveway fetches you between $200 and $400. But a full house? I’m talking roof cleaning, pool cleaning, wood deck/fence restoration, gutter cleaning, window cleaning, and/or exterior painting. That’s $1,000 to $1,500 per job. Not to mention corporate clients, which average $3,000 per job. By offering numerous upsells, you raise your average job revenue by 50 percent, from $480 to $720.
				
				
- 
					

Recurring Revenue: You’ve been working way too hard, selling one pressure wash at a time. Moving forward, you’re going to offer your customers a subscription, so they are opted into repeat services. You’ll give them three upsell options: big package, medium package, small package. You’ll also sell biannual pressure washing and schedule their neighbors on the same dates to maximize efficiency. This raises your number of jobs per year by 50 percent, from 175 to 263.
					

For those keeping track at home:
					
						
- 
							

Total leads: 500
						
						
- 
							

Conversion: 33 percent ↓
						
						
- 
							

Paid jobs: 263 ↑
						
						
- 
							

Average job price: $720 ↑
						
						
- 
							

Annual revenue: $189,360 ↑
						
						
- 
							

Annual expenses: $10,000 ↑
						
						
- 
							

Annual profits: $179,360 ↑
						
					
					

Your salary has increased by 176 percent, with only 50 percent more jobs. So far, so good. But we’re just getting warmed up.
					
				
- 
					

Outsourcing: You have been running around town and handling every job. You’ve been the only technician, doing all the work yourself. Today, that stops. You decide to bring on a full-time technician to replace you. They handle 100 percent of the manual labor for all 263 jobs. You pay them an annual salary of $40,000. Suddenly, you’re making $139,360 in profit…without doing any of the work. Nice. You decide to expand your service area and hire two more technicians at $40,000 each. (Pro tip: One in hiring is none. Have multiple techs and your life will get easier.) You also upgrade from pressure-washing machines to power-washing trucks—nicer equipment that’s more efficient and heats the water, but costs more to operate (roughly $35,000 per truck). Together, your three technicians complete a total of 700 jobs per year, at an average price of $720. Sweet.
					

Time for an update:
					
						
- 
							

Total leads: 2,100 ↑
						
						
- 
							

Conversion: 33 percent
						
						
- 
							

Paid jobs: 700 ↑
						
						
- 
							

Average job price: $720
						
						
- 
							

Annual revenue: $504,000 ↑
						
						
- 
							

Annual expenses: $235,000 ↑
						
						
- 
							

Annual profits: $269,000 ↑
						
					
					

That’s a total increase of 3.1X in profits. You’re a quarter of the way to becoming a millionaire, and we’re still not done.
					
				
- 
					

Sales Response Time: When you were a solo owner, you couldn’t answer leads fast enough. Your average response time was twenty-four hours. As a result, you lost an estimated two hundred leads per year. Ouch! Now that you work on your business (instead of in it), you use approachment.com[*2] to implement a system that responds to new leads within sixty seconds. Your conversion rate skyrockets to 50 percent. With your expanded service area, your team is now able to close two hundred more leads per year. Total paid jobs per year increases to 1,000, and your new annual salary climbs up to $485,000.
				
				
- 
					

Five-Star Reviews: Your happy clients give good tips, but they rarely take the time to leave reviews. On the flip side, the few clients you’ve had who were unhappy with your services consistently go out of their way to write negative reviews. The result? An underwhelming Google Business page that makes your competitors look a lot more attractive. After doing some research, you learn the cold, hard truth about reviews:
					
						
- 
							

93 percent of customers read reviews.
						
						
- 
							

More than 90 percent will not use your product if you have less than 3.5 stars.
						
						
- 
							

58 percent of consumers will pay more and wait for a better-reviewed company.
						
						
- 
							

Unhappy people are 10X more likely to leave a review.
						
						
- 
							

It can take ten to twenty positive reviews to negate one negative.
						
					
					

You implement a tech tool that automatically asks customers for a five-star review after a job is completed. Within a matter of months, the appearance and SEO ranking of your Google Business listing improves dramatically. Your total jobs sold increases from 1,000 to 1,100 per year.
					
				
- 
					

Magic Moments: Now the real marketing fun begins. You study the viral power-washing videos on YouTube and find a channel that’s accumulated more than 500 million views. You notice they’ve uploaded simple videos (albeit a thousand of them!), and decide you can emulate what they’re doing. You tell your technicians to record one job per week, using a tripod and their cell phone’s time-lapse video feature, then send you the footage. (I’m getting excited just imagining the before and after clips.) You pay an overseas video editor $1,250 per month to edit four videos a week, in a similar style as the viral videos, then post them to YouTube. After a year, your channel has two hundred videos posted, five million views, and twenty thousand subscribers. The videos boost your company’s SEO ranking on Google, resulting in forty more jobs for your crew per year. Cherry on top: YouTube AdSense brings in $5,000 in revenue, and you land a $15,000 brand deal with a cleaning solutions company.
					

All right, it’s time for the final tally. Let’s see how we did:
					
						
- 
							

Total leads: 2,300 ↑
						
						
- 
							

Conversion: 50 percent ↑
						
						
- 
							

Paid jobs: 1,140 ↑
						
						
- 
							

Average job price: $720
						
						
- 
							

Annual revenue: $820,800 ↑
						
						
- 
							

Annual expenses: $250,000 ↑ ($15K to video editor)
						
						
- 
							

Annual profits: $570,800 ↑
						
					
			
			

Just like that, you’re halfway to becoming a millionaire. You’ve also managed to 8X your profits. To be clear, if you have a business that does a half million in annual profits, you can likely sell it for more like $1.5 million to $3 million. If this tickles your fancy, I have a full breakdown on how to scale the business to $3 million in annual profits (see the appendix), then you’re in a prime position for a private equity firm to buy you out. They’ll do it for 6 to 10X annual profit, which means $18 million to $30 million in your pocket. Cha-ching.
			

Is it easy? Nope. Is it guaranteed? Absolutely not. But it isn’t rocket science. That’s the beauty of owning a boring business. Mark can vouch for that.
			
				

Text Message Exchange
				

Mark: What a f*ing ride. $4.4M in 4 years, getting paid to take ownership.
				

Codie: I’m so proud of you. You’re a great dad, family man, helping out your community, making millions, and crushing jujitsu!
				

Slack Message to Contrarian Thinkers Community
				

Mark: LOI signed! $5.1M. I’ll get $1.38m at close and $550,000 over 3 years. 90 day exit for me. So grateful for all of you!
			
			

All right, let’s get back to your business again. Here are four growth tactics you can put into place within the next thirty days. Get ready for the ultimate listicle with only what you need and nothing you don’t.
			

## Step 1: Raise Your Prices by 5 to 30 Percent

			

Which prices should you raise? All of them. When should you raise them? Right now. Really? Yes. Inflation is anywhere between 4 and 9 percent (depending on who you ask; it could be much higher). That means you are losing money every year you don’t raise your prices.
			

Before I buy a business, I’ll run a price analysis ahead of time:
			
				
- 
					

What are the current prices?
				
				
- 
					

When were they last raised?
				
				
- 
					

How often are they raised?
				
				
- 
					

What are their competitors’ price points?
				
				
- 
					

How much are similar products in the market selling for?
				
			
			

After you run this analysis, my guess is you’ll quickly see that you can comfortably raise prices anywhere from 5 to 30 percent, sometimes higher.
			

Whenever a business owner feels reluctant about an arbitrary price increase, I tell them this: Solve rich people’s problems. They pay better. Do you have a luxury or ultra-premium option in your pricing? If not, it’s time to add one. If I bought a laundromat, I’d add wash-and-fold at 5X the price. If I bought a car wash, I’d add the elite option for unlimited washes. If I bought an accounting firm, I’d add white-glove concierge annual plans.
			

Most small-business owners “live in their own wallet,” meaning they won’t charge any higher than what they would be willing to pay. That’s dumb. Let rich people pay you lots of money if they want to. You’ll be surprised how often they will.
			

## Step 2: Clean Up Your Prices

			

Please stop giving prospects more than three options. Confused prospects do not buy. I prefer the sandwich method. One low price, one meaty price, one high price. Make them go for the meat.
			
				
					

The Sandwich Method: Make Them Go for the Meat
				
				
					
				
			
			

## Step 3: Add Subscriptions for Recurring Revenue

			

One of the things they never tell you about being in business is that people love to not pay you. So you want to get paid in full, up front, and often, whenever and wherever you can. The magic words: recurring revenue.
			

Recurring revenue simply means ongoing payments for ongoing services. Unlike one-off transactional sales, these recurring revenues are stable and predictable. You can count on them at regular intervals. They are the Dom Pérignon of business revenue. Here are a few examples:
			
				
- 
					

Subscriptions to your car wash
				
				
- 
					

Monthly automated payment for landscaping service
				
				
- 
					

Annual subscriptions for extermination companies
				
			
			

After you’ve raised your prices, add subscriptions. This is money you don’t have to chase, and it’s worth more when you sell, making it a move you’ll never regret.
			
				
					

The Six Types of Recurring Revenue
				
				
					
				
			
			

## Step 4: Update Your Website

			

Don’t spend tens of thousands on it. Do make sure the buy button is big. The call to action is obvious. The testimonials are new, with real human names and faces. The email capture is obvious. The services lead with benefits, not features. And somewhere in there, you remind them that you’re owned by humans who are there to solve their problems.
			

## Step 5: Respond to Leads Immediately

			

When it comes to sales, the single most important factor is speed. Do you want to close more leads? Respond faster. The first business to respond to a potential lead typically wins it. Studies have shown businesses are 20X more likely to convert a lead if they reply within five minutes (compared to thirty-plus minutes).
			

Do you know how long it takes the average small-business owner to respond to a lead? FORTY-SEVEN HOURS! That means if you are spending money on leads and not replying to them within five minutes, you are wasting your money. The good news is that the bar to beat your competition is unbelievably low.
			

But I’m too busy doing the actual work to respond to new leads! Use approachment.com, a service that deploys real human agents to respond to inbound leads within sixty seconds. I use them for my businesses to monitor and instantly respond to all inbound inquiries, whether by text, WhatsApp, Facebook Messenger, Instagram Messenger, or Twitter. Their live team monitors your channels to assist, engage, and categorize leads. When the conversation is done, Approachment lets your team know via CRM integration or by emailing you the transcript.
			

## Step 6: Referrals for Money and Humans

			

Most businesses have zero referral processes. Remember, it’s more profitable to sell your current customer new things than to find new customers. You have $0 customer acquisition cost, confirmation bias, recency bias, higher trust level, client buying history/data, and their contact info. So, what do you do? Upsell, cross-sell, affiliate sell, add subscriptions from monthly to annual plans. This is the way. At least for me. Call and get to know your ten to twenty happiest customers and ask them this question:
			
				

We’re a small business that only really grows by people like you referring us to others. Who else do you know who might be a fit? We’ll give them a discount for being your friend.
			
			

Then call your next ten to twenty customers and ask them what else you can do for them. Do you think they remember that you sell Christmas tree lighting, gutter cleaning, and landscaping? Probably not.
			

The second type of referrals are for employees. Finding good ones is always hard. Pay your best people to refer more people. Five hundred to five thousand dollars per hire (that stays for more than six months) will get your people recruiting.
			

## Step 7: Ask for Reviews

			

Small businesses have no brand. Want proof? I bet you can’t name a single landscaping or plumbing company, even if you’ve used them for decades. That means you will most likely never be “the Nike of roof cleaning.” So, how do you get people returning to you again and again? Word of mouth. That’s getting other people to flap their lips about you. In order to do that, you have to start treating every single customer as though they are your only option for generating new business.
			

Enter nicejob.com, or any of the many software tools that put reviews, referrals, and social media content on autopilot. After each service, your customers will automatically be asked to leave a review on Google, Facebook, and/or Yelp. If that goes unanswered, the text message will be followed up by two well-timed emails, each of them politely asking for a review. And if that’s ignored, the customer will get a little follow-up phone call.
			

Does it work? Well, in less than a year, a local cleaning company run by my friend Johnny added three hundred–plus reviews to their listings across Yelp, Facebook, and Google. Those rankings boosted them to the top spot for “window cleaning” searches in their area. Their review count is now so high that it’s 3X more than the next two companies combined. If you can optimize for one thing, make it reviews. They are worth their weight in gold.
			

Oh, and while we’re talking about it: If this book has been helpful to you so far, would you mind popping onto Amazon and leaving a quick review? It would mean a lot to me and will help any future readers who dream of owning a business. I treasure you!
			

## Step 8: Sale-EO Not CEO

			

For your first year in business, if you are the CEO, you are the salesperson. Make sure you schedule your time accordingly. That means six to ten hours a week selling. Asking friends to test your products and report back. Writing and testing individual sales emails. Playing in customer service to turn mistakes into money. Your bank account will be a reflection of your time spent.
			

## Ownership Autopilot: Managing Your Business without Going Crazy

			

## Your Business Scorecard: Monitoring Metrics That Matter

			

Elon Musk famously once said:
			
				

My mind is a storm. I don’t think most people would want to be me. They may think they’d want to be me, but they don’t know. They don’t understand.
			
			

I’m not a genius like Elon, but I can appreciate the sentiment. Elon is running not one multibillion-dollar company, not two, but three: Tesla, SpaceX, and Twitter (now called X). It turns out that when your businesses are that big and that many, the only thing you know for sure is that at any given moment something somewhere is breaking. You might want Elon’s bank account, but you do not want that mental burden. Let’s break down a few ways you can live your life and run your businesses without your businesses running you.
			

Don’t drown in data. Every business has a path that your clients travel to pay you money. We call this the “Deal Driveway.” At my friend Ayman Al-Abdullah’s former company, AppSumo, it looked like this:
			

He’d close a deal (with a software partner that would give him a cut to sell their software), write copy (his team would write a GREAT sales email about the software), send email (he would send the email to hundreds of thousands of business owners), then make money (they would buy the software from AppSumo).
			

Ayman knew exactly which metrics he needed to track for the business to succeed:
			
				
- 
					

Deals closed
				
				
- 
					

Emails written
				
				
- 
					

Emails sent
				
				
- 
					

Money in
				
				
- 
					

Email addresses collected (front end)
				
			
			
				
					

Deal Driveway
				
				
					
				
			
			

You need to find your Deal Driveway. Ask yourself: If I could look at only three to five numbers, which ones would tell me the most complete story about my business?
			

Most businesses boil down to three to five of these numbers:
			
				
- 
					

Money: Revenue, expenses, profit, EBITDA, cash flow, cash on hand, burn rate
				
				
- 
					

Clients: Net new, churn, lifetime value (LTV), average order value
				
				
- 
					

Marketing: Followers, subscribers, emails, sales calls, meetings, site visits
				
			
			

Put those metrics on a scorecard and start tracking them, developing a trend line that reveals growth or decay.
