As management guru Peter Drucker once famously said, “What gets measured gets managed.” Your high-level scorecard with three to five metrics should measure two things: output and input (or what I like to call “activity”). An output-based scorecard measures things like:
			
				
- 
					

New users
				
				
- 
					

Website traffic
				
				
- 
					

Revenue
				
				
- 
					

Growth week over week
				
			
			

These are numbers you don’t have control over but need to see moving forward. They are often lagging (following) indicators of the next scorecard, which is activity-based. The activity scorecard measures:
			
				
- 
					

Number of calls made
				
				
- 
					

Number of content pieces posted
				
				
- 
					

Hours worked across staff
				
				
- 
					

Machines in operation
				
			
			

These are things you can control that likely will increase your output scorecard. Now you know what you want to measure, which means you know what you want to spend your time on. Ayman would break his week (or his team’s) down like this:
			
				
					
					
					
					
					
				
				
					
						
							

Monday
						
						
							

Tuesday
						
						
							

Wednesday
						
						
							

Thursday
						
						
							

Friday
						
					
					
						
							

Closing deals
						
						
							

Writing copy
						
						
							

Sending email
						
						
							

Customer support
						
						
							

Sourcing
						
					
				
			
			

What gets measured gets managed. What gets managed gets scheduled. What gets scheduled gets done. That’s the execution triangle.
			
				
					

Execution Triangle
				
				
					
				
			
			

Again, don’t drown in data. The numbers should leap out at you. Anything more is noise. In my businesses, I rarely want in-depth reports. I want short and sweet scorecards that allow me to view the business with an eye to profits and costs. That’s it. To get your team to do this, praise good briefs, scorecards, and updates, and push back hard on bad briefs, scorecards, and updates.
			

Here’s an example of a great scorecard. It’s short, shows trends week over week, and gives the original source.
			
				

Referral Program: Weekly Update
				

Hey team, now that we’ve relaunched our referral program (on February 22nd to be precise), here are our referral numbers for this week. We’ll be logging them week over week to keep us on top of our game.
				
					
- 
						

We had +28.81% subscribers last week compared to the one before
					
					
- 
						

Referrals however dropped by 15.38% week over week
					
					
- 
						

The new reward system, which offers a reward for just one referral, is generating high volumes of one-referral sign-ups
					
					
- 
						

Looking at the last thirty days, we’ve had way more referrals: 35.5% more than the month before that.
					
				
				

Any questions, let me know!
			
			
				
				
			
			
				
					
					
					
					
				
				
					
						
							

Weekly Referral Data
						
					
					
						
							

Date (WoW)
						
						
							

New Subscribers
						
						
							

New Referrals
						
						
							

Referral Traffic
						
					
					
						
							

February 27
						
						
							

6,001
						
						
							

26
						
						
							

239
						
					
					
						
							

March 7
						
						
							

7,730
						
						
							

22
						
						
							

276
						
					
				
			
			

How do you write a great brief? Keep it as short as possible. You don’t need to write a full page, just a few paragraphs that aim for total clarity on these three items:
			
				
- 
					

Problem (What, Severity, Priority)
				
				
- 
					

Potential Solutions (What, Cost, Time)
				
				
- 
					

Ask (Help, Cost, Time, Team)
				
			
			

Briefs that lay out the Problem, Solutions, Ask Model above will save you decades in management.
			

## Decentralized Management: Stop Barking

			

Let’s call this the Warren Buffett method. Warren manages Berkshire Hathaway’s 360,000 employees and still reads six newspapers a day. How? He uses Tom Murphy’s (former head of Capital Cities, then ABC, then Disney—what a slacker) management principles of decentralization. That means he hires the best possible people, lets them do the work, and watches the costs. Rocket science, I know. And yet so few do it.
			

“We are both proponents of a decentralized management philosophy,” Murphy said, “of hiring key people carefully; of pushing decisions down the organization; and of setting overall principles and resisting the temptation to be involved with details. In other words, don’t hire a dog and try to do the barking.”
			

Stop barking and let your dogs do the work. And no, I’m not calling employees “dogs,” it’s just a great analogy. Easy, buddy. It’s only natural to assume that no one can take care of your assets better than you. This is especially true when the assets are businesses. That’s why it’s common to see a lot of micromanaging from owners in their first business acquisitions.
			

But first, I like to ask myself…Are you sure you’re the best? Hard to be. Is there no way you could find someone better? Well, there is a way. Do you have to do it all? No, probably not. Then don’t. Because we’re empire building. After all, what happens when your portfolio expands to three companies? To five, ten, or twenty? Will you still involve yourself in the day-to-day running of each one? Will you even be able to? Let me save you some tears. No, you won’t.
			

The key to managing businesses is putting the right people in charge and limiting your direct reports. Keep that group small and tight. Biggie had it right. More direct reports, more problems, or something like that. Buffett runs his headquarters with twenty-five people who, in turn, manage the other 360,000 employees. His companies are worth billions and billions in assets. Follow the Sage.
			

Let’s take my companies, Contrarian Thinking Capital, Contrarian Thinking Media, and Main Street Holding Company. Main Street Holding Company is the umbrella company, overseeing businesses that collectively do nine figures annually, with hundreds of employees and contractors. But only five people report to me—the COO, head of Portfolio, head of Content, head of Product, and head of Finance. I meet with each twice a month, and they work with all the others.
			

Here’s the structure for my portfolio holding company:
			
				
- 
					

Head of Portfolio: Earns $100,000 to $200,000 (plus bonus) to oversee the businesses and grow revenue
				
				
- 
					

CEO for each business: Owns 5 to 10 percent of the company and earns $75,000 to $300,000 (plus bonus) to run the business
				
				
- 
					

CIO/head of Finance: Earns $100,000 to $300,000 or more to provide me with financials for each business
				
				
- 
					

Investment analysts: Earn $75,000 to $150,000 for finding new deals, analyzing and assisting the portfolio management
				
			
			

There is a phrase I heard once: “purposefully absent.” That the best CEOs allow their people room to figure it TF out. The most unhappy CEOs try to solve every problem. Your job as a leader is to use this line often:
			

I’m looking forward to hearing your solutions to that problem. Keep me updated.
			
				
			
			

Your fastest way to millions is hiring the right people. Your fastest way to misery is hiring the wrong people. Back to Warren Buffett, who’s managed Berkshire Hathaway for the last twenty-five years. Their team is only twenty-five people, and their turnover during that time was 2 PERCENT. Wow.
			

How do you keep your people around? I once worked for a company called First Trust. There were many things I did not like about the place—like 99.9 percent of the senior managers (who, to be fair, probably didn’t like me much either because I’m unemployable). But one thing I have to give them credit for is that the CEO is a savant at cult—oops, I mean culture building. In the middle of nowhere (Wheaton, Illinois) he built a multibillion-dollar business with a few hundred employees. They weren’t all paid supremely well, yet the average tenure was decades. That’s because their CEO created a community, not a company. He is categorically brilliant.
			

I remember the first company Christmas party I attended. I was shocked. They flew in employees, kids, and spouses and partied in a large ballroom complete with a Santa Claus, presents for each employee, and a meal. But the most interesting part was the annual PowerPoint. It didn’t show quarterly earnings. It didn’t highlight big wins. It was a glorified photo album. It showed the 38 mothers and daughters who worked at the firm. It showed the 102 siblings who worked there, and the 44 husbands and wives (I’m making up those numbers because I can’t remember, but they were HIGH). It was a private company where the CEO was fond of saying, “We’re private, so we do what we want!” Create a culture that isn’t run by PC, woke, and HR nonsense, and you’ll create a cult of like-minded humans focused on the mission.
			

In a world where conglomerates are trying to dominate, small companies have a way of restoring our faith in the world. It’s not hard to find studies that reveal how much happier small-business employees are. Ninety-five percent of small-business employees said their happiness stems from the small-business environment. Eight out of ten employees say they feel appreciated by their bosses and more respected than those who work for big companies. Go small and you might win big.
			

## Buy Solutions: Why Your Business Shouldn’t Rely on Your Heroic Efforts

			

Have you ever felt like you are failing no matter what you do? You can’t be saved because the failure is you. That was me. I was toiling away in my newsletter business, which was growing at a pace that would make a slug lose its patience. I was depressed. Despite tweeting, Instagramming, LinkedIning, and Facebooking, I couldn’t grow fast enough. I think it bothered me more than any other business because my face was on it and the numbers were public. Almost like throwing a daily birthday party for your sixteen-year-old self and no one showing up. Not fun.
			

One day I saw a friend of mine post, “Welcome to the 20,000 new people who are on this newsletter here.” WTF. Twenty thousand subscribers in a week?!
			

Prepared to call him a liar, I called up my friend to ask how the royal F he had managed that huge increase. His answer: I bought a failing newsletter using seller financing.
			

Oh. Duh.
			

Even I forgot to apply what’s called “add-on acquisitions” to my own business. Since that day, I’ve acquired three businesses to roll into my newsletter business (Approachment, BizScout, Unconventional Acquisitions). I’ve also sold a newsletter (Growth Hackers). Those acquisitions led to more than one hundred thousand new subscribers and millions in revenue. As each business gets rolled in, I reuse my current systems and add new clients or products to our budding media empire. Magic.
			

If you want to add steroids to your process of acquiring new customers, there is no faster method than buying businesses. I turn to it each time I’m stuck. It is not easy though. Merging two companies is categorically harder than buying an individual company. Complexity creates difficulty.
			

A friend of mine, Dan Sullivan, wrote a book called Who Not How. It’s brilliant. The idea is that whenever you have a problem, don’t ask “How can I fix it?” Instead ask “Who can fix it for me?” I would add another question to the mix: “What can I buy that would fix this?” In other words: buy, don’t build. When something isn’t working, try buying the solution.
			

We had a company called Sublime in our portfolio and they spent $2 million a year on rolling joints by hand. Yes, it was a cannabis company. For a company that was only doing $10 to $20 million per year, that’s a lot. So one of our clever partners, Joe, decided to see if there were machines that could do that instead of humans. Turns out yes. They were $750,000 each and we’d need two. Now Joe could have gone out and bought them, but then he’d have to pay cash out of the balance sheet and full price. Instead, he went looking for companies that weren’t doing well and could be rescued with a small amount down (plus some equity in Sublime). He found one. For roughly $500,000 in total, we got two machines, some employees, and saved ourselves about $1 million a year in costs.
			

Acquisitions are not the only way, but they are usually the least-thought-of way.
			

## Scaling Up: How to Expand Your Business Responsibly

			

## Business Blinders: Ignoring Shiny Objects in Your First Twelve Months

			
				

Whenever I meet a successful CEO, I ask them how they did it. Mediocre CEOs point to their brilliant strategic moves or their intuitive business sense or a variety of other self-congratulatory explanations. The great CEOs tend to be remarkably consistent in their answers. They all say, “I didn’t quit.”
				

—Ben Horowitz
			
			

Do you too suffer from Shiny Object Syndrome? Do you worry about being trapped with your boring business forever and hating your boring life? I know I did. Who wants to run a laundromat, car wash, or multifamily rentals forever?
			

I remember my mom used to say, “One day you’ll realize the grass is never truly greener.” Ahhh, but Mom, are you sure? That perpetual itch for the unknown. Chasing the squirrel. Humans are hardwired with a need for novelty. In truth, newness makes us happy.
			

I love doing one thing—for a minute. Right about the time I become quite good at it, I’m on to the next challenge. Sam Zell once said he wanted to be “chairman of everything and the CEO of nothing.” A jack-of-all-trades and master of none. If that sounds like music to your ears, this section is for you.
			

Your road will start with your first business, but at some point, about two-hundred-ish days later, you’ll get the itch. That’s the restless urge to grow into your second, third, or fourth company. Even oak trees start as tiny acorns.
			

Don’t believe me. Believe the billionaires:
			
				
- 
					

In 2018, there were 1,426 billionaires in the world.
				
				
- 
					

Of those, about 960 were self-made.
				
				
- 
					

Of those 960, more than 830 of them—that’s 86 percent—made money from multiple businesses.
				
			
			

Branson, Cuban, Adelson, Musk, Bezos, and Jobs all followed their curiosity, even to the moon. The big difference between them and you? They knew this truth. Your lack of focus will kill you before your competitors ever get a chance. When you start, you focus on the few. When you get rich, you diversify with many. This is the key to making money and keeping it.
			
				
			
			
				
			
			
				
			
			

To be clear: You are not avoiding new opportunities; you are avoiding distractions. Think of it as being a “selective opportunist.” Especially in those first twelve months of new ownership, you’ll need to put on blinders.
			
			• • •
			
				

He that hunts two rabbits catches neither.
				

—Erasmus
			
			

I became friends with Geoffrey Kent by accident. We sat side by side on a plane from Cannes to London. He created the luxury travel company Abercrombie & Kent,[*3] played polo with King Charles III, traveled the world, and is a bit like Richard Branson before Richard’s time. After a few drinks, I asked him what the key was to running his company so successfully and making a billion dollars. Cheeky question, I know. He said:
			

The main ingredient is one we all have, but we rarely use it correctly: time.
			

He ran his business for sixty-one years. What if that’s the main ingredient? What if the recipe is simply Time + Focus + Consistency? I think it might be. Every successful entrepreneur has faced moments of doubt and temptation to jump ship. What sets the great ones apart is their ability to stay the course. Don’t take your eyes off the prize. Stay focused, and when you feel you’re getting derailed, remember Morpheus in The Matrix:
			

Were you listening to me, Neo? Or were you looking at the woman in the red dress?
			

I was—
			

Look again.
			

And then Neo finds himself with a gun in his face. Don’t let that be you. Distractions, even beautiful ones, can kill your business in the beginning. Stay focused.
			

## Platform Acquisitions: Maximizing Your Revenue Streams

			
				

If you’re going to dedicate your life to a business, which is the only way it will ever work, you should choose one with the potential to be huge.
				

—Stephen A. Schwarzman, the richest man in private equity
			
			

Carlos Slim. Bernard Arnault. Charles Koch. Do you know what these names have in common? They are the wealthiest men in the world, who all made their money the same way: platform acquisitions. They grew their billions by buying them.
			

In this section, you’ll get a sneak peek into how you can scale your own business using acquisitions. But please tread carefully. I have seen a graveyard full of sunken ships upon the shores of too many deals done at once. You can be very dangerous when you use the f-word: focus.
			

With that warning front of mind, let’s look at seven ways to do add-on acquisitions.
			

## Seven “Income Streams” from One Business

			

There is a saying that the average millionaire has seven to twelve streams of income. Probably true. But those streams are typically interwoven as threads to a rug. You use what you know to create more of what you want. Let’s illustrate this with numbers from a hypothetical laundromat as an example. We’ll “turn” its yearly $67,000 in profits into more than a million dollars in annual profits. And we’ll do it largely using creative financing.
			
				
			
			

## Step 1: Buy a Platform Business

			

The wrong way to get seven income streams? Go off and open seven new businesses at once. A spectacular way to fail. Instead, buy what is called a platform business. One you can add to with more acquisitions, layering it like an onion. Here’s your starting point:
			
				
- 
					

Income: $67,000 profit
				
				
- 
					

Money down: $0 down, with profit payments up to $15,000, for the price of $100,000
				
				
- 
					

Income stream count: 1
				
			
			

## Step 2: Add Your First Acquisition

			

After you get the first ninety days of operations under your belt, it’s time to add another income stream. Start small. Why not install some vending machines in your laundromat? Think snack vending, soap vending, and toy vending for kids.
			

How profitable are vending machines? Well, my Twitter friend Quinn Miller had:
			
				
- 
					

27 vending machines
				
				
- 
					

65 percent profit
				
				
- 
					

20 hours per week of work
				
				
- 
					

$15,000 monthly revenue
				
				
- 
					

$600 start-up cost
				
				
- 
					

All-in costs for the machines: $20,000
				
			
			

Quinn started the business after a chat with one of his mentors. The man had sold his business of hundreds of vending machines to a private equity firm. They’d paid him a fat eight-figure check. Quinn’s take after speaking to his mentor:
			
				

You find a spot, negotiate with the building owner, buy a machine for $500 to $3,000, fill it with a couple hundred dollars’ worth of product, throw it on an app, and it breaks even thirty to sixty days later.
			
			

I’m in.
			

So, you buy vending machines and get cash flow from them. Then you expand. First, to the businesses close to your laundromat. Then to other locations in a one-mile radius. After that, even more vending machines in the shopping area where your laundromat is located. Your laundromat operator can manage them.
			

Here’s why this is brilliant. At $1,000 per machine for twenty machines, you’re in for $20,000. If they make $500 a month at 40 percent profit (make sure you double-check this math as markets move), you break even in five months and net $4,000 a month after that.
			

Now you have $67,000 annual profit from the laundromat and $48,000 in vending machine profit.
			
				
- 
					

Income: $115,000
				
				
- 
					

Income stream count: 2
				
			
			

## Step 3: Vertical Acquisition

			

Now your laundromat is fully operational. As you grow the business, you get to know your competitors and other operators in the area. You find Bob. Bob is a pro; he’s run his laundromat for twenty years, and as the years passed, the city he lives in changed. Austin became a little too “weird” for Bob.
			

One day, he tells you he wants to move to the country. He has dreams of wraparound porches, horses, and not a goddamn neighbor in sight. During one of your monthly meetups, you casually ask if he might sell you his laundromat to pay for that country home. He says, “Well, reckon I might.” Seller financing lets you use future profits to buy his business. Now each month you send Bob a check and plan to pay him off in three years. Bob is happy as a pig in sh*t. He’s getting paid for work you’re doing, he’s got his wraparound porch, and you’ve got a new revenue line, baby.
			

Bob’s place is a bigger, more established laundromat than yours. It has a wash-and-fold service making $1 million in annual revenue and $300,000 in profit. Now you have his $300,000 in profit (bringing you to $415,000 in annual profit) and can offer wash-and-fold services to your customers. This adds $50,000 a year in revenue. Since Bob’s operation was a bit dated, you add a new website, signage, subscription models, and vending machines. Those value-adds bring $125,000 in additional profit.
			
				
- 
					

Income: $540,000
				
				
- 
					

Income stream count: 3
				
			
			

## Step 4: Asset Acquisition (Machines)

			

After Bob teaches you the ins and outs of his laundromat, you realize something. This laundromat has the capacity for 30 to 40 percent more machines. More machines = higher revenue. You shop around for some used machines. A broker tells you about a store on the other side of town that’s closing. You snap up their equipment for pennies on the dollar. Your 30 percent expansion adds another $50,000 in annual profit.
			
				
- 
					

Your increased capacity = $50,000 per year
				
				
- 
					

Income: $590,000
				
				
- 
					

Income stream count: 4
				
			
			

## Step 5: Satellite Acquisition (Delivery)

			

You love your laundromat—you’re making $590,000 a year, what’s not to like?—but you’re hungry. You want more margin and higher-end customers. How could you do that? Delivery. So you buy a van delivery fleet from another company, add a wash-and-fold delivery service, and earn another $250,000 in income.
			

(Sidenote: One of the laundromats I invested in made $3 million in a wash-and-fold business, The Folde. That’s not counting the added tax benefits: when you buy equipment, like vans or machines, you can employ accelerated depreciation and write-offs. Call your accountant.) The downside is these add-ons come with a lot of work, logistics, complexity, and the occasional meth addict break-in. Let’s not minimize that.
			
				
- 
					

Income: $840,000
				
				
- 
					

Income stream count: 5
				
			
			

## Step 6: Horizontal Acquisition (Products)

			

One of my favorite ways to diversify my income streams is what I call a P&L review. I look at every expense to see if I can turn my costs into profits. Try it out.
			

What’s the biggest cost that you could change at your laundromat? The soap. Why not buy a mixer of commercial-grade soap, brand it, bottle it, cut your soap costs 30 to 50 percent, then sell it to your competitors under a different name? That’s clever. So you buy a soap business or acquire the equipment to do it yourself. Or you partner on it and take a revenue share or an affiliate fee. That’s another $200,000 per year.
			

The beauty of these acquisitions is that you’re using your business profits or seller financing to do each step, all while decreasing your taxes and keeping cash in your wallet. I’m oversimplifying to illustrate, but you get the high-level point.
			
				
- 
					

Income: $1,040,000
				
				
- 
					

Income stream count: 6
				
			
			

## Step 7: Hard-Asset Acquisition (Real Estate)

			

My friend, you are cruising. You have a lil budding business empire doing $1,040,000 profit. Most likely you’re doing $3 million to $4 million in total revenue. You grow and add efficiencies to bring that number even higher. In laundromats, your biggest expenses will be water, electricity, labor, and then your lease. Let’s decrease one of those.
			

Why not buy your strip mall and be a landlord to your businesses? This secures all the write-offs, depreciation, and tax benefits that come with real estate. You buy your building using a loan. You now use the other businesses’ rent to essentially subsidize your own.
			
				
- 
					

That deal equals another $100,000 in profit.
				
				
- 
					

Income: $1,140,000
				
				
- 
					

Income stream count: 7
				
			
			

Will it always work like this? No. Could it happen faster or slower? Yes. Should I keep asking myself questions on your behalf and answering them? Probably not. The point is, you can either grow organically in your businesses or you can buy your growth. You have more cash flow to buy more businesses or to scale. So if you think I’m not out here buying up media and newsletter companies to add them to my pie, you’d be mistaken. Don’t beat ’em; buy ’em.
			

## Exit Strategy: How to Sell Your Business and Start Over

			

At some point, you are going to want out. I promise. Entrepreneurs often love building their empires…until they find themselves being eaten alive by their creations. My billionaire friend Andrew Wilkinson said it best:
			
				

Every Founder in Year 1: “I love my company. These people are my family and I will run this business forever.”
				

Every Founder in Year 8: “I hate my life and my employees hate me. Please buy my business and let me go away for a very long time.”
			
			

There will come a day when you will want to pass the torch. Sure, Jeff Bezos ran Amazon for twenty-seven years, but he eventually opted for yacht life.
			

Start with the end in mind. Less than 30 percent of small businesses sell, and less than 20 percent of small-business owners plan their exits. You are not most people. You will sell one day, and you will make millions because you prepared for it. I wish that for you.
			

My friend Sam Parr built his company, The Hustle, up to tens of millions in revenue and millions in profit per year. He was in his late twenties/early thirties and a millionaire founder. Sounds lovely thus far. Yet sleepless nights, worry about what would go wrong next, and little cash because it was all wrapped up in the business meant he was done.
			

So after five years, when HubSpot came to him with an offer to purchase, Sam feigned disinterest. Like a hard-to-get girl at the bar. But let’s be honest, he was ready to get lucky. He wrapped up a sale that, even today, he says was a bit early for him. Why? Because running a bootstrapped start-up is miserable. It’s like war: “Months of boredom punctuated by moments of sheer terror.” Sure, you make money and have some prestige. But anyone who tells you running a business is stress free is lying. So he sold and made himself tens and tens of millions of dollars.
			

Let’s tell another sales story. It involves a beat-up old laundromat getting a facelift and three young entrepreneurs tackling a dirty business. Mark Vlaskamp and his buddies bought a laundromat in my hometown of Austin, did a fixer-upper on it, and then added a few more. Why? Most people think laundromats are going the way of typewriters. Even people living in five-hundred-square-foot studio apartments often have a washer and dryer. Who goes to laundromats anymore? Was this a bad investment?
			

Not when Mark is running the show. The laundromats soon made millions. His big, clean, and convenient laundromats were busy during the day with customers who needed the speed, size, and convenience of his industrial units. And then Mark launched a wash-fold-and-deliver business to keep his machines spinning twenty-two hours a day. He does laundry for visitors in town on vacation or for the South by Southwest festival. He does the laundry for visiting professional sports teams. He has an enormous back room filled with rack after rack of bags full of dirty clothes coming in, and clean clothes heading out for delivery. He’s got himself a little Amazon warehouse of unmentionables humming in smelly and cruising out shining. All night long, his crew is running orders through the machines. Folding all clothing precisely according to the company’s standards. Delivering fresh, clean clothes to happy customers as far as forty miles away. In two years, he built a $3-million-a-year business that he’ll be selling for nearly $4 million. Mark is a good operator, but at some point he realized he was ready for his next venture. And it wasn’t the first business he bought.
			

With a little luck and a lot of hard work, this will likely be you.
			

## When Is the Best Time to Sell?

			

A good business can always be sold or bought. Even if there are rising interest rates, declining sales prices, pandemics, and all manner of economic factors lining up. Doesn’t matter. A good business can always be sold or bought. Yes, economic factors will have an impact on negotiations, but you can figure that out. For example, when interest rates are high, buyers may offer less and sellers may need to offer financing to fetch the price they want.
			

As more boomers realize the value of the businesses they built, the inventory for sale could go up. That means more competition among sellers. But will that actually happen? In 2023, most business brokers reported closing more deals than they had the previous year. The number of available businesses was up, but so was the number of interested buyers entering the market.
			

Remember, too, that selling your business isn’t a fast process. Just preparing to sell a business can take up to a year. Like anything in life that’s worth it, it isn’t fast and it isn’t easy. You can’t bake a turkey in an hour by putting the oven to 900 degrees. Whatever economic issues are affecting today could be gone by the time the sale rolls around. But the unprepared transfer their money to the prepared in times of crisis. I invested in several car washes across Texas for 8 to 10X profit. Now they’re going for 12 to 24X profit. Bad economies only ruin bad deals.
			
				
			
			

## Valuation Booster: How to Increase Your Business’s Value

			
				
			
			

What does the above graphic mean? It’s not simply how much profit you make; it’s how likely that profit will continue to exist going forward, divided by the pain in your business, minus processes you put in place to decrease that pain. I could write an entire book about selling your business for more (in fact, I might). For now, we will keep this part short and give you only what you need.
			

How much is your business actually worth? On the one hand, it’s worth whatever people are willing to pay for it. On the other hand, you can firmly (and legally) put your thumb on the scale. So let’s stack the deck in your favor.
			

To start, there are three main ways your business can get a valuation:
			
				
- 
					

 Get a certified business appraisal. This could cost $15,000 or more, and it isn’t required prior to selling (although the buyer’s lender may require them to get one).
				
				
- 
					

Have your CPA, business broker, or adviser establish an informal value. Base it on the sales of similarly sized businesses in your geographic area.
				
				
- 
					

Take your profits and multiply them by two to ten, depending on your industry. This is my preferred method when I am buying a business. But if you are selling, you want to skew this number as high as possible.
				
			
			

## Sleepless Nights: What Buyers Need to See to Rest Easy

			

Why do less than 30 percent of businesses sell? To answer that, you have to think about what the buyer needs. The buyer must be certain that:
			
				
- 
					

The business is worth what they’re buying it for.
				
				
- 
					

They can make the payments on the loan they use to buy the business.
				
				
- 
					

They are getting a good deal that makes sense for them.
				
			
			

If you want to sell your business, make it feel like they’re getting a deal so good it’s illegal. Make them feel like the numbers are so obvious they could review them with a permanent marker. Make them feel like a bank would be crazy not to loan on this deal. Or make your business so profitable that everyone is competing to buy it.
			

Keep in mind all the thinking you did when you were a buyer. You had a lot of questions. You wondered: Why is the owner selling now? What’s their primary motivation? Does the company have a strong future? If so, is the owner selling because they don’t have the desire or energy to make the changes to reach that potential? As a buyer, you committed the time and patience needed to tease out the honest answers to these questions. Expect your buyer to do the same. If they’re a smart buyer (like you were), they will want to take some time to get to know you.
			

Be prepared for that. You can anticipate their questions, so have some answers ready. In addition to asking you about your motivation for selling, they’ll want to know how you got started in business and why you chose this business. What do you love about the work and what do you dislike about it? What kind of mistakes have you made along the way and how would you have handled key challenges differently? Be honest: What’s a typical day like? What are your plans after the sale?
			

Your buyer will wonder if the business can function without you. Can the business continue to be strong after you’ve left? It’s up to you to ensure that it will. Before selling, “remove” yourself from the day-to-day work. Bring on a trusted general manager or management team. Train them to understand and operate the business as well as you do. Even if you don’t sell, this kind of contingency planning will protect your business in case something happens to you. So just picture those mattress commercials: “After buying this bad boy, you’ll only ever be a winner or a snoozer.” Dad jokes.
			

Buyers are like Chihuahuas. They’re nervous, a little shaky, and barking all the time. Rightly so. It is no small thing to buy a business and dedicate your life to it.
			

I have a process I call the Cashout Cake. Here’s what it looks like:
			
				
					

Cashout Cake
				
				
					
				
			
			

Ever tried to bake a cake without following the recipe exactly? It doesn’t end well. Martha Stewart I am not, but even I know you can’t just wing it in baking. When it comes to making your own Cashout Cake, you need the right ingredients. Here they are:
			
				
- 
					

Simple Finances
				
				
- 
					

More SOPs Than a Pilot
				
				
- 
					

Loyal Employees
				
				
- 
					

Not Run by You
				
				
- 
					

Matching Outfits
				
				
- 
					

Eggs in Many Baskets
				
				
- 
					

Sales Team
				
			
			

When you follow the recipe and use all these ingredients, it will make it so much easier for you to sell your business for millions. If your business lacks any of these ingredients, fix them and prove they work. Then you’ll be able to sell.
			

## Ingredient 1: Simple Finances

			

Here’s what NOT to do when sharing your finances with a potential buyer.
			

We walked into one accounting practice that was closing and potentially going to sell for a steal. The problem? Imagine a 1970s office complete with orange shag carpet, linoleum, and a couple of light bulbs missing. It is covered from ceiling to shag with boxes of tax returns, files, financials, and what appear to be many a coffee stain. When we asked for their financials, they looked over at two boxes with papers messily spewing out and told us to feel free to take a look. Astonishing for an accounting practice, when we reconciled the books, they didn’t match their tax returns. Irony. When we asked about cash versus credit card payments they didn’t know. Their financials were so sloppy the IRS would have fainted.
			

Compare that to a step-by-step, easy-to-follow recipe:
			

You walk into a company. Their tax returns and QuickBooks accounts are immaculate. They clearly show revenue, expenses, and how they have changed over the years. Everything is accessible with one click in a beautiful spreadsheet. They show a steadily improving bottom line, suggesting their businesses still have room to grow. This makes it easy for buyers to see the key levers in the business. Their finances are audited. They have financial statements, and their accountant is available to review them with you. Their overall debt-to-income ratio is at or below 43 percent. The lower the debt, the higher the price, obviously. This cake is so tender you don’t even need a knife. That’s how to do financials right.
			

## Ingredient 2: More SOPs Than a Pilot

			

SOPs are standard operating procedures. Sounds boring, I know. But things that used to bore me now get me all hot and bothered. Why do I love SOPs? Very simple: Do you like repeating yourself? Me neither. So stop by writing it down once and never saying it again.
			

How to do this right? You institute the rule of three: Anything you or your employees do more than three times, or that has at least three steps, is documented. A buyer doesn’t have to figure out what your team needs to do, because it’s written down. In fact, they can even see everything your team has done in the past.
			

You have training materials like Mark. He even teaches his workers how to fold undies, T-shirts, and fitted sheets the RIGHT way. You pay someone to come into your business and document your systems for you. Or you ask each employee to document the ten most important things they do and create instructions for each. Everyone updates with one Loom (a free screen-casting service) and Google Doc every week, so it’s a living, breathing organism. You keep notes of past meetings. You have a scorecard for each business unit and individual job. You have historical job descriptions and an organizational chart for the entire business.
			

You, my friend, have a culture of consistency. You are such an organized hominid that you have templates for various company correspondence. Things like emails to vendors, reminders, and meeting agendas. You have standards for how fast employees must respond to coworkers and customers. Because you know that if you don’t have systems and processes, then you have a job, not a business.
			

How to F this up? You have no idea how Joe versus Jake in marketing does their job. Every time your team goes to visit a client, there is no checklist for pre or post or follow-up. Your team doesn’t use tech tools for training and oversight. You write things down on paper, and even that’s disorganized. You wonder sometimes if people are even doing their jobs, so you hover over everyone all the time. There is no scorecard except the one in your brain. Org charts? Those are for MBA nerds, not real owners. Flow charts? What’s that? Everything in business usually works, but you don’t know how or why unless you pry it out of your noggin.
			

This, my friend, is most small businesses. It’s a nightmare.
			

## Ingredient 3: Loyal Employees

			

Buyers don’t want to see high turnover. So you obsess on your good record for keeping employees.
			

The Bad: Your business is a revolving door of talent. You always have a Hiring! sign up because you can’t keep good people. Your management team is inexperienced and new. Proven players aren’t part of your equation.
			

## Ingredient 4: Not Run by You

			

Your management team is so solid you go on (and document) a monthlong vacation a year because your team runs without you. You have a set process for hiring, it’s tight, and people who work with you celebrate five-, ten-, and twenty-year anniversaries. A business reliant on the owner is an unsellable business. Bring on the confetti cake.
			

## Ingredient 5: Matching Outfits

			

The ideal situation in a business sale is when you could change the name on your P&L and tax return, and the figures would match up like a pair of new socks. The SBA requires those documents for approval when a buyer borrows money for acquisitions. You need to be aware that any undocumented profits or benefits will likely need some guarantees during the deal negotiations. That may hurt your final sale price. For every dollar that doesn’t make it onto the tax return, you need to have a detailed explanation. You also need a clear, easy-to-understand “add-back” strategy, which could mean tens of thousands more in your pocket when you sell. I’ll break down exactly what that means on this page.
			

## Ingredient 6: Eggs in Many Baskets

			

The Good: You pull in customers through a variety of channels, from Instagram to Amazon to Google to paid ads to organic. You, sneaky devil, even have a traffic gap audit to identify areas where your traffic could be improved, and you diversify if you need to. Talk nerdy to me. You’ve got so many baskets you won’t even let any single customer make up more than 15 percent of your business’s revenue.
			

The Bad: All your customers come from Facebook. Then Zuckerberg gets pissy and turns off your account. Bye-bye, business.
			

## Ingredient 7: Sales Team

			

In selling, two salespeople are always better than one. Process beats people. To have predictable, recurring revenue, you need to have a sales engine. One that a buyer of your business can drive and continue bringing in predictable revenue.
			

When I first started First Trust’s Latin America business, my “territory” was literally all of Latin America. We had zero clients and I was the main salesperson. I’d do steak dinners, endless PowerPoints, and tap dances to close. As I grew the business to $1 billion in sales, I added team members and trained them on my process. We had April in Mexico, Colton in Peru, Carlos in Colombia. We had a team, a bench, and a process. That’s what you need.
			
				
				
			
			

## Turning the Tables: How to Sell for Maximum Profit

			

More Profit → Higher Price. It’s that simple. Buyers are first and foremost interested in one thing: profit. They don’t care how many weekends you had to work building your business. They are only mildly intrigued by all the great opportunities you saw and didn’t capitalize on. Top of mind for buyers is cash flow, pure and simple.
			

Stick with me for some boring stuff that turns into dollars. Buyers will base their offer on a multiple of your profit. If the business is over $3 million, they may use EBITDA. For anything smaller, they’ll likely use seller’s discretionary earnings, or SDE. SDE is your annual profit added to any other benefits the seller gets from the business, such as medical insurance or a gym membership.
			

There are two magical words in increasing your sales price: add-backs. Buyers hate them but you love them. If you don’t carefully calculate SDE, the price you command could be tens of thousands lower than it should be, or more. My friend Joe Valley, author of The EXITpreneur’s Playbook, says not factoring in add-backs is one of the biggest mistakes owners make when selling their business. Millions are left on the table here.
			

Add-backs are benefits and onetime expenses that increase the seller’s net income. For instance, if you bought yourself a nice $75,000 truck to use sometimes for your business, that’s an add-back. You’re keeping the truck, but the money you spent on it was part of your company’s income and should be counted. The owner’s hefty salary, health insurance, and other “owner benefits” are also add-backs. Charitable donations and payments into your individual retirement account are add-backs.[*4]
			

It’s best to be as granular as possible, but watch out: this can potentially backfire. For example, Valley points out that the buyer may look at your salary and ask if that reflects a forty-hour workweek. If you’re working eighty hours a week, the buyer may want to renegotiate the price down. That’s because he’ll have to hire an employee to replace all the work you were doing. Yikes.
			

If you know you’re going to be selling in a few years, get with your accountant to front-load your deductions and notate while documenting your add-backs. That way, you can show maximum profits in the months or years heading into a sale. Anyone who’s ever itemized a tax return understands the need to reduce your taxable income. But increasing profitability (on paper) may be in your best interest now, even at the expense of a bigger tax bill.
			

## Improve Price, Multiple, or Both

			

For most small businesses, your multiple will likely be two to three times your SDE. So, if you’re pulling $100,000 out of the business every year, you’ll likely get offers for $200,000 to $300,000. Some go as high as 5X SDE, depending on the industry and all the ingredients in the Cashout Cake.
			

Several factors can affect that multiple, including:
			
				
- 
					

Comps: If companies like yours are selling for more than 2X or 3X, your multiple could go up too.
				
				
- 
					

Assets: What’s the age and condition of your equipment? If the washing machines in your laundromat are all twelve years old, a smart buyer will realize they will have to be replaced soon. And those puppies aren’t cheap.
				
				
- 
					

Moat: How protected is this stream of income? How heavy is the competition for customers and what’s your business’s competitive advantage?
				
				
- 
					

CRM: How strong is your client relationship management? Do you even have a CRM?
				
				
- 
					

Reviews and reputation: What’s your web presence? What are your online ratings?
				
				
- 
					

Profit margin and recurring revenue: It’s great if you have increasing revenue, but buyers also want to see a strong profit margin. Companies that reduce gross profit margin in favor of revenue aren’t worth as much. Recurring revenue—such as service or maintenance agreements, subscriptions, and memberships—increases your margins.
				
				
- 
					

Your goals: What do you want out of this sale? Is cash up front more important to you than ongoing payments? Do you want to ensure all your employees’ jobs are safe? How long are you willing to stay on as a consultant?
				
				
- 
					

Commitment: How much time is the new owner going to have to commit to working in the business? Buyers will pay a higher multiple for a business that requires less work, has better management teams, or is de-risked. They’d rather get paid to manage an operator for a couple of hours a week versus having to work full time. Say you have a $1 million company and you work fifty hours a week for a $300,000 SDE. If the buyer needs to replace you, they may only offer a 1.5X multiple ($450,000). But if you have an operator in place, your multiple might go up to 3X or 3.5X ($900,000 to $975,000). Most buyers want to purchase an income stream, not a job.
				
			
			

## Seller Financing

			

You already know all about this. Want a higher sale price? Let them use seller financing. If you don’t need to get all the money right away, financing the new buyer can help you get a better price. You can avoid a huge tax hit by spreading your gains over multiple years, and you can increase your payout by charging interest on the financing. Consult an attorney on structuring the deal and terms to allow you to take back the business. Certain cases will make sense, such as your buyer defaulting on payments or large declines in revenue or profitability.
			

## Brokers: Swipe Left or Right?

			

Selling a business is a bit like sex—it’s damn hard to keep going when you’re thinking about leaving.
			

Brokers help. They typically charge 10 percent commission, but a great one is worth it. A broker can save you time by marketing your business, weeding out the lookie-loos, and finding (and vetting) potential buyers. They’ll help you pull together all the documents you’ll need for the buyer’s due diligence. Some will offer advice on how to quickly improve the value of your business prior to sale. Let the broker do the work while you run the business over the nine months or so that it takes to sell.
			

That said, finding the right broker is harder than finding a vegan in Dallas. Some brokers make you sign a twelve-month agreement in exchange for them placing an ad. Bad ones will do practically nothing to get your business in front of serious buyers. You don’t want that. You want someone with experience selling businesses in your category and a track record of success. You want someone with a Rolodex full of potential buyers who already have their financing lined up.
			

Your best bet: referrals. Who do you know? Interview the candidates. How will they find buyers and qualify them? What kind of offering books and listing ads have they created for their other clients? What kinds of questions do they ask about your business? A good broker will want to know as much as they can about your business.
			

Great brokers can be the best investment you ever make in selling your business. Mediocre brokers will drain the life out of you.
			

## Round Two: What to Do After You’ve Successfully Exited

			
				

You aren’t truly in the game unless you find yourself in the dead of the night, head in hands, sitting in the dark, with no idea what to do next.
				

—My dad
			
			

One night you’ll be wide awake, sitting in an armchair in the dark in your living room, feeling nothing but regret. You will not like things the buyers do to your business. You will wish you had sold for more. You’ll get calls from your old employees. Selling is emotional.
			

But I can also promise you something else: This is not your only shot in the arena. The journey for acquirers is never-ending. The siren songs of businesses will call to you again with their addictive sound. Laundromats were my gateway drug to acquiring businesses. For you, it might be car washes, or mini storages, or ice-vending machines, or private airplane hangar rentals. Who knows. The only thing that is certain is that if you make your first deal a success, and especially after you sell it, you will want to do it again. And again.
			

Once you become a dealmaker, everything in life will be seen through the lens of a deal. There is always another company to add, an idea for expansion, a dollar to invest. Dealmakers play chess in a world of checkers. The only limit is your drive to keep playing. And although we call them “boring” businesses, this game is anything but boring—it’s a blast.

Final Thoughts, Main Street Millionaire
		
		
		

	
	
		
			

## Final Thoughts

			

## The Last Chapter: Your Call to Arms

			

Let’s take a step back to look at everything you’ve learned so far. You now know the secret to wealth and freedom: ownership. You also know why most start-ups are doomed to fail (no revenue), and the proven road to cash flow: buy an established boring business.
			

The R.I.C.H. method—Research, Invest, Command, Harness—showed you how to buy, run, and grow your first small business. We covered everything—from picking the right business for you to learning how to lead and shape its culture. The simple marketing strategies you learned are battle-tested and can immediately boost your profits. You also learned how to know when it’s time to exit, and how to get paid to sell your business.
			

None of this is rocket science. It is simple, yet hard, grueling work, which you are likely very capable of. The formula is: keep going after things get hard.
			
				
			
			

Now, it’s time to go use what you’ve learned. Start small if you must, but start. Look for simple businesses with plenty of room to grow. Do your homework. Get out and beat the pavement. Make calls, meet people, get your fingernails dirty while opening boxes of financials yellowed with age. Then start adding those zeros to your bank account. The tools, strategies, and stories in this book will not only help you reach financial freedom, they’ll help us save America.
			

## The Silent War: Main Street versus Wall Street

			

We are at war, whether we like it or not. It’s a battle that invisibly pits everyday men and women against the behemoths. The BlackRocks, the Blackstones, the KKRs, and the Amazons of the world. They are “the 1 percent,” and they want to own everything.
			

In 2022, they bought one out of every four houses. They also own one out of every three local businesses. One out of every three. You don’t just work for them; you live in their world. As silently as a creeping mist, the big keep getting bigger, and we become a nation of serfs and renters.
			

How do we fight back? The answer is not to “shop local,” or get more regulations, or to protest and riot in the streets. The way to fight back is by using their strategy against them. In a word:
			

Ownership.
			

I wrote this book for one reason: I believe we can win this war. And you are one of the few who can make it happen. You are an entrepreneur. You’re one of the crazy ones who can will things into existence. You are a part of our generation of explorers, risk-takers, and world builders. You are one of us.
			

If you want to change the world, then take on the mantle of ownership. You can buy Main Street businesses for the money, sure. But join the cause, because this is a war, and if you don’t take real action, we—the people—will lose.
			

This is what I ask of you: Do not shy away from your greatness. Do not downplay your potential to change our world. It only takes 1 to 5 percent of a population to alter the course of history, for better or worse. Buying and upgrading a local Main Street business might seem like a small act in the grand scheme. But it is a small act of defiance that ripples into the global economy, and out to future generations. With every acquisition, we are taking back control and saving our towns. We are creating a better world for our grandkids.
			

Our country does not need more dating apps. We don’t need more drop shippers playing middlemen with manufacturers in China. We need our young entrepreneurs to carry the torch of the older generation. They are the ones who can save the small businesses that power our local economies. They are the ones who can preserve the charm and vibrancy of our cities. They are the ones who can win this war.
			

You change the world one community at a time. Buy an established business, then turn it into the modern company you know it can be. Ownership is the key to your freedom, to freedom for all of us. It’s what will save our country, one “boring business” at a time.
			

## Join the Movement for Ownership

			

Before you go, I have three final things.
			

Number 1: Join your fellow owners. If you want to take the next step, join our group of owners at contrarianthinking.co. In this group, there lives an army of people just like you. Those who will build, buy, sell, and carry on for the next generation of owners. You might just find the next business you want to buy there.
			

We also have a Mergers & Acquisitions institute, which you can always join later if you are serious. Here’s who we are and what we do:
			
				
- 
					

Seven experts with a combined billion dollars in transactions
				
				
- 
					

More than twenty in-person events a year
				
				
- 
					

Seven calls a month for one year
				
				
- 
					

Twenty expert office hours monthly
				
				
- 
					

Three-day annual conference
				
			
			

Number 2: MBAs are broken. Wharton offers a three-day M&A class for $15,000. Lord, give me the confidence and audacity of Ivy League degrees and their entitlements. I hope this book served you better for the thirty dollars you spent, versus the institutions that take our tax dollars and leave us with opinions instead of skills. Which brings me to…
			

Number 3: Leave a review. If you believe in this movement toward ownership, one of the easiest ways to support it is by reviewing this book on Amazon and Goodreads. I promise, I read every single one. So pretty please, it’d mean a lot to me.
			

Thank you so much for reading this book. I will never apologize for loving and admiring those of us who choose this path. We will continue to have freedom because of people like you. I hope you push yourself to do the thing that scares you. To experience that otherworldly feeling of standing in front of a store that belongs to you. Choose the hard thing, take a chance on yourself. And f*ck that boss who didn’t believe in you.
			

If I can do it, so can you. See you on the inside.
			

Codie Sanchez
			

July 2024
			

Austin, Texas

## Acknowledgments

			

If I could have one word written on my tombstone, it would be Author. Books have been one of my greatest loves in life. I owe that to my mother. Summers spent checking out so many library books that they’d stack like a tower on my trembling little kid arms. Bliss. If you enjoyed this book at all, you can thank my mom. The best teacher I’ve ever known.
			

To my Christopher, it’s always been us against the world. Marriage to you is the best investment I’ve ever made. Thank you for inspiring me to jump every time I’m sure I’ll fall, and for your strength in always catching me.
			

Familia y sangre. Mom and Dad, my inspiration to teach and do the work is due only to you. Colton, hermano, you are my ride or die. I’d call you if I needed to bury a body (metaphorically) or if I wanted to laugh until I cried. To my aunts and uncles, your entrepreneurial pursuits bled into me before I knew it. Thank you for lighting the path. Also, y’all are the perfect amount of absolute crazy. You inspire me to build, take risks, and dance wildly inappropriately. Grandma Gin, thank you for letting me steal all your books as a kid and for employing my brother and cousin to sell them. Who knew it’d be my first business? Grandpa Juke and Grandma Flo, from the turquoise mines of Arizona to the corner store in Miami, you were the original Main Street owners. I never forget how hard your road was so mine could be smoother.
			

To my editing team, especially Charlie Hoehn, for late-night phone calls and for convincing me that some of my stories were likely too un-PC, even for me. But mostly for relentless editing to the point of perfection. Thank you—you’re a rare talent and I’m honored you chose me too. To Chris Piper, Jim Sloan, Josh Troy, and Lloyd Silver: How many edits did we do on this? Too many to defy reason. Good thing you’re all definitively unreasonable! Thank you seems too small a phrase.
			

To the woman who made me an author, my editor at Penguin, Helen Healey-Cunningham. You believed in my story of ownership so much you bought a damn business yourself. Then you let me call you nonstop while you were on maternity leave and talk about the perfect way to say just about everything. To the entire Penguin team—Adrian, Niki, Megan. Remember those awful early titles we had? Pete Garceau for your beautiful design. Thank you all for never settling and reminding me how much this book matters whenever I got nervous, thinking, What if it’s not good enough?!?
			

To my friends who edited, gave advice, inspired me, and listened to my giant ideas for this book: Stacey and Alex Duran, Shaina Shiwarski, Vanessa Van Edwards, Kim Kaupe, Fernando Vigil, Donald Park, Nick Gray, Sam and Sarah Parr, and so many more. If I ever lost it all, I’d consider myself rich because of each of you.
			

To the Main Street Millionaires I somehow got lucky enough to call friends. You all do not just build; you share how you did it along the way. Bill Perkins, Jim Bowen, Geoffrey Kent, Sam Parr, Noah Kagan, Andrew Wilkinson, Nick Huber, Alex and Leila Hormozi, Joe Craft and Jane Brady, Alex Leiberman, and Dan Martell. And also to every MSM we’ve documented—from porta potties to crime-scene cleanup to street sweeping—I’ve never been dirtier or happier than when working alongside all of you.
			

My brilliant friends who are now in the media. Thank you for sharing your brilliance with me and casting a light on this book. You are so giving and rare. Steven Bartlett, Jay Shetty, Andy Frisella, Tim Kennedy, Tulsi Gabbard, Balaji Srinivasan, Lewis Howes, Ali Abdaal, Chris Williamson, Sahil Bloom, Anthony Pompliano, Pace Morby, and so many of you creators. You are the voice of this generation and you gave your microphone to this book. I’m still stunned by your generosity.
			

To my team at Contrarian Thinking and Main Street Holding Company. What a ride, eh? Thank you for making this book a success, for your sleepless nights, wild ideas, and belief in making others rich and free. When I wanted to skeet shoot the book, light a cake on fire, and create four hundred better, cleaner graphics, you said, Yes…let’s do that. Jacob, Jazmine, Lindsey, Tanner, Samuel, Christian, and the whole content team. You are my wild band of renegades, and I adore you.
			

To my Contrarian Thinking Community. It all started with you and me, baby. You bought businesses, you built Main Street, and you did the rarest of things…you became doers. I had a dream to build the best academy and community in the world around buying, building, and selling small businesses. Together, we did it. You proved that normal people like you and me can do what Wall Street does: own. And we’re just getting started, aren’t we? When it’s tough, when you want to give up, I hope you’ll remember how strong you are. Main Street over Wall Street FOREVER.
			

Finally, to you, dear reader. My fellow unemployable human, may you never listen to anyone who says you can’t. The world will try. But you know the truth: No one is coming to save you. It’s all up to you. Be an owner in a world of squatters. Your life and our country depend on it.
			

OWNERS!!!
			

## —Codie

About the Author, Main Street Millionaire
		
		
		

	
	
		

## About the Author

		

Codie Sanchez is the founder and CEO of Contrarian Thinking, a financial media company with millions of followers. After earning the Robert F. Kennedy award in journalism for covering human trafficking and border crises, she transitioned to finance to help people achieve financial freedom, holding senior roles at Vanguard, Goldman Sachs, State Street, and First Trust.
		

Sanchez is also the owner of Main Street Holding Company and Contrarian Thinking Capital. She owns many Main Street businesses, including car washes, laundromats, and roofing, painting, and handyman companies. Which business is her favorite? She’ll never tell.
